GOMEZ WILL DISOWN
THE CUBAN ASSEMBLY
Special Cable to The Pall and the Nrw York
Herald. Copyrighted. IS9&, by James Gor
don Bennett.
HAVANA, March 10. â General
Gomez will disown the Cuban
Assembly. Disgusted with the
petty jealousies of the members.
and irritated at the antagonism
evinced toward himself, he will dis
claim all connection with that body
and dispute its right to control his ac
tions.
Relying upon his popularity with the
Cuban people he will proceed to discuss
with General Brooke the settlement of
the problem of the pacification of the
country without regard to the action
of the Assembly, which has by its un
dignified conduct and bitter sentiments
of hostility to Americans lost the con
fidence of sober-minded Cubans.
This was the statement made to me
to-night by a member of the Assembly
who has looked after Gomez' Interests
there and possesses the old chief's con
fidence. It Is confirmed by other offi
cials who are in touch with the Cuban
general.
The conservative element in the city
expresses its satisfaction that General
Gomez, whose honesty of purpose is be
lieved in, has cut himself loose from a
faction whose sole work appears to be
to discuss in secret session who shall
be the first President.
It Is also declared that Gomez has
decided that th- $3,000,000 granted by
the United States shall be distributed
only to soldiers who actually served in
the Cuban war and were under arms
before the declaration of war between
the United States and Spain. This will
probably reduce the number of par
ticipants to 30,000 or under.
SANTIAGO, March 10.â The results
of the policy inaugurated at Havana of
discontinuing public works in this
province and throwing men out of em
ployment are beginning to show. Yes
terday six bandits were captured in the
neighborhood of Concepcion, all of
whom were formerly workers in the
road gangs. They say they have no
means and must either rob or starve.
They have been lodged in the City Jail,
but considerable sympathy is displayed
in their behalf by the populace.
Much the same sort of thing is hap
pening in the district of Holguin. where
a brush between bandits and gendarmes
result* in the killing of one of the
former. Nevertheless it is not ex
pected that these troubles will take a
serious turn, owing to the probability
that work will be resumed next week.
TO ADVANCE
ON MALOLOS
IMMEDIATELY
General Lawton Will Lead
the Americans to Agui
naldo's Capital.
Otis to Give His Attention to Civil
Government and the Outside
Islands.
Special Dispatch to The Call. |
NEW YORK, March 10.â Wash
ington correspondent of the Herald tel- j
egraphs: The arrival at Manila to-day
of General Lawton with reinforcements, .
as cabled to the War Department, j
means the beginning of an active cam- j
paign against the Filipinos. General ,
Lawton will have, under General Otis, j
command of all military operations in j
Luzon, while 'tis, as Governor General, j
will direct the conduct of civil affairs j
and the military operations in other
islands. 1
It was thought the advance of General
Hale's brigade several days ago was
the beginning of a forward movement,
but it is supposed that General Otis
decided to await the arrival of Lawton.
Now it is expected that offensive opera
tions will begin at once and be directed
toward Malolos, the seat of the .insur
gent government, which the officials say
will soon be occupied by American
troops.
Instructions were cabled to General
Otis to-day directing him to detach
Brigadier General M. P. Miller from the
command of troops at Iloilo and order
him home for retirement. General Otis
will choose General Miller's successor,
who will probably be General Anderson
or General Mc Arthur.
-General Miller's retirement on March
27 will create a vacancy in the grade
of brigadier general of the regular
army, which in all probability will be
filled by the appointment of General
Sumner. The latter is likely to be re
tired at once and General Anderson
made brigadier of the regular service
so that that officer will not be given a
volunteer commission as major general,
as originally determined.
The cablegram received at the War
Department to-day dated Manila,
March 10. says the transport Grant ar
rived with the troops in good condition.
The Arizona and Newport leave to-day
for San Francisco, the Arizona via
Hongkong and the Newport via Na-
I gasaki. The Grant carried to Manila
the Fourth Infantry and four com
panies of the Seventeenth Infantry.
This adds to the troops under the
command of General Otis 42 officers and
1716 enlisted men. The Grant, with
.General W. B. Lawton in command,
Bailed from New York January 19.

War Department Decides It Has
Authority to Keep Officers
and Men.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
WASHINTON, March 10.â One of the
paragraphs of the army reorganization
act contained an, authorization for the
enlistment again of the volunteer
troops now in the Philippines. j It was
stipulated that these troops should be
re-enlisted only for a period of time
necessary to replace them by regular
troops, in no case longer than . six
months. r .
The question has been presented to
the War Department of whether or not
it was aide, under this provision, to
avail of th" services of the officers of
the volunteer regiments as well as of
the enlisted men until the troops can
be replaced by regulars.
The law officers of the department
have concluded that the word "enlist"
employed in the act was used in a broad
sense and that therefore the President
has authority to take over all the vol
unteer regiments now at Manila and
j other points in the Philippines into the
; service of the United States in organ
' izations, with their full complements
I of officers and men.
It has been presented that the vol
unteers there are longing to return to
the United States and to private life.
The officials say that this is a mistake.
General Otis has reported that the men.
volunteers as well as regulars, do not
want to come home, but are anxious to
fight and crush the insurgents. Nearly
every company has suffered through
the wounding of some of its. members
at the hands of sharpshooters and they
are. anxious to make the account even.

ARTILLERY ORDERED
TO THE PHILIPPINES
WASHINGTON. March 10.â In view
of the possible necessity of having to
use more artillery at Manila in the fu
ture than has been required in the past,
the War Department issued orders to
Battery B, Sixth United States Artil
lery, now at Fort McHenry, to move as
soon as possible to San Francisco, there
to take ship for Manila. Two light bat
teries of the Sixth are already at Ma
nila, and the rest of the regiment has
been ordered there, but some of it may
stop at Honolulu, relieving the Second
United States Volunteer Engineers
now there. The Sixth is one of the two
regiments authorized by Congress last
March.

TURKS FIGHT ARABS.
LONDON, March 11.â According to _ a
dispatch to the Morning Post from Bom
bay severe fighting has taken place be
tween the Turkish troops and the Arab
tribesmen near Shalil, in the province of
Yemen, Arabia, where an insurrection has
been in progress for ten months. The
Turks lost 160 men and the Arabs 300.

NEW FORK
WANTS THE
CONTENTIONS
In the Fight Early for the
Big National Party
Gatherings.
Bryan's: Opposition May Keep the
Democrats From Going So
. :â  Far East.
Special â¢ Dispatch â¢to The Call.
Call Headquarters, Wellington Hotel,
â¢â¢-. Washington, March 10.
If San Francisco is to make a bid for
one or both of the national conven
tions next year, it behooves her citi
zens to begin their campaign even as
early. as this, for New York has ac
tually entered the field to capture both.
A committee has been appointed- by
the Boards of Trade to look after the
matter. New York has had a hanker
ing for a political national convention
for a good many years, but it has
usually waited until the eleventh hour
before making any move toward se
curing it.
New York .possesses many attrac
tions as a convention city. It has a
building suitable for the purpose, and
it can also accommodate all visitors
without the slightest inconvenience. It
is safe to say that the national con
ventions next year will be either held
in. New York or Chicago. After the
experience -of the Republicans at Min
neapolis in 1892 and' at St. Louis in 1896.
the leaders of that organization are
convinced that it. is a mistake to hold
their convention in any place outside of
Chicago. or. New York.
The Democratic .leaders hold similar
views. They selected Chicago In 1896.
although that town offered no special
inducements.
Mr. Croker: and his friends will no
doubt endeavor to have the Democratic
National Convention of 1900 held in
New York. The .supporters of Bryan
favor Chicago, and as they are In
control of the Democratic National
Committee,' the chances are that the
latter city will be selected. However
if New York . offers sufficient induce
ments It may stand a snow of getting
the Republican convention. .
The., principal objections heretofore
urged against San Francisco have been
distance, lack of telegraph facilities
and the difference in. time.

PLOTTED TO
MURDER THE
AMERICANS
Porto Ricans Want Their
Freedom and Will Fight
for It.
FEELING VERY BITTER
Conspiracy Discovered to Enter
Barracks and Machete Soldiers
by Wholesale.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
NEW TORK, March Colonel
Hubbell, who returned with his com
mand, the Forty-seventh Regiment,
from duty in Porto Rico on the trans
port Manitoba to-day, gave some inter
esting information to-night about the
condition of affairs in our new West
Indian possessions.
"There are many persons," he said,
"who think we were welcomed to Porto
Rico and sent away amid a shower of
bouquets. This was not the case. The
Porto Ricans do not like the Ameri
cans. They want and are planning for
their freedom.
"We had more or less trouble all the
time with these natives, but their hos
tility was more openly displayed when
it was learned that we were going to
leave. All the while we were subjected
to petty annoyances. A number of at
tempts were made to knife our sentries.
The natives are treacherous and we
had to use severe measures at times.
"There was at no time an open in
surrection, but I discovered three
weeks ago evidence that the so-called
'Educational Society' was strong and
bitter in its antagonism. They held
meetings which we dispersed. We dis
covered and nipped in the bud a con-
spiracy which had for its object an en
trance into our barracks and the
wholesale macheting of our soldiers.
"Unless there is a great chance, I am
convinced that sooner or later there
will be serious trouble down there. I
should, however, qualify their feeling
by saying that they hate Americans,
not for their personality, but because
they want to be free. The feeling in
the interior of the island is more in
tense than along the coast. The Span
iards are not responsible for it because
they are migrating. --;' The , *__*__ there
do not like their old enemies, either."
NATIVES THIRST FOR
AMERICAN BLOOD
CHICAGO, March 10.â Tribune
will to-morrow print the following spe
cial correspondence from Porto Rico
from a Tribune correspondent:
SAN JUAN DE PORTO RICO, March
_._The real situation in Porto Rico is
not understood. People in the States gen
erally regard Porto Rico as a sort of
haven of peace. The War Department
has just requested of General Henry that
he immediately report how many regular
troops he could spare from the island.
The One Hundred and Forty-seventh
New York started home last Sunday. The
regiments that remain are so scattered
that In many places where there is neces
sity for a strong force only a corporal's
guard can be mustered, and territories
where there are continual mutterlngs and
the greatest evidence of discontent at
American rule are unguarded.
General Henry is in a dilemma. He
has only three regiments of troopsâ the
Eleventh Infantry, the Nineteenth and
the Fifth Cavalryâ and two batteries of
the Fifth Heavy Artillery.
"I need twice the troops I have," said
the general at his residence in San Juan.
"Because, of the easy victory of our
troops here in the war and In the appar
ent friendliness of the natives to the
American soldiers when they invaded the
island, a notion prevails in the States that
there Is little necessity for a strong force
to maintain order in the island. The idea
is erroneous.
"Tift 1 conditions are alarming. These
people are now clamoring for local self
government. They are no more fit for
local self-government than I am to run
a locomotive. More troops are ( needed in
the island. The seeds of discontent plant
ed by professional agitators are rapidly
growing' and can be kept down only by a
strong military force.
"The 111 feeling between the natives and
the American troops' seems to grow
stronger every day. The American offi
cers have to maintain the greatest vigi
lance to prevent their men from wreak
ing vengeance on the natives, especially
on the native police, for acts of violence
that are continually committed against
the troops."
At Caguay, a small town on the mili
tary road twenty-live miles from San
Juan, a soldier of the One Hundred and
Forty-seventh New York was murdered
last Saturday night in a manner that
illustrates the treachery of the natives.
Private Michael Burke of Company I had
entered the Porto Rico Literary Club ot
Caguay and was sitting at a small table
reading, when a native slipped up behind
him and with one stroke of a machete,
sharpened to razor edge, severed the sol
dier's head from his body and sent It roll
ing across the floor.
Another soldier happened Into the club
some time afterward and saw the dis
membered trunk of the dead man still in
the chair, with the- head lying where it
had rolled. Several Porto Ricans were
standing quietly around discussing the
matter. No one would admit having
seen the murder committed. Suspicion
fixed on one man. but when a provost
guard went out to arrest him he had fled,
and although the entire country around
Caguay was thoroughly searched for the
murderer he has not yet been found.
Only four nights before the killing of
Burke the bad blood between the na
tives and soldiers asserted itself in a
brutal assault on Private Fitzpatrick.
The native police arrested Fitzpatrick
without a warrant, and when he resisted
beat him into Insensibility.
The soldiers. of the four companies of
the One hundred and forty-seventh sta
tioned at Caguay waited until night and
then started out to "police the town," as
they called It. Three cafes were "po-

THREATENED
BY FLOODS
Hotel on the Levee at Shawneetown Where the
Water Is Steadily Rising.
SHAWNEETOWN, 111.. March 10.â city is threatened with a flood
similar to that which almost swept the town away a year ago. The
residents are moving out as rapidly as possible 'and all the livestock
has been removed. The levee is thought to be weak in some places
and a break is liable to occur at any time. Many men are patrolling
the levee. Business is suspended and the citizens who remain are in con
stant fear of being swept away.
Last April the levee broke without warning. Twenty-five people were
â¢drowned and many residences and business houses destroyed. The town
has been practically rebuilt, and, should another flood occur, the loss would
be tremendous.

NEW YORK, March 10.â Her
ald's Washington correspondent sends
the following: Information has been
received by the State Department
showing that serious consideration is
being given by the Chinese Government
to a proposition to make San Mun a
free port. By this action China hopes
to checkmate Italy's plan for the seiz
ure of that port and the creation of a
sphere of influence in its immediate
vicinity.
Since China's refusal to cede the ter
ritory to Italy the Rome Government,
has not pressed its demand and in its
failure to act the authorities be
lieve they see evidence that Great Brit
ain and Japan haVe taken such meas
ures as to show Italy the inadvisability
of continuing her efforts to acquire
Chinese territory.
In throwing open San Mun to the
trade of the world China will take a
step which will be entirely satisfactory
to Great Britain and Japan and pleas
ing to the United States.
Because of the important effect which
the granting of the Italian demand
would have had on the partition of
China the United States authorities
have been closely following the devel
opments in the Far East, and they now
appear to be satisfied that in the face
of the opposition of Great Britain and
Japan Italy's request will not be grant
ed Italy did not make this move with
out consultation with the powers, and
she had good reasons to believe that
she would be supported by them, but
it is now evident they have considered
their promise fulfilled in supporting her
claim with diplomatic representations.
I was told to-day that the matter is
not being vigorously pressed by the
Italian Government, and It is not be
lieved it will be.
No information has been cabled to
the State Department by Minister Con
ger relative to the Niuchwang loan
crisis, because it apparently has had no
effect upon the affairs of this Govern
ment. The newspaper reports showing
that it has been settled, however, are
accepted. as true and both in adminis
tration and diplomatic circles an easier
feeling regarding the Far Eastern situ
ation is apparent.

LONDON, March 10.â The weekly
papers issued on Saturday will treat
China as the cardinal question. The
Spectator will say:
It is coming rapidly to thisâ Europe
must make serious agreements respecting
the spheres of influence In China with well
defined boundaries,* or the effort to pre
vent a general war will be a failure. We
regret the necessity, for we can fore
see the terrible strain on' the resources
of Great Britain, which are already
somewhat overtaxed, but we can perceive
no other way out of the impasse. Lord
Charles Beresford's policy of keeping the
open door by reviving China and so ob
taining a dominant Influence at Peking is
impossible. Corpses never revive.
The Speaker says:
We are â  primarily committed to the
open door and the maintenance of the in
tegrity of China. We are also partially
committed to' an approval of the system
of spheres of Influence which may disin
tegrate China. Must we not insist that
the policy of partition go no further until
the matter has been discussed in Euro
pean conference? Meanwhile it Is our
duty to civilization to defend free trade
by every means.
The Outlook, regarding partition of
some kind as inevitable, urges, before
trouble becomes irretrievable, the con
vening of a conference of the powers to
agree to broad principles on which the
partition of China may be pacifically
effected. It suggests that The Hague
conference put aside academic points
and deal with the greate3t menace to
peace, namely, the international scram
ble In China. Connecting the Muscat
incident with the Far Eastern question,
I the Outlook remarks:
Of the five powers who have estab
lished themselves territorially in China
only oneâ Englandâ has safeguarded "her
seaway thereto by strong places for sup
plies and repair. Until the others are sim
ilarly equipped they must be more or. less
at the mercy of England In the event of
war, which now seems Inevitable.
PRICE FIVE CENTS.
PRESIDENT
WILL FIGHT
TOM REED
Washington Politicians Say
That the War Is On
in Earnest.
THEY ALL DEPLORE IT
Will Be a Historic Battle, and
William McKinley Will Exert
All of His Strength.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
Call Headquarters, Wellington Hotel,
Washington, March 10.
The administration's contest against
Reed for re-election as Speaker is oc
cupying much space in the Eastern
press. It is likely to become historical,
and may have a most important bear
ing on the Presidential campaign next
year. Reed's jealousy of McKinley is
well known. It is of long standing and
appears to have become chronic.
In discussing this subject a leading
Republican to-day said: "I am sorry
that events have so shaped themselves
as to force the President to fight Reed.
Mr. Reed is of course a tough customer,
and he can make a great deal of
trouble, but Mr. McKinley has demon
strated that he is a pretty good poli
tician, and I am satisfied that he will
prove to be equal to the present emer
gency. Mr. Reed was given every op
portunity to adjust matters on. a satis
factory basis, but declined to avail him
self of it. That the administration can
defeat Reed for Speaker of the Fifty
sixth Congress is the conviction of the
great majority of conservative observ
ers. Some Western man will be picked
out for the administration forces to
rally around. It may be Henderson of
lowa, or Hopkins of Illin Is, or some
unknown man, but the decision is in
favor of a man from the West.
"There is method in this, of course.
The bulk of the Republican member
ship of the next House will be com
posed of Western men. They will be
in the majority and should, in the na
tural order of things, dominate the
House. It will be easier to defeat Reed
with a Western man than with Mr.
Sherman or any one from the Eastern
States.
"Mr. Reed can also continue his fight
against the administration from the
floor, but the President believes that if
Mr. Reed can be reduced to the ranks
in the House he will become more tract
able. Anything would be preferable, he
thinks, to his re-election as Speaker."
It is pointed out that Mr. Reed was
opposed to the annexation of Hawaii,
which the administration favored; that
he was at no time in sympathy with the
administration's Cuban policy; that he
opposed the acquisition < '. the Philip
pines and of Porto Rico, and was un
friendly to army reorganization on a
permanent basis; that he opposed the
Nicaragua canal, which is regarded as
essential to the development of the ex
pansion policy, and the Pacific cables,
being responsible for the defeat of both
schemes.
It is suggested that his opposition
thus disclosed to the general policy of
the administration will be almost cer
tain to hinder action if he is Speaker
of the next House, and that moreover,
his great power and prominence en
courage the development of an anti-
administration faction in the party
along almost the same lines of opposi
tion, save on the money question, as
those adopted by the followers of W.
J. Bryan â anti-expansion, anti-army,
anti-almost everything that the ad
ministration is for. Moreover, it is as
serted that even on the money question
Mr. Reed's plans differ from those of
the administration.
Notwithstanding the opposition to
Reed for political reasons, it is con
ceded that he is the biggest and brain
iest Republican alive, and it is probable
that when it comes to a show-down
many of those representatives who now
talk bravely of opposing him will fall
into Reed's lines as heretofore.
MARYSVILLE WOOLEN
MILLS LIE IN RUINS
Frank Peck, a Bay City Wheelman,
Loses His Life During a
Conflagration.
MARYS VILLI:. March 10.â A fire
that broke out at 8 o'clock this evening
destroyed the Marysville Woolen Mills,
located in the heart of the city. The
blaze was first discovered in the engine
room on the lower floor of the two-story
brick structure. The supposed cause
was spontaneous combustion. \ 9y
During the progress of the fire Frank
Peck of Tuba City, aged 19 years, a
prominent . Bay City Wheelman, en
tered the building and was overcome
by smoke. He was burned to death.
Superintendent Knight places the loss
at $100,000; insurance, $80,000. A large
stock of wool and finished goods was
destroyed. The machinery was ren
dered useless. Luckily there was no
heavy wind. The fire- throws 100 per
sons out of employment.

COMPLETE ROUT OF THE MEXICAN
Senators Hoey and Burnett Bravely Stand in

Threats of Dire Retribution.
CALL HEADQUARTERS, SACRA
MENTO. March 10.â Senator Dickinson's
bill reorganizing the State Board of Har
bor Commissioners was finally. laid to rest
to-day, and with it were buried the pre
tensions of power that have been the
boast of Dan Burns, Major McLaughlin,
Hervey Lindley and their army of touts
during the past three months.
in vain did Burns, McLaughlin, Lind
ley and their followers line up the Re
publican Senators and threaten them
with everything from the displeasure of
the chief executive to political ostracism
if they failed to vote to reconsider the
vote by which the bill was defeated on
Wednesday; in vain did they insist that
It was the pet measure of Governor Gage,
and that, as such, it should be supported
by every Republican vote in the Senate,
and in vain did they further debase them
selves by calling to mind their humiliat
ing experience of yesterday when they
crawled in the dust at the feet of the
Grant men and begged to be allowed to
dismiss the Gharges of contempt against
Milton J. Green. The result was in every
case the same. The Grant men scorned
the advances of those who had been will
ing to do a servile act after being beaten
into submission and because of prospec
tive gain; they had the Mexican down and
knew it, and knowing it, were not dis
posed to surrender the advantages of a
victory so hardly won. The other Repub
lican stalwarts, led by Senators Davis and
Boyce, ignored alike threats and offers of
personal gain, and showed the way to
success that marked Burns as a boss
without a following, a politician without
power.
Among those whose votes contributed to
the defeat of the measure on Wednesday
were Senators Hoey and Burnett. Both
of these legislators have been voting for
Burns for Senator since the beginning of I
the session, and the fact that they broke
away from Burns and voted with the op
position on the Harbor Commission bill
caused great surprise and indignation in
the Burns camp. It was upon those two
men that the full strength of the Burns
batteries was turned last night and to-day
in an effort to pull them down and force
them against their convictions to vote to
reconsider the vote by which the hill was
defeated, or at least to vote to postpone
the consideration of the motion to recon
sider until some future time. Both Sen
ator Hoey and Senator Burnett, to their
credit be it said, refused to yield to either
threats or inducements of personal gain.
They had voted against the measure on
principle, and they declared their purpose
to continue to vote against it to the end.
That they carried out this declaration to
the letter was one of the bitterest drops
in the whole bitter dose forced down the
throats of the supporters of the bill this
afternoon.
The defeat of Burns and his followers I
was made more everwhelming by the fact |
that they made their fight not on the I
merits of the bill but upon a motion to
postpone action on the motion to recon- j
sider until Monday afternoon. Wednesday !
a majority of the Republican Senators I
and all of the Democrats put the stamp
of disapproval on the proposed measure
so far as Its merits were concerned. To
day the same majority put the seal of
their disapproval on Burns and his politi
cal machinations and pretensions. Hav
ing killed the bill, they wished to kill
Burns, and they did it so effectually that
there is no need of an autopsy to ascer
tain the cause of death. Should one
be held,- the verdict would be "overin
dulgence in political pretensions."
There were all kinds of lobby
ists on the floor when the bill ;
came up. It was not a good time |
for It. That every one of its friends was i
willing to admit; but there are certain I
restrictions to parliamentary proceedings I
and one of them is that when a Senator
gives notice that on a certain day he will i
move to reconsider a certain vote he must '
.make his motion or let his intention to I
reconsider pass from sight. It was a case j
of Hobson's choice, and Dickinson made !
his motion just after the time for the i
consideration of the Assembly file had j
expired.
In making his motion, he said, he had
one or two amendments to offer to the
schedule of salaries as set forth in me
bill, and he thought Monday was none
too soon to bring them up for considera
tion. Wolfe put the motion in definite
shape by moving that the matter go over
until Monday at half-past 3, and all the
supporters of the bill seconded the mo
tion.
Davis objected. He said the Senator
from Marin had asked that the question
go over from yesterday afternoon until
to-day on the ground that he desired a
full attendance of the Senate, and Sena
tor Davis called attention to the fact that
the Senators were present in numbers as
great as ever could be expected.
Continuing, Davis said: "I withdrew
my objection to a postponement from yes
terday purely out of courtesy. I ex
pected the measure to come up for con
sideration to-day, and so did everybody
else. We have all the afternoon and all
the evening to consider the matter, and
there is no need to put it off any longr.
We can reconsider the vote and consider
the bill, or we can vote it down and finish
it, but there Is no reason to postpone it."
Davis declared the bill was illogical
upon the showing of the author himself.
He called attention to the report of the
investigating committee and to the fact
that it charged corruption in the manage
ment of harbor affairs. He calleC atten
tion to the fact that two members of the
board were, by the terms of the bill, re
tained In office, and the third would be i
out of office, by the expiration of his
term, in two weeks. Both members re
tained must be honest, Davis said, or they
would not be retained, and within two
weeks the Governor would appoint a
third, also honest, and so all the Investi
gating committee could ask would have
been granted and so what was the use of
the bill?
"If there be corruption to correct," de
manded Davis, "why retain two of the
corruptionists in office? On behalf of the
Republican party, its heart and soul, its
rank and file, let us stand by our prin
ciples In spite of the temptation to use
the powers which have been given us and i
we will be doing higher politics than if j
we pass this bill.''
Then Leavitt had something to "say.
Measure after measure, he declared, had
come up, and had been laid over merely

HOEY WIELDS THE GAVEL
WITH MARVELOUS EFFECT
CALL HEADQUARTERS, SACRA
MENTO, March 10.â Assemblyman Law
rence Hoey of San Francisco was in the
chair this evening in the absence of
Speaker Anderson. The claims of most of
the newspapers of the State for the publi
cation of constutitlonal amendments were
the special order for the evening. All but
three of them were passed. The three
went over on the unfinished business file.
Mr. Hoey'_ troubles began when he
banged the gavel for order. Some one had
put a handful of parlor matches on the
block beneath it, and after the smoke had
cleared away he sent a page behind the
chair to hunt for his mallet.
"The House will come to order and the
clerk will read the title," said Mr. Hoey.
"What title?" asked Mr. Belshaw.
"To the bill." said Hoey.
"What hill?" Inquired Dibble.
"Any bill." yelled Hoey, and the clerk
picked up the file.
Then Belshaw respectfully called at
tention to the fact that Crowley of San
Francisco was smoking a cigarette
against rule 69.
"I don't see him doing it," said Hoey.
"The sergeant at arms will put Mr. Bel
shaw in his seat."
Down came the gavel and off went an
other bunch of matches and work was
resumed on the file.
Despite the "joshing," Hoey proved an
excellent Speaker. He got through with
an enormous amount of work, and dealt
without a shudder on points of parlia
mentary law that even Dibble would not
have dared dally with.
In a ringing, appreciative speech on be
half of the San Francisco delegation Dib
ble passed up to the desk one of the finest
things in the gold brick line that has been
handed out during the session. Hoey de
clared that "gold bricks being out of or
der we will proceed with the file," and
the fun was over for the evening.
The day session was consumed in the
final consideration of the "fender" and
"Santa Fe" bills, and no other measures
were passed.
This evening the following hills were
read the third time and passed to the
Senate: Trinity Journal, Fresno Expos
tor, the Capital, E. C. Rust. Chico Record,
Inyo Independent, Labor World, Madera
Tribune, Town Talk, Willows Promoter,
A. A. Taylor, Ventura Democrat, Butch
ers' and Stock Growers' Journal, Dally
Willows Journal, Herald Publishing Com
pany of Los Angeles, Blue Lake Advo
cate, Allen B. Lemmon, Evening Post
Publishing Company, San Francisco News
Letter, J. L. Childs, Mountain Messenger,
Napa Journal, San Jose Herald, Healds
burg Enterprise, J. B. Sanford, San
Francisco Call. Bakersfleld Californlan,
Sacramento Evening Bee, Big Valley Ga
zette, Vallejo Chronicle. Shasta County
Democrat, Gonzales Tribune, Williams
Farmer. Events, M. F. McFarland, Ful
lerton Tribune, Merced Star and M. C.
Duffle.

evening. It is the same bill that has been
brought up in various forms by different
Senators in the last two or three sessions,
and has never before reached so much as
a favorable vote.

ricultural societies may test their claims

provements out of their own appropria
tions. In Tuolumne the directors of the
fair in that district have expeneded toOOO
in improvements on the understanding
that the State would pay certain other ex
penses of the fair, principal among which
was the money given in prizes. The fair
grounds in Tuolumne County were pur
chased in 1889, and in that year, 1891 and
1893 the State allowed the prize money,

The gate receipts for each year have
reduced the total of the Indebtedness to
about $2000, and this is the amount of the
debt which now lies upon the association.
In 1895 and 1897, however. Governor Budd
vetoed the claims for prize money, and
that left the association so much behind.
Curtin's bill makes no appropriation. It
only places the claims in such a position
that at any time the agricultural district
may make a claim for money expended on
agricultural fair grounds which are the
property of the State and then test the
claims before the Board of Examiners.
Should the Board of Examiners pass the
claims under the act just passed by the
Senate it will establish them as just
claims: if the board refuses them it. will
allow the bringing to suit and the testing
of the claims in a court of law. where
they may be finally settled. There was
a good deal of active opposition to the
bill, but after some time spent in argu
ment by Its author it was passed.

DAN'S HENCHMEN
SCORE ANOTHER
SIGNAL DEFEAT
Promises of Reward
Unfulfilled.
TWO HUNDRED FAT JOBS LOST
TOUTS ARE BECOMING DISCOUR
AGED AT THE OUTLOOK.
Hints of the "Colonel's" Withdrawal
From the Senatorial Contest
in Circulation at
the Capital.
CALL HEADQUARTERS, SACRA
MENTO, March 10.â In the Senate to-day
the Burns forces were defeated and hu
miliated. The touts, big and little, sought
to secure a reconsideration of the bill to
reorganize the State Board of Harbor
Commissioners, but the friends of the
people on the floor of the Senate stood
together manfully and effectually disposed
of the scheme.
The engagement had a bearing on the
Senatorial contest for this reason: Prom
ises were made right and left that the
supporters of Dan Burns should be re
warded with the official spoils of the party
to which he claims to belong. There were
not enough offices in sight to meet the
demand of the touts for places at the
public crib; hence attention was turned
to the scheme to reorganize the Harbor
Commission. The bill proposed to oust
200 employes from office and give the
patronage, aggregating #2so,ooo per annum,
to the touts.
The Grant men saw the danger in the
proposition, and did not propose to be
caught napping or be misled by the cry
that to the victors belong the spoils. They
believed the pretense that places were to
be filled by loyal and faithful Republicans
independent of Senatorial considerations
was a fraud and a sham.
Diligent Inquiry and common-sense in
duced them to believe that Burns was in
a desperate strait for help and that the |
success of the bill would give him an op
portunity to promise 200 places. Beyond
that the Grant men were convinced that
the passage of the measure would be
regarded as a Burns triumph and the
people of the State were not in a frame of
mind to justify legislation favorable to
the Senatorial aspirations of Burns.
The monotony of the Senatorial situa
tion was not broken by the joint ballot
to-day, but it was gratifying to note that
one hundred and twelve members of the
Legislature responded to the rollcall. The
number required for a choice was fifty
seven. Considering that the members are
working without pay, the attendance is
very good. There are no indications that
the number will be diminished next week.
In fact the supporters of General Barnes
and Mr. Grant are receiving commenda
tory letters and telegrams from constitu
ents urging them to remain at their post
of duty to defeat the election of Burns.
To-day one member who Is voting for
Barnes was assured that the people of his '
district would not allow his pay to stop I
because the State had ceased paying tne i
legislators. The message said; "In op- i
posing the election of an unworthy man |
and advocating the cause of a clean and
able candidate you are working for tne
people, w ho will manifest their apprecia- '
tion of your services."
Inquiries at the various Senatorial i
camps to-night do not foreshadow Im
portant changes to-morrow, but It is be
lieved, nevertheless, that accessions to the
Barnes column will soon be recorded. His
supporters are confident and enthusiastic.
The Grant men are displaying fresh signs
of courage, and are naturally elated over
the defeat of the "organization" in the
Senate to-day.
The Burns men, with the aid oi the
Democrats, may attempt to force an ad
journment sine die at an earlier date than
Saturday of next week, but such an at
tempt will provoke a bitter controversy.
" There is no caucus talk to-night. Had
Burns succeeded in his legislative
schemes he might have started in anew
to agitate the calling of a caucus, but the
defeat In the Senate to-day has stunned !
the managers of the "Colonel's" fight.
Everybody says there will be "something
doing next week," but it Is the general
opinion that few important movements
will be made In the open before Monday
next.
The surmises pertaining to the events of
next week are varied and conflicting. Men
whose opinions are entitled to respect ex
press the view that Burns will withdraw
from the contest in order that a Repub
lican Senator may be chosen at this ses- |
sion. Others entertain the opinion that
Burns is too selfish and bitter to make
any concessions for the sake of the Re- |
publican party, and that he will use all j
the means within his reach to defeat the
election of anybody but himself. ThÂ« j
anti-Burns Republicans in the Legislature
may take the subject in hand and elect
despite the programme of "Burns or no
Senator."
The Southern Pacific is sharply criti
cized for its brazen attempt to force the
election of Burns, contrary to the ex
pressed will of a majority of the Repub
lican members of the Legislature. It Is
the comment that the corporation should
now retire from the contest and permit
the representatives of .the people to set
tle the Senatorial question in their own
way. ;-'._

Port of San Francisco, arrived this even
ing. â

BOYCE TRIES TO STOr
UNSEEMLY LOBBYING
CALL HEADQUARTERS, SACRA
MENTO, March 10.â Senator Boyce Is
getting tired of lobbyists and Senators
who lay aside their dignity for the pur
pose of securing another vote for a fa-
vorlte measure. He took his first step
toward their suppression by submitting
the following resolution:
Whereas, The exhibition on the floor of this
Senate on Thursday, March 9, during the roll
call on Senate bill 367, was not calculated ' to
promote deliberate and free action on the part
of Senators upon pending legislation; now,
therefore, be it
Resolved. That while this body Is taking ac
tion upon pending measures and during the
roll call thereon, no Senator, member of the
Assembly or other person shall solicit any
member of this body to vote in any particular
way, or induce or attempt to induce, any mem
ber of this body to vote or change his vote
upon the same, nor attempt by private argu
ment or solicitation to affect the action of this
body or members thereof.
Senator Rowell Is responsible for Senate
bill 367. It Is a measure defining terms
when used in connection with assessable
property, and there was much opposition
to that clause relating to money depos
ited in banks and making such money
taxable as part of the property of the
bank or corporation. When the hill came
up there was not much said, but by the
time the roll had been called several Sen
ators had left their seats and were rush
ing from desk to desk, while changes
from "aye" to "no" began to be heard.
It was finally announced from the chair
that the bill had been defeated, but later
In the afternoon the chair announced that
there had been a mistake in checking up
the roll call, and that the bill had In fact
been passed.
Senator Leavitt moved for a reconsid
eration and that ended the matter for the
day. but Boyce's resolution will proba
bly bring It up again.
SENATE STILL JUMPING
ON THE NEWSPAPERS
CALL HEADQUARTERS, SACRA
MENTO, March 10.â Senate bill 530, which
provides that newspaper articles of a cer
tain kind shall be signed, came up before
the Senate this evening, and was made
more emphatic by a vote refusing to re
consider the vote by which it was passed
yesterday.
The motion to reconsider was made by
Senator Smith, who, in moving to take
the matter up again, made another plea
for the . rights of a newspaper and the
general responsibility which has always
characterized it and which has made it a
power in the land. To make of the news
paper but a collection of signed articles
was, In his judgment, to rob it of the
element which has made it what it is,
and he declared that the bill should not
only be reconsidered but defeated.
Shortridge backed him up in his state
ments and then added a few of his own.
He said the law Was a dead letter any
how, and would never amount to any
thing, besides being vicious anad danger
ous legislation and a blow at the guaran
teed liberty of the press.
Gillette, who had voted for the bill, fa
vored a reconsideration because, he said,
he had discovered that the bill allowed
suit for omission of the signature to be
brought by any one and half of the fine
would go to the patriotic citizen who had
the nerve to bring the suit. It mattered
little whether the plaintiff was the one re
ferred to in the article; any one could
bring suit and half the money was his if
it turned out successful.
He thought the bill was loosely drawn
In that respect and so he said he would
vote to reconsider. The final vote on re
consideration showed only defeat for it
by the following:
Ayesâ Braunhart. Bulla, Curtin. Doty,
Dwyer, Flint, Gillette, Jones, La Rue,
Prisk. Rowell, Shortridge, Smith, Strat
tonâ
Noesâ Ashe, Bettman. Boyce. Burnett,

IS NOW IN THE
GOVERNOR'S HANDS
Passes the Assembly, After a . Hard

ACTUAL COMPETITION IS ASSURED
Southern Pacific's Great Bival Enabled to
Consolidate Its Interests in This State
and Carry Out Its Purpose.
CALL HEADQUARTERS. SACRA
MENTO, March 10.â Senate bill 27. by
Simpson, went through the Assembly
on its final passage this afternoon, with
only twelve votes against it.
It gave rise to the first redhot debate
that has occurred over any bill during
the present session. For a matter of
two hours or more there was some
mighty spell binding. Caminetti of
Amador made most of the noise; Dib
ble and Johnson did most of the work.
The measure before the House was
the much-discussed Santa Fe bill. It
permits the Atchison, Topeka and
Santa Fe people to acquire the 'Federal
franchise of the old Atlantic and Pa
cific road between Mojave and the
Needles, the Southern California Rail
way from Barstow to Los Angeles, the
right of way over the Southern Pa
cific's line from Mojave to Bakersfleld
and the San Joaquin Valley road from
Bakersfleld to San Francisco. All of
these franchises and rights of way the
road needs, that it may bring into San \
Francisco the first competing line that
has come to California's relief.
When the bill came up as a special
order for this afternoon Caminetti
opened the proceedings by calling up
his substitute bill, which was already
printed in the journal."*
Judge Dibble asked him to explain
before further action "was taken just
what his substitute was designed to
effect. Caminetti explained. It took
him just sixty-six minutes, but he ex
plained.
"In the first place," said he. "nothing
but the stock of the California rail
roads is permitted to be sold by the
provisions of my amendment. The
franchises and rights of way cannot
be transferred. In other words, the
buyer becomes a stockholder rather
than an owner.
"In the second place, bill 57 says in
one clause that no competing road may
be bought by its competitor. Thai's all
right as far as it goes, but the next
section nullifies it by providing that
any line now leased, competitive or
otherwise, may be purchased.
"What I fear, and the reason for my
amendment is, that in passing bill No.
57 you take from California the right
to revise or repeal these franchises by
transferring them to a foreign corpora
tion."
Then Mr. Caminetti opened out. For
over an hour he contended with much
eloquence that the bill was no more nor
less than a scheme to assist the South
ern Pacific to destroy possible compe
tition by amalgamating the new line
with its own. True competition, he
said, was practically an impossibility.
Judge Dibble pulled the constitution
on Caminetti and proved to the satis
faction of more than enough of the
members to pass the bill that Senate
bill 57 provided none of the fearful
things the gentleman from Amador
claimed it did, and after < a brilliant
speech in favor of the measure gave
way to Grove Johnson, who, in his own
meek way, told Mr. Caminetti he was
playing to the gallery.
Then a vote was taken on Caminetti's
substitution and it went down, 56 to 14.
The question was then on the final
passage of the original bill. Caminetti
jumped in with more -amendments, and
Dibble threatened to head him off with
a demand for the previous question if
he persisted in dilatory tactics.
"Choke me off!" yelled Caminetti.
"But answer to the people for what
you have done!"
Then Grove Johnson got up again and
delivered a telling speech.
"If I did not know the gentleman
from Amador as well as I do. if 1 did
not know his reputation for honesty
and integrity in all things, I should be
led to believe by his action here to
day that he was doing railroad politics:
that he was opposed to the Santa Fe
people in their endeavor to bring a
competing line into California to for
ever relieve her of the one road domi
nation that has held he> down.
"God speed the day when we shall see
in every pass and canyon of the
Sierras the headlights of competing
railroads. As a man who has his all.
little as it may be, tied up here, as a
citizen who loves his State, as a man
whose children and grandchildren have

making one occupation assist the other to
their mutual advantage.
On February 17 Mr. Johnson Introduced
In the Assembly a bill entitled "An act
to amend section 1880 of the Code of Civil
Proceedure. relating to witnesses." It
Is in the provisions of this bill that Mr.
Johnson's fine legal hand appears.
By some legislative process known to
those who are familiar with the by-paths
of legislation as it is enacted in the Cali
fornia Legislature, Mr. Johnson secured a
favorable recommendation for the bill by
the Assembly Judiciary Committee and
then secured its passage by the Assembly
without attracting to it the attention of
the several lawyers who are members of
that body.
It was then transmitted to the Senate.
where ft was referred to the Judiciary
Committee of that body, and but for the
discovery made to-day It is more than
probable that it would have received the
Fame consideration at the hands of the
upper house of the Legislature that it did
in the Assembly. As it is. there must be
an counting rendered by Mr. Johnson,
and that right speedily. ' - .

Now comes the nigger in the woodpile.
The law as it stands on the statute books
to-day Is the same as the above. bill with
the exception of the portion set in black
face type. This portion is Mr. Johnson's
addition to the existing law. Attention is
also directed to the enacting clause which
â  makes the law as amended by Mr. John
son take effect immediately, and also
makes it applicable to all eases now pend
ing in the courts.
Mr. Johnson has two cases pending In
court which would be materially affected
by the new law. So much so. in fact, that
Mr. Johnson would probably be enabled
to win a decision should the bill become
a law. , 'â¢
Mr. Johnson is attorney for the plain
j tiff in a suit now pending against the
Murdock estate. The suit is for $100,000.
j The details are not at hand to-night, but
â¢it Is said that Mr.' Johnson's proposed
I amendment is in keeping with the neces
sities of the case as viewed from his
standpoint.
Mr. Johnson is also the attorney for the
plaintiff in the case of Amanda Austin vs.
George Wilcoxson, executor of the es
tate of Jefferson Wilcoxson, deceased.
The action is for $150,000 alleged to be due
the plaintiff for services as nurse and
housekeeper for Jefferson Wilcoxson dur
ing a period of eighteen years. Roughly
estimated the plaintiff estimates her ser
vices to Wilcoxson as being worth about
Sent a month during the term of her al
leged services.
Under the present law Johnson can call
the plaintiff as a witness and examine her
as to all of the facts relating to her
claim as have occurred since the death
of her former â  employer but cannot go
back to a time anterior to such death.
Should the condition of the case make it
necessary in order to make a proper de
fense for the attorneys for the defendant
to call the plaintiff as a witness Mr. John
son would, under the provisions of the
proposed law, be entitled to go back to
the time of the plaintiff's employment by
the deceased, because the witness had
been called by the executor.
It will at once be apparent to all law
yers' that this amendment to what has al
ways been considered as one of the wisest
provisions safeguarding the rights of a
deceased person will work incalculable
harm if it becomes a law. If It be true.
as alleged, that by the passage of the bill
Mr Johnson seeks to gain an advantage
in two lawsuits -in which he is interested
as an attorney, both involving large sums
of money and both based on apparently
manufactured grounds, the matter be
comes sufficiently serious to warrant an
investigation by the Legislature.
FELL FROM THE DECK
INTO AN ANGRY SEA
W. LOWMAN DROWNED FROM
THE SHIP S. N. CASTLE.
Was Returning From an Ocean Trip
Made to Regain His
Health.
The ship S. N. Castle, which arrived In
' port late last night from Honolulu,
| brought the sad tidings of the drowning
! of L. Lowman. a passenger, who was.
making a trip for his health.
The accident occurred last Friday in
! longitude (150.35 west, latitude 34.11 north.
The vessel had been lying to for about two
â  days in a dead calm.- Thursday afternoon
I a breeze sprang up and the vessel again
i got under way. Friday morning the
! breeze was so strong that it was found
necessary to shorten sail. The sea was
boisterous and the vessel was rolling
! heavily. Mr. Lowman strolled on deck
and stopped at the after companion way
to talk to C. F. Merrill, a fellow passen
ger. A sudden lurch of the vessel caused
I him to stop back several steps in an ef
i fort to gain his balance. He struck the
rail and to], pied over it into the sea. Mr.
| Merrill at once sprang for the lifebuoy
! and threw it over to the struggling man,
who was fast being left astern. Captain
Hubbard was at the wheel at the time
I and at "once brought the vessel to and or
â¢ dered the small boat to be lowered.
A diligent search was kept up for over
an hour by the men in the boat, who were
' in imminent danger of losing their lives
owing to the heavy sea. Nothing was
found but the lifebuoy, and the boat final
ly returned to- the ship.
It is thought that Mr. Lowman was un
able to swim, as the buoy thrown by Mr.
' Merrill struck within a few feet of him.
He was about 40 years of age, unmarried,
| and resided at Los Angeles.

Appoint a Committee to Meet the

MB. MORELAND'S SUCCESSOR. j
! Rev. David Claiborne Garrett of
Portland Will Fill St.
: 0J; Luke's Pulpit.
Rev. David Claiborne Garrett of St.
: Mark's Church, Portland, Or., has ac
j cepted the pulpit of St. .Luke's Church
In this city, vacated by the elevation of
! Rev. TV. H.'Moreland to the bishopric
i of Sacramento.
. Mr.' Garrett was born in Burlington,
I lowa, December 23, 1850. He was edu
cated in the public schools of Burlington
and in.Griswdld College, Davenport. He
afterward went to Harvard University,
where he received the degree of A. B. in
1592. His first . pastorage was Trinity
Church of Davenport, where he built lip
the parish from a small congregation to
a large and flourlsh-ng church, clearing
off all indebtedness, erecting a rectory
[ and doubling the number of CQimnuni
-1 cants.
In 1890 Mr. Garrett received a call to'
St Mark's Church. Seattle, a newly or
ganized parish. After visiting the field
THE SATS FRANCISCO CAI/L, SATURDAY, MARCH 11, 1899.
he felt it his duty to accept and there
repeated the experience of the seven
years of his- rectorship in Davenport.
Within a year the church was enlarged
three times and a handsome rectory
built. Mr: Garrett remained through all
the hard times, declining numerous calls
to higher parishes. â¢
After three years he was called to go
back to his old parish in Davenport, and
also in Christ's Cathedral, New Orleans.
In 1896 he was elected rector of Trinity
Church, Portland, but felt obliged to de
cline that. The next spring through his
efforts the handsome new St.. Mark
Church was erected. Then Trinity Church
of Portland sent a second call and Mr.
Garrett, considering his work in Seattle
completed, went to Portland.
' During his rectorship in Portland the
congregation increased to such an extent
that the vestry is now planning to put
pews in the gallery. It was largely
through his influence that a debt of $40,000
has been virtually raised by the sale of
bonds to the church members. Mr. Gar
rett was married in 1883 to Miss Lily
Selmes of Boston and has three children.

LEAF TOBACCO TAX
FULLY EXPLAINED
INTEBNAL BEVENUE COMMIS-
SIONEB'S BULING.
Dealers Have No Excuse for Disre
garding the Law and It Will
Be Enforced.
On February 20 Collector John C. Lynch
prepared a very full statement of the con
dition of the trade In leaf tobacco and
forwarded It to the Commissioner of In
ternal Revenue,- requesting answers to the
different questions that seemed in doubt.
In answer to this letter the Commissioner
has given full information as follows:
Dealers in leaf tobacco will not be permitted
| to sell leaf tobacco In quantities less than a
i hogshead, case or bale. Neither will they be
permitted to sell directly" to consumers, and
they will be required to confine their sales to
! three classes of purchasers, namely:
Firstâ To other registered dealers in leaf to
hacco. â¢_*â â "; _
Secondâ To duly registered manufacturers of
: tobacco, snuff or cigars.
!* Thirdâ persons who purchase leaf tobacco
! in packages for export:
Dealers in leaf tobacco selling to other per
sons or in any manner than - as above
will be regarded as manufacturers of tobacco,
1 and all tobacco so sold by them will be re
' garded as manufactured tobacco and liable for
the tax of 12 cents per pound. .
-v qualified manufacturer of tobacco has the
rignt to sell leaf tobacco to other manufac
! turers of tobacco or clears in quantities less
! than a hogshead, case or bale. ,__._.
Dealers in leaf tobacco have the ri_nt_ to
qualify as manufacturers of tobacco, but
' should keep their business as leaf dealers
I separated from that as manufacturers.
Leaf tobacco sold by a manufacturer of to
bacco directly to consumers or to persons
other than duly registered manufacturers of
; tobacco or cigars is regarded to be a manufac
turer of tobacco subject to tax.
The Commissioner further says that the
permits required for retaining leaf to
bacco may be given at the end of the
month for all tobacco sold during the
month, but this is to be done under su
pervision of the Collector and must be
verified by a deputy. Finally, the Com
missioner adds that his "office notes with
satisfaction that you state there la no re
tailing done In your district in the sense
of direct sales from leaf -dealers to con
sumers." This is a' point on which the
Revenue Department will strictly enforce
the law. These decisions are given by
Hon George W. Wilson.' the newly ap
pointed Commissioner of Internal Reve
nue Mr. Wilson has been connected with
the' Revenue Department for many years
and Is thoroughly conversant with the
whole body of the revenue laws and
equally with the requirements of the
trades that these laws affect. Collector
Lynch and Revenue Agent Thomas be
lieve that the trade is sufficiently pro
tected by these rulings, and that the leaf
dealers especially have every cause -to be
grateful to the department for the very
liberal construction put upon the act of
August 28. 1804. which, If taken literally.
absolutely prohibits the sale of any small
quantity of leaf tobacco.
LIFE-SAVING STATION
FOR POINT BOMTA
BIDS WILL BE OPENED ON NEXT.
TUESDAY.
The Necessity for Such a Station Has
* at Last Been Made Obvious.
Work Will Begin
Soon.
Bids for the construction of the life
saving station at Point Bonlta will be
opened at the office of the general super
intendent of the Life-saving Service,
Treasury Department, Washington, D. C,
at 2 o'clock on the afternoon of March 14.
The construction of a life-saving sta
tion at this point will . provide a long
needed protection . to ships in .distress.
Marine men for years have looked upon
this promontory as dangerous, and com
plaint after complaint has been sent to
Washington without effect until recently.
The specifications call for a two-story
building of brick for the accommodation
of the keeper and crew; also a lookout
tower, an inside boathouse, an outside
boathouse, an outbuilding for storage and
a flagstaff and a drillpole. The inside
boathouse will he located near the point
on the Golden Gate side, and the lookout
on the old lighthouse site. The dwelling
Is to be on the coast about 500 yards from
the inner boathouse. The outside boat
house will be located on the north side.
A number of local contractors have for
warded bids.

new arrangement inaugurated at the
mole by the Southern Pacific Company. It
is the general opinion that the system is
calculated to injure traffic with the sub
urban towns by reason, of the discom

ACQUIRES PLACER GROUND.
PAYS OVEB TWO* AND A HALF

From the Beach of a Lake Drained by
a Big Tunnel the Investors
Expect to Extract Vast
Quantities of Gold.
BY HAL HOFFMAN.
Special Correspondence of The Call.
JUNEAU, Alaska, Feb. 27.â of
the largest and most unique mining
deals ever made in any country has re
cently been completed in connection
! with Pande basin, near Sitka. The
j parties to the transaction are the Pande
; Basin Gold Placer Company and Fred
! crick P. Voorhees, who is said to rep
; resent a New York syndicate of $20,
--j 000.000.
The property of the company has
been leased to Mr. Voorhees "for the
term of five years at and for the fixed
and certain rent of $2,650,000," to be
' payable in installments every three
j months. Twenty thousand dollars has
i already been paid; the first payment of
! $60,500 is due on June 30 next, and the
j second of $50,000 on September 1 next.
| After that each quarterly installment
| will be $132,500.
The output of the mines in excess of
I $500,000 for each, six months is to be di
' vided equally between the parties to
i the lease. All buildings, plant, tools,
. ' machinery and supplies used by the
i ! lessee shall belong to the lessor at the
â¢ expiration of the lease without any pay
i ! ment therefor". â¢ The properties are
placer and quartz.-
Maurice L. Muhleman, who was In
the sub-treasury in New York under
Sub-Treasury . Agent Jordan during
j Cleveland's second term. is president of i
; the Pande Basin Gold Placer Company
and H. S. Martin secretary. Benjamin
P. Moore, formerly Collector of Cus
! Toms for Alaska, and General H. I.
"Willey,. formerly Surveyor General of
i California, are among the leading
stockholders of the company and pro
: motors of the lease. General Willey is
now in California, having left Sitka for
j the south several weeks ago.
In Pande basin is a lake out of which,
j early last January, was drained sixty
one feet of water through a tunnel in
! order to leave exposed gold-bearing
â¢ sand on the beach. This was quite an
! engineering feat. A full description of
it was published in The Call on Janu-
I ary 13. A mining expert named Turner
1 of New York, from assays made from
Â» sand taken out of twenty-six holes dug
in the shore around the lake, reported,
I it is said, to the New York syndicate
' represented by Frederick P. Voorhees,
that the sand would yield $83 and some
I cents to the ton and that there was
j then, on. that average, at least $7,000,000
in sight. . - . . :
i Many mining men here are Incredu
lous pver this deal, and the second pay
ment of $60,000 will be a test of the
serious intentions of Mr. Voorhees and
his associates.

Miller. California's eccentric poet, who

Water Famine Imminent.
VALLEJO. March Several towns
throughout Solano County are threatened
with a water famine. The springs sup
plying Suisun are now dangerously low.-
The Trustees of Suisun at their last regu
lar meeting ordered notices posted for
bidding the use of water for any but
domestic purposes. Dixon, Benicia and
Valli are also short of their regular
supply.

DR. KARTELL'S
SLAYER CAUGHT
George LaymanceTaken
in Arkansas.
BETRAYED THROUGH HIS PEN
WROTE A LETTER TO A PERSON
IN OAKLAND.
Sonoma County's Sheriff, After a
Long Chase, Runs Down
the Healdsburg Mur
derer.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SANTA ROSA, March 10.â The long
search of Sheriff Frank P. Grace for
George Laymance, who last December
murdered Dr. Kartell at Healdsburg,
has ended. Grace telegraphed to the
Sheriffs office here this afternoon from
Missouri that he had captured his man*
and would arrive with him next week.
The telegram was as follows: '..':,
NORTH SPRINGFIELD, Mo., March 10.
To J. S. Austin, Under Sheriff, Santa
Rosa, Cal.: Arrested Laymance thirty
miles south of Fort Smith hint night on
line between Arkansas and Indian Terri
tory- Will be home in eight days. â 
FRANK P. GRACE. .
The crime for which Laymance must
answer was committed before Sheriff
Grace's term of office commenced. Lay
mance had been drinking and upon
coming out of a saloon in Healdsburg
was met by his wife, who- implored him
to come home. Laymance at once be
gan to abuse her, and, according- to
statements made by witnesses, at
tempted to strike her. Dr. Kartell, a
retired German physician, remonstrated
and Laymance whipped out a Â«kiiife and
disemboweled him on the spot. Lay
mance then disappeared. Kartell, after
lingering in agony for almost two
weeks, passed away.
Kartell's death occurred only a few
days after Sheriff Grace's term com
menced. Grace and his deputies at
once began a search for the murderer.
Two weeks ago a letter in Laymance's
handwriting was received at the Post
office in Oakland, and by a rare piece
of luck was recognized. The letter had
been posted at a .ail tow in Arkan
sas. Grace was notified and at once
set out in another effort to find the
man. Arriving in the Arkansas town
he found Laymance gone, but* the fugi
tive had left a clew and after chasing
him for over a week through all kinds
of country he located him late last
night and placed him under arrest.

FOR HEAVY ALIMONY
SAN JOSE. March 10.â suit of Mrs.
Emily Spring for a divorce from H. M. N.
Spring, the merchant, came up before
Judge Lorigan to-day on the question of j
an allowance of alimony. Society is deep
ly interested In the suit, as the principals i
are social lights. Spectators were denied \
the privilege of hearing the proceedings, i
however, as the court on motion of the I
defendant's attorney ordered the doors j
closed and outsiders excluded.
In her complaint Mrs. Spring asked for j
$400 a month alimony for the support of
herself and three children. Mrs. Spring
testified that her husband allowed her
J4oo a month while keeping house here.
Out of this she paid all hills. She was <
willing to take 800 a month until the suit !
was settled. Spring's attorney offered
$100 M month. This sum was not accepted i
and the court took the matter under ad- ;
visement, i Mrs. Spring told- her husband \
she was determined to have possession of i
the children and would spend her last cent
to accomplish this. She admitted having
it fortune of her own. enumerating among
her possessions an interest in the Gilroy
Hot Springs, the Williams Hotel at Gil
roy,' me lots in this city, and several
thousand acres of timber land in Mendo
cino County. The divorce will be stub
bornly foug"ht by both sides.

A CHINESE OPIUM
SMUGGLER CONFESSES
SAN QUENTIN PRISON, March 10.â
Another scheme for smuggling opium Into
the prison was nipped ln the bud by War
den Hale yesterday. The plot was dis
covered through the confession of a Chi
nese who used "dope" consigned to other
prisoners and consequently ' got into
trouble.
Ah Pue is a Chinese convict who does ]
the housework for Secretary Joseph El- |
lis. Forsome time he has acted as the' â 
medium between the users of the drug
and the "outside men." The opium would
be cached at night in the rear of Ellis' !
house and Pue would send it into the
prison wrapped ifi. soiled linen Intended
for the laundry.
Of late the wily Chinese has been in the
habit of using the opium himself. For
this a convict named Williams; serving
five years for a burglary committed in
San Francisco, threatened to kill Ah Pue.
The Mongolian became frightened and
finally confessed to Warden Hale that he
had been smuggling the contraband ar
ticle Into the prison. Williams at first
denied the charge, but at length admitted
his guilt and was thrown into the dun
geon to await the action of the Board of j
State Prison Directors to-morrow.

GERMAN CONSUL
ROSE DEFENDS HIS
ACTION AT APIA
Attributes the Trouble to the
Attitude of British and
Americans.
BERLIN, March 10.â The Reichzanger
published a series of . official dispatches
from Samoa, Herr Rose, the German
Consul, in his report of .the events prior
to January 4, dated January 23, says the
decision of Chief Justice Chambers in fa
vor of Malietoa Tanu was- bound to have
a stupefying effect, inasmuch as in a
written statement dated October 5, 1595,
Mr. Chambers declared there was no
ground for contesting Mataafa's assump
tion of the sovereignty if he were 'elected
King. During the course of the discus
sion prior to the outbreak respecting the
measures to be adopted to assure public
security, Mr. Maxse, the British Consul,
and Captain Sturdee.ofthe British cruiser
Porpoise, are reported to have intimated
their intention to forcibly prevent any ad
vance of the Mataafans and -to actively
protect the Tanuites. While' the Germans
only announced such military j measures
as were prompted by the necessity to
protect the lives and property of the
whites, the British guards stationed a
Mr. Chambers' residence would, owing to
the locality, have been drawn directly
into conflict with the natives.
Herr Rose then relates how he rode on
the afternoon of January 1 to the Tivoli j
Hotel in order, like many whites, to
watch, events, and how on his way there
he had to traverse a body of Mataafans,
to whom, however, he did not speak.
Shortly after 4 o'clock, the German Con
sul .continues, he rode with Lieutenant
Krelinghaus of the German- warship Falke
to Songi and home again. Thus, the Con
sul concludes, Mr. Chambers' accusation
that, he rode at the head of a troop of.
Mataafans' at 4:30, when they attacked
the Tivoli Hotel, is "pure invention," as
proved by the written testimony of Lieu- j
tenant Freiinghaus'. The Consul adds
that the first two. shots were tired by the
Malietoans.
Recounting the establishment of the
Provisional Government Herr Rose says
Dr. Raffel, the German president of the
municipal government at Apia, by his ac
tivity, soon won universal approval,, and
quotes the thanks he received from the
British Cousul and the captain of the
Porpoise. Thus, the Cons_il continues,
! the provisional Government was already
j an accomplished fact on January 3.
Relating to the attitude of the warships
the Consul says Dr. Raffel took measures
: for the protection of the British mission,
and he concludes by declaring Mataafa,
; did not take part in the fighting. More
over, he points out, he was recognized on
the English side by a letter published in
the Apia newspapers. According to Con
sul Rose Mataafa was not responsible for
the war, but up to the last endeavored to
prevent it.
In another report Herr Rose describes
the events between January 4 and 9. He
says Dr. Raffel closed the Supreme Court
on January 9 because he thought Mr.
Chambers was precluded by law from ex
ercising the functions of Chief Justice as
long as. the Government was' installed
contrary to his decision and- he remained
In office. After opposing Mr. Maxse's
views that Dr. Raff el's action was an in
sult to the British Hag, Herr Rose de
scribes the forcible opening .of the . Su
preme Court on January 7 by Mr. Cham
bers and Mr. Maxse, while the latter
threatened the bombardment of Apia and
j the Porpoise trained her guns on the Su
preme-Court. Continuing, Herr Rose
i says that at the time of the compulsory
reopening of the Supreme Court he- re
frained from applying to the captain of
1 the Falk.; to protect Dr. Raffel, because
: he did not wish that "armed detachment

of Guam.
EIGHT STORES AT AGANA
OF THESE THE JAPANESE IS
THE BEST.
One Run by an American Is More
Pretentious, but Its Stock
of Goods Is Very
Inferior.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
WASHINGTON, March 10.â The Navy
Department has received a dispatch
from a naval officer connected with the
Bennington regarding conditions in the
island of Guam. The report is from
San Luis d'Apra and describes the con-*
ditions at Agana. At this latter place,
he says, there are eight so-called stores,
besides smail wretched huts where na
tive aguardiente is sold. It is made out
of. fermented cocoanut milk.
The stores are classed: First, Manila;
second, Japanese; third, Chinese;
fourth, Chimarro (native), and fifth,
American. There are three of the Ma
nila stores where there are sold ready*
: made articles of apparel, notions in
1 general demand and canned goods of
: poor quality, but expensive. There are
also poor cigars made of â¢ native to
j bacco. The Japanese store is one of
I the best, carrying the same class of
: goods', with some additions. It has
| eggs and bread, the latter baked every
i other day and of a poor quality. The
Chinese store is "very poor. The native
store has a supply of native coffee of
fair quality and excellent chocolate,
also cotton goods.
i The American store is more preten
tious, but inferior to the Japanese. It
carries a large supply of canned goods,
clothes, notions and shoes and furni
ture.
Flour is difficult to obtain. Butter and
lard are not good, owing to the warm
climate.
- Chickens and eggs are plentiful. The
beef is poor and no sheep are raised
inland. There are plenty of pigs. Yams,
sweet potatoes' and corn are'abundant.
Â. Bananas, cocoanuts and bread fruit
are the chief sources of food of the na
tives. There is little fishing, fairly
good clams and a very small oyster of.
sweet taste. There are plenty of deer,
goats, wild turkeys, ducks and. other
game. _____________________

PEACEFUL MISSION
OF THE PHILADELPHIA
WASHINGTON, March 10.â It is de
nied positively at the State Department
that Rear Admiral Kautz has gone to
Samoa on the Philadelphia to restore
the statu quo existing before the last
emeute, to depose Mataafa and restore
Malietoa Tanu, even if force is neces
sary to accomplish these objects. It is
said the admiral has gone to Samoa to
meet the treaty obligation that is im
posed upon the United States to send
a warship there at least occasionally as
an evidence of the interest held by the
United States in ' the islands. While
there he will make a careful examina
tion of the conditions in the islands and
look Into the events up to the installa
tion of the provisional government, re
porting all the facts to Washington.
His uniform, under former regulations,
when the navy had an admiral, wpuld
be four stars on his shoulder straps,
,the end stars being upon an anchor.
Upon his shoulders he would wear two
very broad bands -of gold, between
which would be a band of yellow half
the width of the outside bands. The
yellow on his sleeves would extend
nearly to the elbow. By a special act of
Congress passed in the closing hours
Admiral Dewey will receive the pay of
the last general of the army.
FEW PROMOTIONS
FOR VALIANT SERVICE
"WASHINGTON, March Applications
for army appointments continue to pour
into the White House, from which they
are forwarded to the War Department.
It is a fact that more than 20,000 applica
tions have been, received for the 101 places
to be filled. > -.
Senator Warren said this morning he
thought the President would yet be com
pelled to use some of the 35,01*0 additional
troops authorized by Congress. Wyoming
is entitled to only one of the lieutenants
to be appointed, and the Senator desires
to give this to a sergeant serving in the
Wyoming regiment at Manila. He thought
merit, bravery and service in the army
should be recognized by promotions.
The comment is frequently made that
among the many promotions to the line
as the outgrowth of the war with Spain
few went to privates and non-commis
sioned officers who distinguished them
selves. New men have received the com
missions in nearly every instance. Prac
tically all the Influence now being exerted
is for the benefit of sons, nephews and
friends of Senators.

well and render wires for telephones su
perfluous. â¢ ;--/ .:
Orling. who Is a native of Stockholm,
has traveled and studied in America.
ENTOMBED ALIVE.-
DAVISVILLE, March 10.â A. E. Larke,
a tinsmith and well-borer, while sinking
tubing in the bottom of a 17-foot well on
the ranch of Mrs. C. E. Greene was
killed this afternoon by caving earth.
He was burled under three feet of earth,
and his cries for help could be heard
for five minutes after the accident. An
other man, Frank Noel, who was ip tne
well with Larke, had a narrow escape.
He was burled to the neck and had to be
dug out before the men could reach
Larke. It was twenty minutes before
Larke was reached, and he had expired
from suffocation. The well was a large
one and was not cased. It was to be used
for Irrigation purposes. Larke leaves a
widow and six children.
FROST DOES DAMAGE.
VISALIA, March Last night's frost
ruined the apricot crop. Peaches and
prunes were not injured to any serious
extent.
CHICO, March 10.â Heavy frost here
this morning caused great damage to the
almond, cherry and peach crops. Al
monds were well advanced, but suffered
more than other crops. At 6 a. m. th*.
mercury was down to 29.

PETER CASTRO
ARRAIGNED IN
HIS HOME TOWN
Angry Men Gather About the
Court, but No Violence
Is Attempted.
SALINAS, March 10.â people of
Castroville were under high tension
from sunrise till dusk- to-day. There
were rumors that attempts would be
made to lynch Peter Castro, the alleged
assailant of Mrs. James L. Douglass,
and counter rumors that friends of the
accused man would try to take the
prisoner from the Sheriff and liberate
him. The cause of all this excitement
was the fact that Castro was taken
from the prison here to Salinas to-day
for his preliminary arraignment. Sheriff
Farley was fearful there would be some
sort of demonstration on the part of
the enraged citizens of the town and
had taken every precaution to guard
his prisoner, but though throughout the
entire day men gathered in knots on
'street corners and about the court
room and angrily discussed the crime
and the punishment that should be
meted to the suspect, the proceedings
were concluded without an outbreak
and the prisoner was returned to the
Salinas jail.
When Castro appeared before Judge
Clark in the town named after his
grandfather he was worried and ill at
ease. Sheriff Farley kept a close watch
over his man and at no time was Castro
allowed to talk with any of his friends.
Castro, when asked if he had a law
yer, replied he did not and that he
needed none. Justice Clark gave him
until to-morrow to get his attorney and
set the preliminary hearing â¢ for that
time.
When it was asserted the prisoner
wanted no attorney the rumor at once
gained currency that he "would confess
and expose the gang -which has so long
terrorized the community. He did not
say anything damaging while In court
to-day, but it is thought he will con
fess soon. His family is anxious that
he ,tell everything about the Douglass
affair and divulge the names of his
accomplices in other crimes, if he is
guilty; but if he is innocent his rela
tives will defend him through the
courts. Mrs. L. Castro, the mother of
Peter, and his brother, William Castro,
of Watsonville, came over from Castro
ville yesterday and remained for some
time with the accused man in his cell.
William Castro said the purpose of
their visit here was to get Peter to con
fess. â¢-â¢ " .
Peter Castro is the grandson of Man
uel Castro, the -founder of Castroville.
The Castro family at one time owned
27,000 acres of land in one ranch and
