STRENGTHENING ESQUIMALT
VANCOUVER, B. C, March 10.â ar
rival to-day of six royal engineers from'
England for Esquimau excited some peo
had much wealth besides. The town was
founded before Salinas and it was the
most prosperous place in this valley for
some time. The Southern Pacific ex
tended its line south," left out Castro
ville and thoroughly established this
place, eight miles away. The wealth of
Castro dwindled, but even yet the heirs
of this old Castilian family retain some
of the original grant. They will let it
all go to save the wayward grandson.
A ripple of excitement was caused to
day when P. W. alias "Buck" Silva re
appeared in Castroville. Silva left on
the morning after the Douglass tragedy
and was last seen in Watsonville. Silva
asserted in his usual boastful . man
ner that he had come back to "face the
music" and if any one wanted him he
would find a warm reception. It is
now believed fact asserted by some
of the vigilance committee that Silva
will turn State's evidence and tell all
he knows. However, he most emphat
ically denies any knowledge of the
Douglass tragedy. Silva to-day said
that he was with Castro part of the
evening at a saloon. He got intoxi
cated and retired in the hotel at about
10 o'clock, as he expected to leave town
the next day. On the next morning
when he awoke he found Castro in the
room and dressed. " Castro said he, too,
wanted to leave town. Silva made the
further' assertion that on the night be
fore when he informed Castro of his
intention to leave Castro said he would
go too. Silva told him he had no spare
money to pay two fares whereupon
Castro exclaimed:
"I'll get the money to-night, you bet."
Each day brings details regarding
other crimes committed by the gang in
the past. Following the attempt to rob
and possibly murder Mrs. Johanna
Daily on January 7, a Chinese cabin
wherein lived several Mongolian farm
ers on the outskirts of Castroville was
broken into and the inmates badly
beaten. This crime was committed by
several men, and it is claimed "Buck"
Silva was the leader. Castro also is
asserted to have been one of the party.
The Chinese offered, great resistance
and succeeded in not only saving them
selves from robbery, but disfigured one
of the assailants.. Two of the Chinese,
were badly battered. "Buck" Silva was
arrested. It is claimed the jury was
"fixed," and he went free.
Even Father Mahoney, the Catholic
priest of that parish, was not exempt
from the depredations of the desper
adoes. The clergyman one night was
aroused by a knock at the door. With
out opening the door he asked what
was wanted. A voice which he believes
he recognized replied that they "wanted
to sing for him." The father grew sus
picious and politely informed his visi
tors he would have no singing at that
late hour.

JOHN BLUNDELL LEIGH
SUES FOR A DIVORCE
NEW YORK, March 10.â special
cable to the Journal from London says:
The first steps have been taken in a di
vorce case that will become famous.
The principal figures In the case are
the young Earl of Cottenham and Lady
Rose Leigh, both of whom are very
prominent in titled society.
Lady Leigh is a daughter of the Mar
; guis of Abergavenny, former president
! of 1 the Carlton Club. She is titled in
I her own name, having been Lady
Neville before her marriage to John
Blundell Leigh. She is a noted beauty.
! The Earl of Cottenham Is 25 years old,
; six feet tall, a master of hounds, un
| married, and much sought after in so
i ciety.
A Scotland Yard detective and Mr.
I Leigh recently followed. Lord Cotten
j ham and Lady Leigh to a hotel close
' to Leicester Square and broke in the
door of their room. The proprietor at-
I tempted to interfere and was thrown
I down two flights of stairs by Mr. Leigh.
The husband then in dramatic fashion
: pulled aside a curtain and revealed the
presence of Lord Cottenham and Lady
| Leigh. Mr. Leigh was prevented wreak,
i ing vengeance on the young Earl, who
; submitted with humiliating weakness
to his exposure.

STOCKTON'S ELECTION.
STOCKTON. March 10.â At a consulta
tion of the City Council to-night it was
decided that the city election should be
held under the primary elections law, and
one will be called for the 4th of next

first city to hold an election under the pro
visions of the new law. Republicans will
elect 104 delegates and Democrats 102 dele
gates to their respective conventions, and
the ballots will he counted* out of the
same box. The city election falls on the
16th of May. â  . .

LEARNING NOW
BEEF IS CANNED
Further Inspection at
Chicago.
ARMY COURT'S INVESTIGATION
CONTINUING THE FARCE AT
THE STOCKYARDS.
Will Test the Meat Packed in Their
Presence to Determine the
Amount of Nutriment
Contained.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
CHICAGO, March 10.â The Court of
Inquiry resumed its work regarding
beef to-day by continuing the investi
gation of processes of preparation at
the stock yards. Several more of the
packing houses were inspected, most of
the time being spent in examining the
methods of one of the companies which
deals exclusively in canned goods. The
various stages from the killing of the
beef to the labeling of the can were
witnessed.
After, having seen an animal killed,
the court followed the carcass to the
refrigerator rooms and from there to
the department where the meat was
cut into pieces for canning. Much in
terest was observed as to the charac
ter of the meat used for canning pur
poses. General Davis was the chief in
quisitor, the other members of the
court being content to simply watch
the various processes. From the cut
ting room the meat .was taken in large
Iron carts to the cooking room, where
it was boiled. Twenty minutes is' the
time allowed for this process, and when
the time had expired, the beef was
taken from the large vats and carted
into the canning room. Dr. Bigelow,
who is â¢ with the court as an expert,
got samples of the extract which re
mained in the vat and will examine' it
to determine the quantity of nutriment
which It contains.
The canning room was next Inspect- S
cd. Here the beef was cut into smaller j
pieces and thrown into a pressing ma- !
chine, through which it passed into the I
cans. The meat was then ready for
the "roasting" process, but as the
ovens were not quite ready,- the court
spent some time in looking over the
departments where the cans are made, j
This was done so that' the court might j
have full knowledge as to the manner j
of getting the cans ready for the beef.
After witnessing the roasting process.
the court left the packing house and
adjourned to meet at 10 o'clock to-mor
row morning.
Three army commissaries have been
summoned to appear, and it is expected j
their testimony will be taken.
SIGAFUS' FORTUNE
TO BE ATTACHED
CHICAGO, March 10.â is likely that
J. M. Sigafus of New York will have to
wait some time before he gets posses
sion of the valise containing $500,000
worth of bonds and certificates of de
posit which was recently stolen from
him at the Polk-street Depot and which
was recovered by the police. The valise
is in the hands of the Wells-Fargo Ex
press Company and was to have been
forwarded to Sigafus. who Is now in
California, but United States Marshal
Osborn has served the express com
pany with notice to he-Id the papers un
til further orders from the Federal
Court.
When creditors of Sigafus in Haver
hill, Mass., who hold a judgment of
$325,000 against him, read of the fund,
they immediately notified their attorneys
to attach the property. The 'judgment
against Sigafus was obtained in Los
Angeles after two years' litigation over
the Good Hope mine, but no property
could be found on execution, although
the creditors claim to have been aware
that Sigafus had a fortune in bonds and
securities.

Passed Many Forged Checks.
SALINAS, March Sheriff Farley of
this county is anxious to capture a forger
whose name is Joseph Lopez, but who ,
travels under many aliases. Lopez halls
from this county, but his operations have
been in other places until quite recently,
when it was found he had forged checks
and passed them in Salinas. One loser is
B. H. Davis, manager of the Southern
Pacific Milling Company in this city.
Lopez, so it appears from letters to the i
Sheriff, is wanted in Tulare, Fresno and
many other towns. He is a Mexican of |
good education and a skillful manipula- i
tor of a pen.
Officials Make Complaint.
SAN RAFAEL, March 10.â The county
officials have been making vigorous com
plaint for some time that the defective
condition of the sewer pipes is fast mak
ing life within the confines of the court
house not only unpleasant but dangerous.
At times the odor on the lower floor of the
building is stifling, and even the Superior
Court room on the upper floor is not free
from the fumes on a warm day. The mat
ter will be drawn to the attention of the
Board of Supervisors at the April session.

TEHAMA COUNTY
SHOOTING AFFRAY
Drunken Debauch Ends
in a Tragedy.
-A
INTOXICATED MEN QUARREL
J. - McGEE FATALLY WOUNDS
HIS STEPSON.
The Affray Occurs While They Are
En Route Home After a pay
Spent in Saloons
in Town.
Special Dispatch to The CalL
RED BLUFF, March Word was
received here from Anderson by tele
phone to-day of a shooting affray
which took place at Manton last night.
Full particulars have not been learned,
as Manton is situated in the mountains
about 35 miles' distant. J. McGee shot
and probably fatally wounded his step
son, named Brown. They were in
town together Thursday, and during
their stay both imbibed liquor freely,
leaving town somewhat intoxicated. It
is presumed .that a quarrel ensued on
the home ' trip and the shooting fol
lowed.
A man from the scene of the affray
walked to Anderson this morning and
telephoned for a doctor and for Sheriff
Bogard, who left for there this after
noon. He probably will return with
his man to-morrow. >

UNION BANK CASE
Board of Directors Not
Yet Named.
COURT WANTS MORE TIME
CONTINUES THE MATTER TO
NEXT MONDAY.
Judge Lorigan Intimates He Will
Reappoint All but Two of the
Members Who Have Just
Resigned.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
BAN JOSE. March 10.â The appoint
ment of a board of directors to close
the business of the defunct Union Sav
ings Bank was not made by Judge Lori
gan this morning, but went over till
Monday morning at 10 o'clock. The
directors, stockholders and depositors
were represented by counsel and
Deputy Sturtevant appeared for the
Attorney General. The court inquired
if a person not a stockholder could be
named as director. Sturtevant was not
certain about this and said the safer
way would be to name only stockhold
ers. He was of the opinion that one
who had acquired stock since the fail
ure could become a member of the di
rectorate.
Judge Lorigan said he was not yet
ready to name the directors. If Direc
tor C. P. Braslan had resigned he would
have had the stockholders elect a board.
As it was he would appoint six direct
ors, as Braslan would make the
seventh. He had received a telegram
from Gilroy asking the court to give
the stockholders of that section repre
sentation on the board of directors. He
felt inclined to give all sides a repre
sentative, but the depositors at the last
meeting decided they did not care about
It. The board of directors that had
just resigned was in charge of affairs
until Its successor had been elected and
it could make arrangements to look
after its interests at the meeting of
Howard's creditors to be held on Mon-
day.
Judge Lorlgan has intimated that he
will reappoint the directors, with prob
ably two exceptions, that have just
tendered their resignations. These are
Amasa Eaton, Cyrus Jones, W. H.
Wright. N. Bowden, L. Callisch, J. W.
Rea and C. P. Braslan. Wright and
Bowden are the only ones who were in
the directorate at the time of the fail
ure and Bowden had been elected but
a couple of weeks when the crash came.
The court has said he would not name
an attorney and in this case Bowden, in
whom all depositors have the greatest
confidence, will not be reappointed.
George T. Dunlop of Gilroy probably
will be named in his stead.
The board of directors of the Union
Savings Bank met this afternoon and
considered Howard's indebtedness.
They selected C. H. Bills as their favor
ite in the choice of a receiver in How
ard's bankruptcy. In case Bills be not
elected they ask the creditors to con
sider Jacob Miller. Ralph Hersey and
G. K. Fleming- for the position, in the
order named.
The depositors are not pleased with
the continual delay in the bank matter
and a large number of them now want
suit brought against the stockholders.

MEN IN DAWSON
Unabie to Leave Because They Have
Not the Wherewithal to
Make the Trip.
JUNEAU. Alaska. Feb. 27.â W. C. Ros
ner of 1611 Bush street, San Francisco,
who has been in Alaska about a year and
was quite a well-known young attorney
of San Francisco, has just returned from
a trip to Dawson. He was in business
there for a time. Mr. Rosner reports that
no such large number of men as were
reported to be on the trail a few weeks
ago are coming out. A decision of the
police to give accommodations at the
posts on the river to no one who had no
rnonev stopped the exodus before, in
fact, it got fairly started. Consequently
several thousand Idle men still have noth
ing to do In Dawson and will remain In
that condition indefinitely. A large num
ber of people will go down the river to
Eagle city in the spring when the ice
breaks.

CANADIANS WILL
BE DRIVEN OUT
Trouble Brewing on the
Porcupine.
AMERICANS TO RETALIATE
WILL BAR ALIENS FROM THE
DISTRICT.
The Miners Are Planning to Formu
late and Put Into Execution
an Exclusion Act of
Their Own.
By HAL HOFFMAN.
Special Correspondence of The Call.
JUNEAU. Alaska, Feb. 27.â A report
which came down from Porcupine
Creek by the steamer Wolcott, plying
j between Juneau and Haines Mission,
I caused excitement here. It* was to the
; effect that the Canadian flag had been
moved down the trail from Pleasant
I camp, on the Dalton trail, four miles,
so as to take in the Porcupine, Salmon
Creek and other placer mining districts
in American territory. Coming as It
did about the time sensational stories
were wired from Washington, D. C,
concerning the acquisition of Lynn
Canal by the British, it received con
siderable credence. Investigation has
j proved the report untrue up to the pres
ent time. It is known here, however,
that a Canadian police officer left Lake
Bennett recently for Lake Tagish with
j instructions to secure there an extra
j detail of police, return to Bennett and
proceed thence to Porcupine post.
The situation on Porcupine Creek is
strained anyhow. There is little doubt
j that should the Canadians get that sec
-1 tion of country many claims would not
; be abandoned without bloodshed.
Sentiment is crystallizing there
j against allowing any Canadian to make
; a location or to mine there in retaliation
I for the exclusion of Americans from
I Atlin. As is well known some of the
; Canadian police at Pleasant camp post
I have staked claims at Porcupine. A
movement is on foot to quietly notify
! all aliens holding locations in American
territory to get out of the country. This
would be done through a miners' meet
ing, and such a meeting would be
j secret, as the law gives aliens equal
rights to mine. Anybody who knows
' anything about miners' meetings knows
; that what the miners decide on is
; usually carried out to the letter. No
man would dare to stay in a mining
camp of the West or Northwest after
having been notified to vamose. It is
ten to one that the proposed action will
be taken in the Porcupine country.
C. P. Cahoon of Minnesota, the loca
tor of discovery claim on McKinley
Creek, is now here. He says he has
heard a large number of men express
themselves against allowing any Can
adian, policeman or not, to work a
claim there. Other locators on McKin
ley Creek are C. <}. Lewis of Los Ange
les, Cal.; Carl West of Los Angeles and
Charles Leitch of Los Angeles, W. S.
} Hawes of Minnesota, Alexander Mc-
I Conaghy of Colorado, Bert Wooden of
Colorado and Ed Hackley of Washing
ton State. Mr. Cahoon says these men
are opposed to allowing Canadians to
mine.
Some claim-jumping is now going on
in Porcupine, but not to any great ex
tent. Small steamers on Lynn Canal
are carrying more freight and passen
gers to Haines Mission each trip. At
the present rate there will be at least
3000 people on the trails and creeks
back of the mission in thirty lays. The
weather is now very cold and windy,
but it does not seem to stop the crowd.
It has been unusually cold at Atlin
and over the summit of White Pass re
cently, ranging from 50 to 60 degrees
below. Many menâ more than half the
construction force on the Skaguay
Railroad will quit work In a few days,
partly on account of the cold, but
mainly owing to a reduction of wages
from $2 50 to $1 70 per day.
STRIKE ON THE YUKON.
Railroad Constructors Oppose a Re
duction of Wages.
SKAGUAY, Alaska, March Seven ',
hundred of the 1400 laborers on construc
tion work of the White Pass-Yukon road
came out on strike yesterday as a result j
of a reduction of wages from 35 to 30 '
cents an hour and an increase of work
to ie;, hours a day. After the men struck
all the others were laid off for a few days
and the seven camps along the road were !
closed for the present. The men are
coming to Skaguay. and all are orderly. â 
No violence is expected. A refuge camp
Will be formed for them here, as there I
are no hotel accommodations. All the men
have money.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL- SATURDAY, MARCH 11, 1899.
THE THIRD WOMAN
HANGED IN CANADA
Crowd Jeers at Her in
Death.
FACES ALL MEN BOLDLY
DIES ON THE SCAFFOLD WITH
HER LOVER.
The Man Is Weak When It Comes
to Paying the Pen
alty for His
Crime.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
ST. SCHOLASTIQUE, Quebec, March
10. â Mrs. Cordelia Poirier and Samuel
Parslow were hanged here to-day at
8:05 a. m. Life was declared extinct in
eight minutes, the necks of both having
been broken. Mrs. Poirier, who said
farewell to her relatives last night, was
firm and collected throughout. She
took part in the mass said at 5 o'clock
this morning and on the scaffold shook
hands with the hangman without a
tremor. The crowd inside the jail
jeered at her, but even then her com
posure did not desert her, and at the
suggestion of the executioner she
turned and faced the jeerers and stood
erect and prayed to the last. Parslow
was more dead than alive when the
drop fell.
The condemned were taken to the
scaffold separately and were kept from
seeing each other by a screen placed
between them. Six hundred men who
had remained up all night witnessed
the execution.
Outside the jail there were 7000 more,
who tried to batter down the gate of
the jail yard and could only be made to
desist by the provincial police firing
their revolvers in the air. The behavior
of the crowd Inside was such that one ;
of th- priests, Rev. Father Meloche, had â¢
to reprove them from the scaffold.
Mrs. Cordelia Poirier and Samuel Pars
low, her reputed lover, were hanged for
the murder of Isldor Poirier, the woman's
husband, in 1897. The crime was commit
ted In the Poirier home in St. Canute.
Quebec. Mrs. Poirier. who was 33 years
of age. was the organist of the Roman
Catholic church at St. Jerome, where
Parslow sang In the choir. Both prison
er- confessed their guilt, each seeking to
put the blame on the other.
Mrs. Poirier was the third woman of
eleven condemned since the confederation
to receive capital punishment. The two
women who had previously suffered the
death penalty since the confederation
were Phoebe Campbell, hanged at Lon
don In 1872, for cutting her husband's
throat with a knife, and Elizabeth Work
man, hanged at Sarnia a year later for
beating her husband to death with a club.
The sentences of eight of the eleven mur
deresses were commuted to life Imprison
ment. The crime of six of these women
was the murder of their husbands.
PROTEST AGAINST
SUNDAY SALOONS
Los Angeles Temperance Workers
Address an Appeal to the City
Council.
LOS ANGELES] March 10.â Temperance
workers who are bestirring themselves
in opposition to the petition of saloon men
asking for the repeal of the midnight and
Sunday closing ordinances are circulating
a remonstrance to be formally presented
to the Council as. an expression of the
sentiments of the signers. It is in these
words:
To the Honorable Council, City of Los Ange
les: Whereas. There is a movement on foot to
influence the City council to repeal the ordi
nance for Sunday closing and midnight clos
ing of saloons In Los Angeles, and enact a
substitute by which saloons may run all night
by the payment of $10 a month, and run open
on Sunday by an additional $10 added to the
regular license of $."0 a month; therefore, we.
the undersigned voters of Los Angeles, respect
fully enter pretest against the repeal or the
law enacted by popular movement nine years
ago We furthermore favor strict enforcement
against all violators. We heartily favor the
recommendation of the Mayor and the Police
Commission that all saloons have but one
entrance, and that from the front.
In view of the strong efforts by the
conflicting forces and the complications of
interests of temperance people, retail
dealers, restaurant men, soda fountain
owners and druggists, the Council ap
pears in no hurry to adopt an ordinance
changing the present order of things.

WAS MRS. LUNG
DEVOTED THE
FLOWING BOWL?
Her Appetite Caused
Much Comment.
SEVERAL DEPOSITIONS READ
CORROBORATE THE HUSBAND'S
DIVORCE COMPLAINT.
Residents of Honolulu Tell What
They Know Regarding the Hab
its of Dr. Lung's Eccen
tric Wife.
The action recently instituted by Dr.
George A. Lung of the United States
cruiser Philadelphia for a divorce from
his wife, Mrs. Elizabeth Lung, on the
ground of Intemperance, was taken up
by Judge Hebbard yesterday morning and
a number of Interesting depositions bear
ing on the wife's devotion to the flowing
bowl were opened and read. Dr. Lung
charges that on many occasions Mrs.
Lung, whom he married in Philadelphia
June 1, 188S, disgraced him while laboring
under hallucinations caused by overin
dulgence in liquor. These charges are
corroborated in a large measure by the
depositions given by various people of Ho
nolulu.
The sworn statement of A. S. Hum
phries, an attorney of Honolulu, first en
tertained the court and spectators. In
the document he states that he was well
aware that Mrs. Lung often indulged in
intoxicants to excess, and when In this
condition she complained to the witness
that her husband was paying too much
attention to Mrs. Jack Winter of Hono
lulu, a charge which Dr. Lung indignant
ly denies. Humphries tells a story re
garding a painful incident which occurred
in the presence of various people when
deponent's wife met Mrs. Lung. The lat
ter referred to her new acquaintance as
a "flat nosed Chinese woman." a remark
that caused no end of scandal in Hono
lulu.
Humphries further stated that Mrs.
Lung's conduct was such that Admiral
Walker of the flagship refused to permit
her to board the vessel and informed the
officers that they should not recognize
her. Under cross-examination witness
testified that Mrs. Lung's remark asso
ciating her husband and Mrs. Winter al
most caused a conflict between Dr. Lung
and Mr. Winter. This trouble all origi
nated at the annexation ball, witness
said, where Mrs. Lung charged her hus
band openly with devoting all his time to
Mrs. Winter.
The deposition of Mrs. J. H. Paty is
short but interesting. She met Mrs. Lung
in a car one day, so she alleges. Mrs.
Lung was intoxicated and swore at her
tor no cause, much to her discomfort.
Miss Mabel l. add, who resides at ?â¢*>
Nuuanu avenue, in the island city, says
that Mrs. Lung was Of a jealous, excita
ble disposition and was given to drinking
to excess. When asked on cross-exami
nation If she did not know that Dr. Lung
treated his wife cruelly and consorted
with disreputable people, the witness dis
claimed all knowledge of such affairs.
Next Friday a demurrer to the com
plaint will be argued, and in event it i
overruled the trial will commence Imme
diately It is Mrs. Lung's purpose to
fight the case to the end. an intention
which may reveal a family skeleton of
striking characteristics.

Committee Expects to Make It Up
Within Thirty Days.
Ways to obtain means to liquidate the
$4000 still owed by the Democratic State
Central Committee were earnestly dis
cussed yesterday at a meeting of the se
lect committee recently appointed to raise

GRACE GREGORY TO
RETURN A BRIDE
(Photo by Alme Dupont.)
THE charming bride-elect of Senator Fred S. Stratton of Alameda County,
Miss Grace Gregory, now of New York, was formerly a resident of Oakland.
Some fourteen years ago she left this State, at first traveling abroad, then
settling down in Paris to a close study of music under the direction of the
famous singer. Boutry. Possessing the sweetest of voices, she often delights her
New York audiences with her chant-like melody. Her father was S. W. Gregory,
once prominent in the machinery business in this city. The family lived in the
Charles Webb Howard house on Harrison street. Oakland. The future Mrs. red
S. Stratton will find that she has not been forgotten by the old friends she left
years ago.

THE HARBOR COMMISSION BILL.
rROM every point of view the defeat of Dickin
son's Harbor Commission bill by the decisive
vote of 24 to 11 in the Senate is commendable and
gratifying. The measure was of the kind popularly
known as "peanut politics." It was pernicious in
principle and would have been wasteful and offensive
in practice. The Republican party can take credit
from the fact that the bill was killed by its representa
tives.
As has been pointed out by The Call, the bill was
without a single redeeming feature. It was a political
job, not pure and simple, but impure and complex.
It bore on its face every evidence of having been de
signed to create new offices with salaries for the bene
fit of office-seekers, and opened a way for the appoint
ment of an additional lot of political henchmen to the
payroll of the State.
The character of the support given to it in the Sen
ate was in due accord with that of the bill itself.
After Senator Boyce had made clear the facts that the
bill provided no remedy whatever for existing evils in
the administration of the affairs of the harbor, but on
the contrary tended to make them worse by the addi
tion of two more spoilsmen with their following of
hungry tax-eaters, Senator Morehouse frankly de
clared himself in favor of that kind of thing. He sup
ported the bill because it was a spoils bill, and made
no attempt to prove it be anything else.
The better element of the Republican party did not
lack a champion to reply to the advocate of the spoils
system. Senator Davis in a vigorous speech upheld
the pledges of the party to the people, and declared he
would not be one to make all the civil service planks
in Republican platforms to be but lies. As the result
proved he spoke the sentiment of the great majority
of Republicans in the Senate as well as in the State,
and nothing the friends of the job had to say in re
ply weakened the effect of his masterly defense of po
litical honesty against jobbery and the betrayal of
pledges.
The defeat of the bill is beneficial in many ways.
It protects the revenues of the State against the waste
of a useless increase of salaries, it tends to check cor
ruption in the management of the harbor and opens
a way for true remedial legislation, it baffles the
efforts of the Burns gang to provide more patronage
to be distributed in the interest of their boss, it up
holds the standard of Republican honor and marks
the taking of an important step toward ridding Cali
fornia of that petty spoils system of politics which has
been so pernicious to all the interests of her people.
It is now clear that this Legislature will deserve a
high degree of approval from the people. It has stood
firm against the intrigues of the Southern Pacific and
a gang of corrupt politicians backed by the Exam
iner to elect a disreputable and dishonored man to the
United States Senate, and in repeated instances has
shown a resolution to give the State an economical
administration. It has not been favorable to cinch
bills nor to political jobbery. It has given many evi
dences of a desire to keep faith with the people, and
one of the most -striking of these was the overwhelm
ing vote that crushed the schemes for jobbery and
boodle involved in the Harbor Commission bill.
Just why anybody should be posing as "Soapy
Smith" is not easily understood. Soapy was one of
the worst scoundrels who ever met a merited fate in
getting shot, and if the pretender can get himself ac
cepted as genuine there ought to be a lot of jails
yawning for him.
That regiment of immuncs which has been painting
a streak of red across the South seems to overlook the
fact that peace has been declared. If terrible in
war as after war is over, they would be bad people
to meet in action.
The spectacle of American soldiers being baptized
in the Pasig River will strike native observers as pe
culiar. Only the other day an army of natives was
driven into that same river, and so far as revealed here
is there yet.
Town Topics, a New York weekly publication, is
assailing General Miles. While this is doubtless un
pleasant for the gentleman, the situation would be
much worse if the paper were praising him instead.
While London anarchists who claim responsibility
for the explosion at Toulon arc in all probability ly
ing, there could be no harm in taking them at their
word this time.
Perhaps if packers were in the habit of canning dis
eased meat they would refrain from the practice while
a court of inquiry was engaged in inspection.
That Admiral Dewey's hair should have turned gray
rapidly is not surprising. The head under that hair
hasn't been turned any.
THE BURNS FLOP.
ANOTHER turn has occurred in the* Burns
policy. It is so acute as to excite derision and
supply the element of humor in this long and
rapidly dying intrigue. On Wednesday the purging of
Milton Green of contempt and his discharge by the
House was gall, wormwood, poke root and every
thing bitter and puckery to the Burns men. The ever
serviceable Dibble, who can always be relied on to
give a good reason for a bad action, in that cheerful
spirit which meets an emergency in politics by calling
forgery a clerical error and perjury a poetic license,
had shouted himself into' prismatic colors in benalf
of the honor of the House. He had asserted that to
purge Green was to disgrace the House. The Burns
touts invaded the floor and left the smell of their
fingers in the buttonholes of the members, and
ranted, persuaded, blasphemed and implored to get
support for Dibble's anxiety that the House should
not disgrace itself. In that behalf nasty blows and
nastier words were exchanged, and Green was not
purged.
Within twenty-four hours the scene and its spirit
changed. Dibble appeared in behalf of purging
Green, and himself, good political apothecary, fur
nished the purgative. One day before this had been
such deep disgrace of the House that Dibble mourned
lest his good name should go into hectic decline if it
were done. What was a deadly sin on Wednesday
was put among the beatitudes on Thursday, and by
the same men.
It was an admonitory* illustration of the idea of
principles, of right and wrong, of, the decent and in
decent, held by Burns and his supporters. One day
an act is denounced by them in every term and phrase
of contempt and deprecation, and the next day, with
a cheek as blushless as a crocodile's, they father the
same act and carry it out.
With them the sole measure and standard is the
effect of any act upon the candidacy of Burns. On
Wednesday they thought opposition to purging Green
would make votes for Burns, so they opposed and
put their opposition upon the high ground of prin
ciple, and declaimed in behalf of the vestal reputation
of the House. But all this brought no votes for
Burns. On the contrary, it repelled some that seemed
inclined to come. Whereupon the same men ex
changed the lilies and languors of virtue. for the roses
and raptures of vice, and, with only one sleep be
tween, on Thursday did that which on Wednesday
they had declared would disgrace the House and stain
it forever!
The quiet people who watch what goes on and
thank God it goes no worse will not fail to prop
erly analyze this change, and will hope that the same
men will not close the session by trying to reseat
Wright as Speaker of the House.
LET US HfIVE ECONOMY.
IT. is apparent now that California is to endure
another dry year. There have been droughts be
fore, but our prospective experience is novel, in
that we are faced with a drought extending over two
years. The field crop planters are already resigned to
the situation, and the deciduous and citrus orchard
ists are wisely sacrificing, in the arid districts, any
prospect of a crop in order to save their trees. In
Southern California, where the Sweetwater and Bear
Valley reservoirs have run dry, the orange and lemon
planters arc cutting their trees back, eradicating all
bearing wood and reducing the bark and foliage, so
that the tree will live on less moisture and be carried
over until normal rainfall is restored. So every in
terest that can protect itself and wait is getting under
cover, and under the circumstances the most unwel
come visitor during the next biennium will be the
Tax Collector.
The Legislature should not forget this. It is no
time for large appropriations to feed fads in State in
stitutions or support new official positions. Every
household in the State is already settled down to
economies fitted to the conditions of the drought.
Let the housekeeping of the State likewise adjust it
self.
The Governor has shown a commendable spirit of
economy and has used his veto in that direction with
praiseworthy impartiality. There are many measures yet
pending which will need his attention, if they pass.
Some of them create new offices and salaries. It is
highly probable that one of these is the Oakland
Harbor Commission bill. There is grave doubt in
commercial circles in Oakland that such an establish
ment is needed or will pay back in any service the
burdensome tax on commerce required for its sup
port. For reasons added to the water-front litigation
and derived entirely from the commercial situation it
is evident that Oakland will in no respect suffer if she
is not now loaded with this rather sumptuously sala
ried adjunct. The sentiment of the State government
should be no expenditure for ornamental or senti
mental improvements, and no new salaried offices,
except upon the plainest demonstration of their ne
cessity.
ADMIRAL DEWEY'S HEALTH-
A SENSATIONAL report sent out from- Van
couver a few days ago to the effect that a
British officer on his way home from Manila
had declared Admiral Dewey's health to be seriously
undermined has been promptly refuted from Wash
ington. Government officials have received no infor
mation of any illness of the Admiral nor of any need
or desire on his part for rest. On the contrary, a
dispatch was received from him some time ago say
ing: "I trust it may not be necessary to order, me to
Washington. Should very much regret to leave here
while matters remain in the present critical condi
tion."
The promptness with which the refutation to the
alarming rumor has been given is gratifying. At 'the
present time the sole danger point on the horizon of
our national affairs is at Manila, and while the danger
even there is not great, yet serious consequences
might result from any mismanagement of our dealings
with the natives and the rights of European resi
dents. It is, therefore, desirable we should have in
command at that point a wise as well as a brave man,
for the successful solution of the problems involved
in the issues requires the tact of a diplomatist as well
as the courage of a warrior.
Of course no man is essential to the affairs of a
great nation. Out of the ranks of a vigorous people
there never fail to come forth leaders to take the place
of those whom wounds, sickness, old age or death
remove from command. Nevertheless, the personal
ity of a strong, sagacious man is always one of the
controlling factors in the history of a nation, and
whenever such a man is found in the right place at a
time when his country needs him anything which
threatens to force his retirement is a matter of serious
concern to all.
Admiral Dewey has proven himself to be exactly
the man whom we need at this juncture in the Orient.
He has served several years in that part of the world
and knows its problems and its people better than
the officials at Washington than any man we
can reasonably hope to find when the time comes to
THE SAN' FRANCISCO CALL, SATURDAY, MARCH 11, 1899.
substitute another in command. Therefore the rumor
of his ill-health, coming from a seemingly well
authenticated source, was a cause of much disquietude
and there will be a feeling of no little satisfaction in
its prompt refutation.
BRITISH NfqVflL ESTIMATES.
GOSCHEN'S speech to the Commons on in
, troducing the naval estimates for the year
dealt with the proposed expenditure of large
sums with such a simple gravity of manner that at
times it was almost amusing. For example, he
gravely informed the Commons that the outlook for
the British navy is not reassuring, because the United
States, Russia, France, Germany, Italy and Japan
have under construction an aggregate of 685,000 tons
of warships, or 225,000 tons more than is contemplated
in the British estimates.
The quiet assumption in this statement that things
are not reassuring unless Great Britain constructs
every year more war vessels than all the other great
nations of the globe, with Japan thrown in, verges
very close on the humorous. The very' gravity with
which the statesmen of the little island in the North
Sea, located between the Irish Channel and the Ger
man Ocean, propose to go up against the whole world
when it comes to a sea fight has in it something of a
gasconade, and is the more entertaining because not
intended for a boast.
Mr. Goschen, however, was not humorous, nor was j
he boastful. He was quite in earnest. He would like
to see upon the ocean a British navy as powerful as j
that of all the rest of the world combined, and, finding I
the desire is not to be attained this year, he regards
the outlook as dubious and perceives nothing on the
horizon which he can point out to his countrymen as
reassuring.
Despite his somewhat despondent tone, however,
the Right Honorable gentleman, the First Lord of
the Admiralty, had a pretty good naval programme to
submit. It comprised in the aggregate an expendi
ture for naval purposes during the coming year of
Â£26,594,000, or considerably more than $100,000,000.
Of course not all the amount is for ships. Some is
for new naval stations, and the Russians and the
Chinese will note with passing interest that something
like $15,000,000 is to be expended in fitting up the
port of Wei-Hai-Wei, where, as Mr. Goschen
pointed out, there is a "valuable anchorage."
In the meantime, since the naval outlook is not re
assuring, her Majesty's Government is heartily sec
onding the peace proposals of the Czar. The confer
ence will not cost as much money as the naval es
timates amount to, nor even so much as the single
item of the anchorage at Wei-Hai-Wei. Perhaps the
British can find some pleasing promise in that pros
pect. It is safe to assert, however, they will none the
less adopt the naval bill without a division and put
another penny on the beer and the income tax and go
along grumbling, as is their custom, because other na
tions spend so much on their navies.
THE Senate passed a bill the other day which
should be promptly enacted into law by the As
sembly. We refer to the measure introduced by
Senator Braunhart and known to the legislative files
as Senate bill No. 202. It is a proposition to amend
sections 204 and 205 of the Code of Civil Procedure
so as to change the method of drawing trial juries in
the Superior Courts of the State. As originally pre
pared and introduced by Senator Braunhart its opera
tion was confined entirely to San Francisco, but the
Senate considered the idea so good that it enlarged its
application to the entire State.
The amendments proposed relate mainly to the
process of selecting the names of those appointed to
serve as trial jurors. The Judges are left to prepare
the lists as at present, but rules are set up which re
quire a more careful inspection of persons and a
greater responsibility is thrown upon the clerks of the
courts, who are commanded to examine the lists and
report upon the personal characters of the proposed
jurors.
Another amendment provides that no person shall
be obliged to serve as a trial juror for more than one
month in the year, unless a longer service is de
manded by a trial for which he has been impaneled.
Nor is any trial juror to serve on more than five
causes during the term for which he may have been
selected, and whenever a juror has served on five
causes the clerk of the court is required to strike his
name from the list. It is thought that this latter pro
vision, by making jury duty easy, will attract to the
box a more reputable class of citizens, or, what is the
same thing, remove some of their objections to
serving.
It is to be hoped that the Assembly will pass this
bill, if for no other reason than to give the new sys
tem a trial. Certainly the selection of juries could
not be looser than it is at present, and anything which
will throw around trials greater restrictions is in the
line of useful legislation.
WORKMEN OPPOSE THE Rf.CES.
I~*HAT workmen as individuals should oppose
racing as conducted at Ingleside is not strange.
That as a body they should have paused in their
routine to denounce it was unexpected, and brings
into the contest a new element to combat the iniquity
of the debased "sport" and to check the tide of gold
flowing into the hands of the touts and bookmakers.
The hangers-on of Ingleside, and also the managers,
threaten dire things in case the Supervisors shall
carry out the expressed determination to deprive them
of their power for evil. It seems certain that the
friendship of the workmen is far more valuable than
that of the gamblers. That their influence is greater
and their honest opinions are entitled to respect there
can be no question. The Supervisors, setting aside
the moral aspect of the situation, can well afford to
listen to the voice of these honest toilers.
Workmen are of the people. In the sweat of
his brow the laborer at any manual calling must earn
his bread,' and as he earns it, rearing a family and
providing for his home out of his wage, he has a
right to protection against the wiles of the confidence
operator. He ought to be assured that while he is at
his forge or bench his boy is not being robbed at the
racetrack, led into evil associations and trained to a
career of uselessness or even of crime.
There are many instances known of women neglect
ing their household duties and their little ones so as
to attend the races, there to bet and to squander the
scanty store which should go for rent or shoes, doing
this while the head of the house was away earning
more and never suspecting that the rounders of the
tenderloin were to have that same money to spend.
In some cases the result has been a tragedy.
It is good that the workmen have spoken officially.
Their clear and decisive words show how wide
spread is the interest in the subject, and will go far
to offset the threats and cajolery of the parasites of
which the community is striving to rid itself.
People with a taste for horse meat probably have
a' right to indulge it, but the average American sol
dier prefers beef.

BROKE DOWN OFF POINT REYES.
THE collier Empire came to grief while on her way to Coos Bay.
She sailed back as far as Duxbury Reef, when the wind became
unfavorable and Captain Mason anchored his vessel. Yester
day morning the steam schooner Luella, from Point Arena, came
along and towed her into port. It will be two weeks before the Em
pire is ready for sea again. ,

SUBSIDIZED FRENCH VESSELS
ELEVEN OF THEM ON THEIR

Helen N. Kimball Overdue at Pana

Flint.
The -team collier Empire returned to
port yesterday in tow of the steam
schooner Luella, The Empire left here
for Coos Bay last Thursday and all went
well until Point Reyes was reached, when
the crank shaft broke. This left the ves
sel helpless as far as the engines were
concerned, but Captain Nelson soon got
sail on her and headed for San Francisco.
When off Duxbury Reef the sea became
rough and a head wind was encountered,
so the vessel was anchored. One of the
black stack tugs went to her assistance,
but a bargain could not be struck.
Early yesterday morning several steam
schooners hove in sight and the Luella
bore down on the disabled collier. The
two captains came to an agreement and
the Luella took the Empire in tow. She
was brought through the Heads without
any bother, and when off Lime Point the
tug Sea Queen came along and relieved
the Luella of her tow. The Luella went
to Third and Berry streets wharf to dis
charge her cargo of lumber and the Em
pire was docked at Vallejo street wharf.
It will take a couple of weeks to repair
the collier, and in the meantime the Coos
Bay Coal Company will have to secure
another vessel to take her place. ,
The owners of the schooner Helen N.
Kimball are wondering what has become
of their vessel. She left Eureka on De
cember 3 last in company with the schoon
er Esther Buhne. The Kimball was bound
for Panama with a load of lumber and
the Buhne for .Honolulu with a similar
cargo. The Buhne arrived at Honolulu
December 30, discharged and loaded and
was back in San Francisco on February
2. A week later she was on her way to
Puget Sound and two days ago she sailed
again from New Whatcom for San Fran
cisco. The Kimball has not been heard
from since she left Eureka ninety-eight
days ago. Panama is about 1200 miles fur
ther south of Eureka than is Honolulu,
but the Esther Buhne has covered three
times that distance since she left Eureka
in company with the Helen X. Kimball.
The owners of the schooner are not
very anxious about her. They think she
may have reached Panama and that Cap
tain Guttormsen has failed to notify them
of her arrival. Still, none of the mail
steamers. mention having spoken her. The
Kimball is a stanch schooner, splendidly
found, and if anything has happened to
her It must have been In an exceptional
blow. She was built in Cuffeys Cove in
1881. and is 188 tons net burden. She Is
112.5 feet long, 80.8 feet beam and 0.2 feet
in draft. The vessel has been in the coast
trade since she was built.
American lumber Is in demand in South
Africa and a number of grain vessels not
liking the outlook for next season are
accepting charters for Cape Town and
Delagoa Bay. The Kennebec arrived at
Cape Town on March 2 from Puget Sound
with a load of lumber and the ships
James Nesmith and John A. Brlggs and
barkentine John Smith are now out
eighty-two, seventy-two and sixty-six
days respectively from points on the
Sound for the ports named. In addition
to these the British ships Olivebank and
British General and the American ship
M. P. Grace, now in this port, have been
chartered to go to the Sound to load for
South Africa.
Including vessels loading at the islands
there are now sixty-five American vessels
listed for this port. At the same time
there are forty-five British vessels, eleven
French, three Italian, two German, four
Hawaiian, one Norwegian, one Japanese
and one Chilean on their way here.
The above figures are given to show
what a subsidy will do for the mercantile
marine of a country. A short time ago
France decided to pay a mileage subsidy
to her sailing vessels. At that time it was
a very rare thing to see the French tri
color In the port of San Francisco while
at this time last year only two French
vessels were on their way here. Now
there are eleven and the chances are that
many more will be seen In these
waters before the year is out.
â  Flint & Co. of New York have decided
that there is nothing In sailing vessels.
Some years ago the British ship Persian
Monarch was wrecked on the American
coast. She was purchased by Flint & Co.,
who spent considerable money in repair
ing her, after which she was placed under
the American flag and renamed the May
Flint. Ever since she has been sailing in
and out of ports In the United States. 'but
now her American owners have resold her
to an English firm and the stars and
stripes are to be hauled down and the
cross of St. George hoisted in its stead.
The name May Flint is to be effaced and
the old name Persian Monarch replaced.
She is now under charter to carry coal oil
from New York to Japan. <-
Three tugs for use in Manila Bay and
among the Philippines have been pur
chased by the United States Government
in Hongkong. They are the Kar Shun,
7.cc Fat and Kura Hing^at present, but
the chances are that Admiral Dewey will
rechristen them as they are handed over
to him. Each tug is 100 tons burden. Â£7
feet long. 14 feet 7 inches beam and 8 feet
draft. They were built and engineered
by Chinese and were especially prepared
for the trip to Manila. They will be used
to transport troops from point to point
when necessary.

Captain Hooper
of the revenue
cutter McCul
loch tells a good
story of the tak
ing of Manila and
Spaniards
FEARED
The Monterey.
the consternation
. 4 Â§>+ created among
the Spanish soldiers when the formidable
Monterey steamed into the harbor. Under
orders from Admiral Dewey, when the
fleet moved into the harbor preparatory
to lowering the Spanish flag and raising
the stars and stripes over the forts, the
Monterey kept well inshore. The remain
der of Uncle Sam's warships, stripped for
action, stood well out In the harbor. Every
one remembers that not a shot was fired
on the city, and the Spaniards made no
attempt to damage the American fleet.
This surprised the naval officers and their
surprise was still greater when they saw
the fortifications and forts which the
Spanish failed to defend. Captain Hooper
was one of the officers detailed to go
ashore to receive the surrender of the Ma
nila forts. In the largest and best fort
were a number of great guns which could
have done much damage to the warships
if the Spaniards had been courageous
enough to use them. The commandant of
the fort was an Intelligent Spaniard and
spoke English fluently. After the surren
der had been made Captain Hooper sat
down to chat with this officer, and during
their conversation said to him:
"Pardon my question, as I ask it out of
curiosity, but why did you not fire on us
when you had such a fort and such guns
as these here?"
"Well, I'll tell you why," courteously
replied the Spanish commandant. "You
see those warships way out there? We
saw what kind of h they raised with
Admiral Montojo's fleet. Well, you see
that thing close inshore? {pointing to
the Monterey). We never saw anything
like it before and we did not know what
kind of h it would raise, and we did
not invite it by resisting the Americans."
Captain Hooper then gave the Spaniard
a general description of the Monterey and
her guns, at the conclusion of which his
listener remarked:
"You Americanos are devils for building
machines to raise h with."

SCISSORS ARE
EMPLOYED TO
HIDE EVIDENCE
A Murdered Man's
Coat Mutilated.
MORGUE OFFICIALS BLAMED
PRELIMINARY EXAMINATION
OF DANIEL SCATENA.
The Testimony of Dr. Zabala, Au
topsy Physician, Leads to Ad
verse Criticism by the
Police.
The preliminary examination of Daniel
Scatena. charged with the murder of his
partner, S. Brancoli, In their fruit store
on Larkin street, was commenced before
Judge Graham yesterday afternoon. Pros
ecuting Attorney Carpenter conducted the
! case for the people, and Attorneys Devoto
! and Demartinf represented the defendant.
The witnesses examined for the prose
! cution were O. Brancoli, Frank Tompkln
! son, D. McSweeney, Mrs. Elsie Willen
brick, Daniel Lyons and Detective Wren.
The evidence was practically the same as
given at the Coroner's inquest. Tompkin
son, the boy employed in the store, saw
two of the shots fired. Scatena came to
him after the shooting and said that
Brancoli had a knife, and the boy, being
frightened, did not contradict him. Yes
terday he testified that Brancoli had noth
ing in his hand. Mrs. Willenbrick also
saw two shots fired, and saw Brancoli
turning around toward Scatena just be
fore the shots were tired. McSweeney tes
tified that he heard the three shot.-, Ihe
first had a muffled sound and the others
two sounded clearer. Scatena told him
that the man who fired the shots had run
a The' theory of the prosecution is that
after the quarrel between Scatena ana
Brancoli the latter turned to leave in
store, when Scatena placed the moh
against his back and fired the first shot.
Brancoli straightened himself up as he
wheeled around, and the other two shots
were then tired at him. According to thi*
! theory the murder was deliberate and
I self-defense could not be established.
i Dr. Zabala, the autopsy physician, was
1 also a witness and his testimony sur
! prised the prosecution. At the inquest he
I testified that the cause of death was hem
orrhage following a gunshot wound. Yes
terday he testified that there were two
wounds. In one Instance the bullet had
entered the back, ranged upward and
came out at the left shoulder and was
the cause of death. The other had en
tered the right breast and lodged in the
chest cavity, where he found it.
The coat worn by Brancoli at the time
of the shooting was produced in court and
"it was in a mutilated .condition. The lin
i ings where the bullet in the back had en
i tered, had been clipped away with a pair
iof scissors removing all traces of tne
powder marks and burns.' The importance
of this evidence is shown by. the fact that
witnesses for the prosecution all testified
that Brancoli's coat was burning at the
back when they saw him. A portion of
the front of the coat had also been cut
Iwav The mutilation of the garment had
been made at the Morgue, but for what
purpose is not known. The matter will be
investigated, as there was apparently an
attempt on the part of some one to de
stroT an imnortant link in the chain of
evidence. The prosecution also contends
that three bullets entered Brancoli's body,
and they base their contention on the con
dition of the bullet holes in the coat.
The one in the back is not disputed, but
in regard to the other two there Is evi
dence that the autopsy physician maybe
wrong in his diagnosis that the bullet en
tering the back ranged upward and came
out at the left shoulder. There are dis
tinct powder burns on the left lapel of the
coat, and the inner part of the cloth Is
ragged, showing that a bullet made its
entrance there, and not its exit. On the
right lapel of the coat there are similar
marks, but the burns are not so distinct.
It is therefore supposed that three bullets
entered Brancoli's body, but the autopsy
physician was only able to find one. It is
also supposed that as each shot was fired
Brancoli was backing away from Scatena.
which would account for the difference in
the condition of the powder burns.
After the case for the prosecution was
all in the defense asked for a continuance
till to-day to consider whether they
would bring forward any witnesses.

WITH SAM RAINEY
Bookmakers Resort to Desper
ate Measures to Defeat the
New Ordinance.
Supervisors Declare That the Evil Must
Be Destroyed in This City
at Once.
The gamblers of Ingleside have over
reached themselves. When they boasted
that they could corrupt the Board of Su
pervisors as they had bought former mv
. nlclpal authorities they called forth a
storm of just indignation. The Supervis
ors declare, without exception, that full
justice shall be done to the people of the
city, and that no technical subterfuge
shall prevent the operation of the law,
which has been Introduced and will be
adopted, to kill the evil which is corrupt
ing San Francisco.
Before the gamblers made their boast
that the present Board of Supervisors
could be influenced there was a disposi
tion on the part of a minority to restrict
the evil of Ingleside rather than destroy
it. The assertion of the gamblers that
they would win their disreputable fight
â  by dishonorable means has had the wel
come effect of crystallizingsentlment upon
one purpose. The Supervisors are deter
mined now to kill the evil of the race
course in this city rather than temporize
with it.
When the gamblers of the racecourse
began their struggle to save Ingleside
from' municipal interference they knew
that they had the best elements of the
city to battle against. Men and women
of . cry class in the community were
arrayed against the racecourse. They j
needed only the inspiration of an official
action to unite and work. The track and ;
its attendant evils had done such incal
culable harm to San Francisco that all
classes were eager to begin a crusade of
suppression. The gamblers thoroughly
understood this fact and they chose what I
they thought to be their craftiest subter- ;
â  fuge.
They declared that they were uncon- 1
cerned over the action of the Supervisors, j
The municipal authorities could do what i
they pleased, so the gamblers of the race- !
course announced. They said they would
go, bag and baggage, to Oakland, where
they would ply their disreputable trade
a? allies and confederates of the Alameda
gamblers. This assertion was made and j
advanced simply as a trick. The Ingle
side contingent at that very time were :
scheming to destroy legislation that had j
been enacted against them.
Conferences were held and several ay- j
enues toward success were suggested.
1 The gamblers determined to buy immu
-1 nity, as they claim they have bought it j
before from the authorities of San
I Francisco. Profess! bribers were con- j
' suited and a plan of campaign was out
.lined. It is known that several of the \
most notorious manipulators of Ingleside
met and conferred with Sam Rainey at
the Palace Hotel. The purpose of the â¢
conference Is patent. The meeting took
:>!ace after the Board of Supervisors
passed theirprohlbitory resolution Monday
night. The gamblers had only one object
ln consulting the politician and middle
man.
Not satisfied with this endeavor, and
determined to leave undone nothing that !
might contribute toward success, the
gamblers of ingleside consulted Chris
Buckley. The Call has exposed the mean
ing of this meeting. A public duty de- ;
manded a plain statement of facts. The i
I result was not unexpected. The expose of .
the'gamblers' scheme cheated the manip- j
lajors of success. The Board of Super- j
visors was justly indignant and the peo
ple of the city were made aware of the
plot that had oeen batched by the mas
ters of the racecourse. The Supervisors
declare tnat no "golden eloquence" will
have power to interrupt the course of the
legislation that means so much for the j

racing blacklegs have advanced another.
hey declare that the prohibitory legisla- j
tion aimed against their criminal practices
is designed to aid a project for the estab
lishment of a. race course In San Mateo

noolselling are to be placed under the
C a - in San Francisco in order that a pro

tom" project of San Mateo County, and

Mr Spreckels denounces this assertion
unces this assertion

"no property interests that are affected
by the new ordinance of the Supervisors, ;

motest thought of doing so. I am con- j

On the War.
IN CUBA WITH SHAFTER
. By LIEUT.-COL. J. D. MILEY
Chief-of-Staff under Shafter.
With. 12 portraits and _ maps from
official sources.
12 mo 228 pp. $1.50.'
Colonel Miley was General Shat-
ter's Chief of Staff during the
Santiago Campaign. His book Is
an authoritative description, from
the headquarters point of view,
cf the difficulties and obstacles
which the United States troops
encountered and of how they were
overcome. His narrative will un-
doubtedly be accepted as the au-
thorized presentation of the cam-
paign. These pages set vividly be-
fore us the scene at Santiago, as
.well as for the first time recount
those details Of the expedition
which not only furnish a descrip-
tion, but also make possible a just
estimate of It.

effort to break them down has made them
more firmly set In their purpose. An in
terview with Supervisor Collins is typi
cal of the opinions of the majority of the
board.
"I voted for the total abolition of the
pool-selling at Ingleside and elsewhere In
this county." he said yesterday, "and will
so vote next Monday. I think nobody can
get away from the fact that the whole
sale betting on races as is done at the
present time is working an awful injury
to the public. Old and young of both
sexes have caught the craze to an alarm
ing extent and are wild to wager their
money. Jt Is time to call a halt to this
species of gambling that is driving so
many to ruin. 'Following the ponies,' as
I believe it is called, is rushing a large
number of young men and boys toward
crime. I believe, and I feel that upon
this Board of Supervisors devolves the
duty to abolish horseracing.
"As for those vile places in the back
streets of this city known as 'pool rooms.'
I am for their total extinction first, last
and all the time, now and forever. I
would not dare to show my face in public
if I voted for anything short of the com
plete abolition of such disreputable
places. They are frequented and support
ed principally by men who never nave
more than a dollar in their pocket at one
time, and by boys whose capital seldom
reaches more than 25 cents. So upon these
poor patrons the pool rooms live.
"I don't think there will be any change
In the vote next Monday, for I believe a
good majority of the board, if not all of
the members, are determined to abolish
the whole business altogether. However,
I suppose the racing people will move
down into San Mateo County, but we will
at least settle the matter here."
THE LABOR COUNCIL.
Carpenters' "Onions Are Booming and
Cigar-Makers Seeking Assistance.
At the meeting of the Labor Council
'last night all unions with the exception
of the Glass Blowers made the discourag
ing report that work was dull. All have
appointed committees to aid the cigar
makers in their fight for the supremacy
of blue label cigars. This fight practi
cally means the life or death of the In
ternational Cigar Makers' Union, and
great interest is being taken in it
throughout the country.
The carpenters reported that unusual
efforts are being made to augment the
membership of local carpenters' unions.
The charters have been reopened and
mass meetings are being held at regulat
Intervals. The next meeting will be held
in Scottish Hall on the 21st inst. Speeches
will be made by prominent labor workers
and the meeting will conclude with an
entertainment and dance. A committee,

attractive, stage and society stars being
pictured. Amateur Sport and World of
Law are particularly readable, as they
bristle with personalities. Eight pages of
exclusive club, society and political chat
are contributed by The Saunterer, who
discusses "Manny" Heller and the new
engagements and weddings. Charlie Ul
rieh's latest play. W. W. Beloin's New
York experience and other lively topics.
The stage and dramatic criticisms are
enforced by a special article by Willard
Batehelder. â¢

SOUSA VS. WAGNER
A Novel Concert at the
California.
THE music of Richard Wagner is
hardly as popular as that of John
Philip Sousa, judging from the mod
erate sized audience that turned out
for the Wagner programme last night and
the tremendous advance sale for to-night,
when an exclusively Sousa programme ;
will be played. Still. Wagner in his own ;
! little way is not an uninteresting com
poser and last night in the California Mr. :
â¢ Sousa stopped acting long enough to give
! excellent readings from many of the .
' master's music dramas. After each Wag
j ncr excerpt there was an encore, some
! times two or three, usually a Sousa
march or something of equal lightness
and dash. So there was music to please
everybodyâ the long haired and the short.
Mr. Sousa danced, pranced and gestic
ulated as usual in the light music. His
personal capers have become a part of the
Sousa marches. We all watch and wait
for them-that Is, all of us except the
bandsmen themselves, who seem to make
a specialty of looking the other way and
blowing straight ahead in legitimate busi
ness-like fashion. But In the Wagner
music Mr. Sousa did not beat the air in
vain and wonderful diagrams. He em
ployed none of his secret signals and wig
wagged not a bit. He directed with mod
! eration and dignity, and drew from that \
! perfect band of his a thrilling perform- '_
'â  ance of the "Gotterdammerung" funeral ,
: march, of the procession of the Knights
of the Holy Grail In "Parsifal" and of
the "Tannhauser" overture. Not since ,
the old days when John Philip played at
the Midwinter Fair have we heard such
good Wagner on a wind band. Miss
Maud Reese Davis sang "Elsa's Dream, .
' from "Lohengrin," in a nice, cool, in
; genue wav, and Miss Dorothy Hoyle gave
another example of her excellent violin
playing In the Wilhelmj arrangement of j
the "Prize Song." Altogether the concert .
I was unique and entertaining. There will
i be a bigger crowd at the matinee to-day ;
i to hear the "ballroom and dancing class
i programme," and a still bigger crowd to
! night for the "nothing but Sousa. fb.

Ladies of the Maccabees.
The first State Convention of the Ladies
of the Maccabees of California will be
held in this city on the 17th Inst, in
Golden Gate Hall for the purpose of elect-
I Ing the State's representatives to the Su
j preme Hive, which holds Its session in
I Port Huron, Mich., in July next. The
convention has been called by Mrs. Eu
! docia S. Moffat, the State commander.
The headquarters will be at the Lick
House. On the evening of the day named
there is to be an exemplification of the
work, and that will be followed by a ban
quet. The next day there will be a school
of methods in the parlors of the Lick
House.
Quick Trip From Alaska.
The codlishlng schooner Winchester
i made the run from Sannak, one of the
j Aleutian Islands, Alaska, to San Fran
; Cisco in the remarkably short time of
twelve days. She left this port on Jan
, vary 3 last, bound for Sannak, and made
i the run to the Shumagin Islands in twen
j ty-one days. Owing to strong northwest
erly gales, she had to seek anchorage at
â  Sand Point, where she remained six days
i under very stormy and alarming condi
tions, holding ground by means of three
anchors and all available chain; yet the
vessel dragged and nearly went ashore.
I As soon as the weather moderated she
proceeded to Sannak, where she arrived
! February 10. On this island the Alaska
| Codfish Company maintains two cod
fishing stations, which were in charge of
Captain Carlson, ho was the skipper of
the schooner Mary and Ida, one of the
company's fleet. The two captains re
spectively of the Winchester and Mary
and Ida started on a tramp to one of the
company's adjoining stations. Captain
Carlson was attacked with heart failure
and died on the road, where he was buried
with appropriate nautical ceremonies by
the crews of both vessels. The Win
"chester is unloading at this port a full
cargo of new codfish, and as it has been
out of the water less than a month the
fish is remarkably white, and it is prob
j ably the first time that a cargo of cod
fish has been landed in San Francisco In
â¢ such a short time after having been taken
I from the sea. This fact makes it most
i desirable for lovers of good fish, espe
j cially those who are adhering to the re
quirements of the Lenten season. The
Winchester will sail again to the cod
fishing station of Sannak on the 15th inst.,
and is expected to return to this port
about May 1, when the San Francisco
gourmets will again have a treat of new
codfish. The Alaska Codfish Company
expects to chronicle the arrival of new
codfish every sixty days, and thus prevent
corners In the market, which we were
threatened with a few days ago.

DISINHERITS
HIS WIDOW
A Lawsuit to Engage
His Heirs.
REASON IS NOW UNKNOWN
A CONTEST TO HIS WILL IS
BEADY FOB FILING.
The Document Was Drawn Five Days
Prior to Fowler's Death and Of
fered for Probate the Day
After.
When D. D. Fowler, a well-known min
ing man, who resided at 634 Polk street,
died on the 9th Inst, he left besides many
desirable legacies a rich opportunity for
bitter litigation. This has been seized
by Ella Fowler, decedent's widow, and
ln consequence the Fowler will contest
may eventually be numbered among the
most bitter ln the annals of local litiga
tion. Mrs. Fowler was disinherited by
her husband for some unknown cause
that is, unknown to the worldâ when
the story comes to light during the trial
of the contest it will doubtless be dra
matically interesting.
Fowler was an old man, having passed
his seventieth year, and was worth about
$200,000. For some time before his death
he was broken in health and it is said
even unable to transact business of the
most simple character. As an instance
of this disability it is stated that a few
days before dissolution he was asked to
indorse two checks, but the pen fell from
his nerveless fingers. Thursday he be
came weaker and just as the night fell
passed away.
Yesterday morning early a document
purporting to be the last will of the de
ceased was presented for probate. It
bears date of March 5, 1899, showing that
it was drawn just four days before the
old man died, and disposes of the estate
as follows: To Lizzie Fowler, a daugh- i
ter. $.000; to another daughter, whose I
name was not mentioned, $2500; to two I
sons, F. D. and D. D. Fowler, $2500 each,
and to Mrs. William D. Farren $2500. The I
residue of the estate was left to dece
dent's children, share and share alike.
The testator's widow was cut off with
out a cent. D. Farren and E. Groeneu
dyke were named as executors.
Farren offered the will for probate and
immediately appeared before Judge Cof
fey with an application for letters of ad
ministration. William A. Beatty of the
firm of Shortridge, Beatty & Brittan, was
present, however, and contested the is
suance of the letters on the ground that
a contest to the will would shortly be
filed by the widow. The contest will set ,
forth that the document was executed un- \
der duress and the undue influence of _.r. j
and Mrs. Farren. It will also be averred
that Fowler was Insane for' some time
prior to his death and consequently un
able to make a legal will; that while in
this condition he was influenced to make
the document, and for these reasons the
dead man's widow will go into court and
battle for a portion of the wealth he left
to his children and strangers to her ex
clusion. â¢
According to the story of the proponents
of the will the marriage of the couple
occurred at San Rafael about sixteen
months ago and the estate Is separate
property. It is said that in the latter
part of last year the wife caused her
husband to be arrested on a charge of
Insanity and had him taken before the
Commissioners of Lunacy, who, upon ex
amination, declared him perfectly sane and
ordered his immediate release. He was i
then taken by his friends to St. Luke's
Hospital, since which time he has lived
apart from his wife.
About a month ago Mrs. Fowler, ac
cording to Attorney Hassett. represent
ing the executor, sued for support, and
three days before his death she caused
the Sheriff to forcibly break in the door
of the room where he was living at the
house of Mr. Farren and had him served
with the summons and complaint in the
suit. The family difficulties appear to
have been due to money matters entirely.
M. Burdick's Death.
Detectives Gibson, Wren and McMahon,
after careful investigation into the death
of M. Burdick, the old man from Tomales,
in the Commercial Hotel from gas, are
i satisfied 'that it was accidental and that
! there is not the slightest foundation for
1 the rumors that he was robbed. He was
I known in Tomales as a "bum," and had
I not done any work for three or four
years. Silvio Capella, saloon-keeper on
Montgomery avenue and Jackson street,
i saw the old man leave his saloon and
' â watched him enter the Commercial Hotel
! between S and 9 o'clock on Wednesday
! night, and the night clerk in the hotel
saw him go upstairs to his bedroom.
I There was no truth in the report that two
I men were with him. All that was found
| in his trunk were an old coat, three dirty
I pairs of socks, an old pair of boots and
some shells in a cigar case.
READ THE SIGN BOARDS
Warner's Safe Cure the Victor
Among Modern Medicines.
It was once believed that a finger ring made from a coffin nail would
cure epilepsy. The tooth of a soldier who had been .killed in battle was regarded
as a remedy for toothaches. Imagine such ghastly nonsense as this parad-
ing as medical science! Tet the human mind, like the human body, must
creep before it can walk. In time men learned to reason and medicine was
put upon a rational basis. Back in the old days, some childish or disgusting
mess would have been used to relieve indigestion. Mark the progress Illus-
trated in this letter from J. E. Stockwell of Lincoln, Neb.:
"I have been badly affected with trouble of the stomach and have used
nearly all kinds of medicines and doctored with local physicians to no avail.
After being almost completely discouraged, I was advised to use Warner's
Safe Cure, which I did, and I am glad to be able to say that it is the only '
remedy that has done me any good. I have used three bottles and am
.feeling like a new man all over. I am thankful to the Warner's Safe Cure
Company for their medicine, and can recommend it cheerfully to those suf-
fering as I have suffered."
Another Instance of the victory of rational medicine is afforded by Jacob
Cramp of Birdsboro, Berks County. Pa., who wrote. May 10, 1898:
"I have taken five bottles of Warner's Safe Cure. It relieved me of the *
pain over my kidneys. When I got down I could hardly get' up again. I
did not know what was wrong with me. I could hardly do my work. I
heard of Safe Cure and thought I would try It. After taking one bottle I
felt better. lam very grateful for Warner's Safe Cure and would advise
people to try It."
Just one case more. Averett Perry, of Louisburg. N. C, says:
"I have taken only one bottle of Warner's Safe Cure and it has done me
more good than anything else I ever took. I had an aching feeling in the pit of
my stomach. I want to thank you from the bottom of my heart. I can
recommend Safe Cure as the finest medicine in the world."
Does the clear and energetic language of these men leave any doubt of
their sincerity? The stomach derangement spoken of is commonly associa-
ted with the approach of Brighfs disease. No painted Indian ever crept to
a settler's cabin more stealthily than Brighfs disease invades the body. A
dull white skin, tendency to perspire, indigestion and short breath give
warning of the enemy.
Lose neither time nor courage.' Take Warner's Safe Cure regularly, and
bear in mind that it has a record of twenty glorious years. It cures all ail-
ments of the kidneys, liver and bladder, including even the terrible disease
named for Dr. Bright. The road to health is open, If only you read the sign
board aright.

COMMERCIAL WORLD.
SUMMARY OF THE MARKETS.
Turpentine advanced 2c.
New Wool coming in on a dull market.
Silver firmer.
Wheat futures declined.
Parley futures still unsettled.
Oats firm and active.
Corn and Rye unchanged.
Another advance in Hay.
Lima Beans still higher.
Bran scarce and in demand.
Rice unchanged.
Potatoes continue to advance.
Onions weak.
Butter and Eggs slightly lower.
Young Poultry weaker.
Fine Oranges ln light supply.
Prunes firm and wanted.
Provisions slow.
Veal and Lamb weaker.
Mutton and Pork unchanged.
Beef rather steadier.
No call for Grain Bags.
DRY GOODS BOOMING.
The New York Commercial says
"The dry goods trade is booming. : If any
proof of that statement is needed a visit among
Jobbers and manufacturers will at once furnish
It. it is almost impossible to And a merchant
who is not busy and who is not more than
satisfied with the present state of affairs.
"With the cutting-up trade spring business
Is at Its height, and the productive capacity
of most houses is overtaxed to meet the de
mand. The clothing and cloak and suit houses
have completed the hulk of their initial busi
ness, and the volume of this has been so much
larger than anticipated that they are forced
t.> place considerable duplicate orders for
piece goods at this date. Much difficulty Is
experienced in securing duplicate lots of
woolen and worsted men's wear and
dress goods in the primary market, since most
mills are now running upon fall lines. In
creased difficulty Is experienced ln obtaining
the best soiling lines of cottons and silks, be
cause of the oversold condition of mills. Ad
vanced prices are willingly paid by buyers
where desired deliveries are to be had. The
men's weai woolen, the clothing and the knit
goods trades are rent with rumors of combines,
some of which are seemingly assuming definite
shape. -The operations of' buyers are only
slightly influenced by these movements, since
they are accompanied by the statement that
the" promoters have no intention of advancing
prices of finished goods."
DRIED FRUIT IN NEW YORK.
The New York Commercial Fays:
"With a steady though only quiet demand
for about all varieties of dried fruits there is
much in the situation that indicates improve
ment. The outlook is sufficiently encouraging
to warrant holding all supplies at high figures.
Conditions are promising, though at present no
especial change is noted. There is a steady
call for at>out all varieties of California dried
fruits, and prices are held firm on all, but
aside from that the market presents no fea
tures. Prunes are meeting a fair request at
steady prices. The pool of Oregon goods holds
prices steady on them, and California- are
in such short supply that the market remains
steady, with upward tendency. Supplies on
the coast are generally small, and the market
is firm at about 3%c basis for Santa Claras
and 3t33'ic asked for outside goods. On the
spot holdings are being rapidly reduced, and
prices remain firm in consequence.
"Peaches ire firm, and there is a strong ad
vancing tendency, even though the highest
prices known in years are ruling. Holders are
not especially anxious to make sales, even at
present high rates, and movement is restrict
ed in consequence. But there is no question
about the ultimate consumption of everything
obtainable, and some dealers say there is lit
tle ieason to doubt a further Increase in prices
as spring demand increases.
"Apricots are much reduced on the spot, and
few further supplies are obtainable on the
coast from first hands. Prices are firm at
the previously reported high range, but there
are indications that they will advance In the
next few weeks. Dealers are so confident of
this that they are refusing any concessions to
influence trade, and expect to compel buyers
to pay their prices."
