WEATHER CONDITIONS AND GENERAL
FORECAST.
The weather is cloudy over the Pacific
Slope from San Francisco and Carson City
north. Light rain Is falling along the Wash
ington coast. Elsewhere the weather is gen
erally clear.
The pressure has fallen decidedly over the
northwestern portion of the country, and a
storm of moderate energy Is approaching the
Washington coast.
The temperature has fallen decidedly over
Arizona and generally risen in other portions
of the Pacific States.
Conditions are favorable ror severe frosts to
night throughout California.
Forecast made at San Francisco for thirty
hours ending midnight. March 11. 1899:
Northern Californiaâ Partly cloudy Saturday,
with light showers on the extreme northwest
coast: warmer; fresh west wind.
Southern Californiaâ Fair Saturday; warmer;
fresh west wind.
Nevadaâ Cloudy Saturday; warmer.
Utahâ cloudy; warmer Saturday.
Arizonaâ Fair Saturday; warmer.
San Francisco and vicinityâ Partly cloudy
Saturday; warmer: fresh west wind.
G. H. WILLSON, Local Forecast Official.
EASTERN MARKETS.
NEW YORK STOCK MARKET.
NEW YORK, March 10.â The movement of
stock prices to-day was irregular, prices gen
erally being fractionally off, while some no
table advances were recorded among the
specialties. Local traction stocks were buoy
ont on the Idea that the action of the State
Senate yesterday diminished the chance of
hostile legislation in restricting the use of
Amsterdam avenue, which would particularly
affect Third Avenue, which rose an extreme
of twelve points, while Metropluan Street Rail
way rose nearly as much. Among the other
specialties to display strength were Tobacco,
American Steel and Wire, People's Gas, Ameri
can Spirits, Standard Distributing, Consoli
dated Gas, Brooklyn Transit. Âew York Air
brake, American Tinplate, Cotton Oil and Ten
nessee Coal and Iron, although the full gains
were not maintained. The railway and some
other standard stocks sagged slightly for lack
of attention. On the announcement that a divi
der..] of 1% per cent had been declared on
Union Pacific- preferred the stock eased, it
having boon previously falsely rumored on the
board that the disbursement was 2 per cent.
In the early dealings London was the princi
pal seller, while in the afternoon' selling ap
peared for both accounts on prognostications
that there would be another heavy decrease In
the surplus reserve in to-morrow's bank state
ment, but prophesies as to the bank state
ment based on the known data of Friday have
often of late proved misleading. The action
of the day's market seemed to show that the
specialties were pre-eminently In favor with
the professional element. The early dealings
were featured by an almost entirely concen
trated Interest in the highly priced stocks,
which moved in a sensational manner. The
standard stocks trailed along somewhat sympa
thetically, but soon developed a yielding ten
dency. In the late session Interest shifted In
the low priced specialties, which had not
moved proportionately with the rise in the in
dustrial division In the morning. In the last
hour's trading, following Union Pacific, the
market was of an exceptionally uninteresting
character, scarcely any Interest being mani
fested in the movement of prices. The clos
ing was dull.
Bonds moved somewhat hesitatingly to-day,
some active lines working upward, while others
yielded in sympathy with stock Influences
Total sales. $3,375, 000. United States new fours
registered advanced % in the bid price.

BRADSTREET'S FINANCIAL REVIEW.
NEW YORK, March 10.â Bradstreefs Finan
cial Review to-morrow will say: Although the
volume of transactions at the Stock Exchange
1 this week suffers by comparison with recent
ones there was In the main renewed exhibition
I of bullish speculation, which, however, had
, many signs of manipulative origin. The an
: thracite coal stocks were the chief features
i and the objects of the principal activity on the
, part of the large interests, and while some
of the industrials and specialties were promi-
I nent in the trading the standard railroad stocks
i seemed to be comparatively neglected. In
: de.-ci. while realizing sales became more abun
j dant as the week drew to its close, the pro
cess was somewhat accelerated on Thursday by
' the fact that the St. Paul directors on that
1 day made no change in the half-yearly divi
dend rate of 2% per cent and that indications
i were considered unfavorable to any increase of |
â¢ the 1% per cent semi-annual rate on Union Pa
cific preferred. It might be observed, how- J
ever, that on Wednesday last the absence of j
i any change in the American Sugar Company's
â  dividendâ the directors declaring the usual 3
per cent on the quarter on the common stock â
temporarily postponed the tendency to liquidate
; and in the opinion of the street at large
checked a disposition on the part of the in
dustrial stocks to break, which it was expected
I would ensue had the Sugar dividend been
I passed or even reduced. As already Indicated
professional leadership was responsible for the
more important movements in the list, and
though there were reports that the sharp up
ward spurt In the coal stocks was encouraged
by powerful Inside interests, who based their
action on the pending announcement of im
portant deals in connection with the anthra
cite trade and the corporations engaged in It,
there was ample ground on which to form the
' conclusion that operations by manipulative
; operators were even more effective. The part
1 which the public, acting through commission
. houses, has played in the movement has been
one of decreased importance. judging from the
activity of such interests, though there is no
apparent disposition to liquidate and profit
taking sales are conducted so leisurely that
the bear traders met with comparatively little
I success in hammering prices. > London's Inter
est In our market has been confined to a few
specialties.
BRADSTREET'S ON TRADE CONDITIONS.
NEW YORK. March 10.â Bradstreefs to
morrow will say: Industrial and trade activity
; Is still a prominent feature of the general bust
; ness situation. Price strength, too, .is notable,
1 especially in view of the midwinter rise of Feb-
I ruary, except ln the case of the leading cereals
j and wool. In manufacturing lines the situa-
I tion is one of great activity. Additional ad
; vances ln wages announced this week eeem
j likely to swell the number of workers whose
I wages are reported advanced to close to 175,
--000. The Iron and steel trade Is still excited
by a feverish demand, inadequate supplies and
further advances in quotations. Shut-downs
for repairs and other reasons have reduced the
pig Iron output materially and current pro
duction is said to be considerably below that
: of January 1. The number of old furnaces be
; Ing furnished up and put in operation Is stead
â  ily increasing, but the effect as yet in pro
duction is not very marked. An easier ten
-1 dency in wool Is one of the disappointing feat
j ures of the week, the outgrowth particularly
I of unsatisfactory demand for men's wear goods,
j but also a reflection of reports of projected
I combinations in the finished goods trade.
Lumber Is active at most markets, and prices
are firm because of well cleaned up stocks. Raw
and refined sugar have alike moved upward, j
partly because of unfavorable Cuban crop ad
vices and also in sympathy with the improve- I
ment going on In European beet markets. Re
fined has been marked up, notwithstanding the
trade war now going on. Wheat is weaker,
notwithstanding crop damage reports, oriel other
cereals are in sympathy, chiefly because of the
growth of bearish views of supplies held by
farmers and a. tendency to discount the gener
ally expected heavy Government estimates of
wheat In farmers' hands.
Reports from leading markets East and West
are of an active spring jobbing demand, par
ticularly for dry goods, but shared ln by
clothing, shoes and other staples. On the Pa
cific Coast business is fairly good except at San
Francisco, - where general trade Is below the
average, owing to uncertainty regarding the
crops.
Wheat, including flour shipments for the week
aggregated 4,398,821 bushels against 5,815,585
! bushels last week: 4.484.761 bushels in the cor
responding week of 1898; 1,589,482 bushels In 1897
--2,401,209 bushels in 1896 and 2.791,416 bushels ln
1895.
Since July 1 this season the exports of wheat
aggregate 173.111.755 bushels against 168,941,514
bushels last year.
Business failures this week number 177, as
compared with ISO last week; 247 in this week
a year ago; 227 in 1897, 282 in 1896 and 262 in
1895.
' Failures for the week in the Dominion of
Canada number 37, as aginst 37 last week 31
in this week a year ago. 56 in 1897. 68 in 1896. !
and 39 In 1895.
KIN'S REVIEW OF TRADE.
NEW YORK, March 10.â R. G. Dun & Co.'s
weekly review of trade will say to-morrow:
By far the best assurance of good business
in the future Is found in the good advances
of wages. The general rise in the earnings
of the cotton mill operatives, in most cases
10 per cent, and of Iron and steel workers,
generally 10 per cent, and tin plate and sheet
workers from 5 to 10 per cent, and of coal
miners in some large districts about as much,
has set a pace which most establishments will
follow which have reduced wages in the past
years of depression. Those who fall to com
prehend what such a change may mean have
only to consider that a 10 per cent increase In
the wages of all labor would put into the
markets a new buying demand. In amount
equal to about three-quarters of the entire
value of exports to all countries. The volume
of business shows no signs of decrease, and
lor - tha first full â  week -of March baa been
THE SAN FRANCISCO CAT.I., SATURDAY, IVIARCH 11. 1899.
about 51.7 per cent greater than the same
week Of Inst year and 61.7 per cent greater
than In the same week of last year, and 60.9
per cent greater than In 1892.
Most people would not expect | the produc
tion of pigiron to be affected by the weather.
Yet in the Pittsburg district alone the fur
naces running are said to have produced 13,000
tons per week ' less â¢ than their January out
put, so that the decrease In weekly produc
tion of all furnaces, 228,191 tons, March 1,
against 237,639 February 1. does not mean
anything more than bad weather. The shrink
age of supplies of Iron In February. Just when
increased supplies were expected and needed,
contributed much to the extraordinary advance
In prices, $2 75 In anthracite No. 1 February 1,
$2 50 In bessemer pig at Pittsburg, and $2 75
In gray forge. The unsold stocks also re
duced In February 46.431 tons, so that the ap
parent consumption was 977.979 tons in the
short month. Nearly all the work done, of
course, is at the comparatively low prices made
tn the great contracts three to four months
ago, but the few works which are still able to
' contract for early delivery are charging about
] what they please, and these quotations appear
in the published statements. In 'many cases
1 $1 to $5 per ton premium Is paid for delivery
! within sixty days. Plates are in special de
' mand and higher, but In - rails, bars and
structural forms the demand and prices are
1 not yet much changed. Tin is firm at $23 85,
I with a Consumption Of 2600 tons monthly, and
copper is scarce for spot at $17 75, with lead
j weaker at $4 'M. The trust refuses quotations
1 for tin plates. The demand for coke exceeds
the transporting capacity for the Connellsvllle
I region, but the output has been 168,679 tons
for the week, furnace being quoted at $1 60
, and foundry at $2 per ton.
The woolen manufacturer has been hampered
by doubts about the price of wool. Now a big
combination of worsted producers has been
I formed, with several other combinations pro
posed in goods of other kinds, but these do
not encourage buying and the season continues
I disappointing. In wool niantets the sales are
barely equal to the ordinary consumption of
| the mills dependent upon them and the prices
are rather weak
Exports of wheat do not yet fall below last
year's as many have for pome montns ex
pected, and Atlantic exports, flour included,
> have been for the week 3,390,402 bushels,
against 2,890,879 last year, and Pacific exports
603.107 bushels, again .t 1,280,618 last year, with
other exports of 407,669 bushels. Western re
; ceipts were 4,071,017 bushels, against 2.752,918
I lor the same week last year, and the output
from the country goes far to check appre
hension as to deficiency of the crop. The
price is about 2c lower for spot.
Failures for the week have been 182 In the
United States, against 248 last year, and 37
: | ln Canada, against 36 last year.

CHICAGO GRAIN MARKET.
CHICAGO, March 10.â The dominant factor
in the early news connected with the wheat
market was the liberal quantity shipped by
Argentine this Week. There was a contro
versy over the amount, one authority, placing
It at 2,032,000 bushels, another at 1.400.000. The
majority of traders accepted the statement
quoting the larger quantity. Liverpool re
ported a slight advance to begin with, but aa
It did not correspond with the gain here yea
terday it was Ignored. May opened %Â®%c
lower at 71%@71%c down to 71c. and with more
sellers than . buyers at the decline. Atlantic
port exports of wheat and flour equaled 689.000
bushels. Estimates ln the visible supply varied
considerably, some looking for 500,000 bushels
and others for that much decrease. The whole
market kept in mind that the Government re
port on farm reserves would . be issued ln the
afternoon, and It waa. assumed that tha total
would be the largest ever announced. The
nearer the market got to the Issuance of the
Agricultural Bureaus estimate the weaker the
prices became. Toward the end quotations ruled
quite heavy. May gradually declined, reaching
70%0 as the session ended There were, sellers
at 70%0 when the closing signal Bounded.
Country buying and moderate i ipts helped
corn nt the start. When wheat appeared hope
lessly devoid of recuperative ability, however.
some timid holders of corn let enough; k" '"
force a sharp decline. May Closed %c lower;*
Very little business was transact. -I In "its.
The market ruled weak and lower In sympathy
with corn. May declined ! lfi V
Liquidation In pork caused a soft spot in pro
visions at the opening. Later, however, the
market steadied somewhat on free buying or
lard and an Improvement In the pre. of hogs
at the yards. May pork declined 2%(&5c and
lard and ribs are a shade lower.

LONDON WOOL SALES.
LONDON. March 10.â There was j the usual
large attendance at the wool auction sales to
day. The offerings numbered 14.166 bales and
consisted of a good selection. A small supply
of scoured merinos sold briskly at an advance
of 6 per cent. Several lota of Russian greasy
were purchased by German buyers, while the
French buyers operated freely and largely In
cross-breds. There was active competition be
tween the Yorkshire and French buyers for
South African wool, which ruled dearer. The
majority, however, was taken by the conti
nent. The home buj-ers purchased largely of
American specialties.

"The Wool market seems to go from bad to
worse. There was a little wool selling last

Eastern markets are reported quiet and con
siderably lower. It is hard to say what the
future will bring forth. It all hinges on the
woolen goods market, and as in former years
enough was placed for heavy weight goods in
the month of January.
"So far this year very little business has been
done in that line and the market cannot
change until manufacturers get orders for
goods. With the severe winter they have in
the East and with the small stock of heavy
woolen goods in the market, things ought to
have a different complexion. But with all raw
materials and other - industries booming all
over the United States, the wool trade Is In
this awful condition.
'â¢The London sales opened Tuesday with a
good attendance, with fine wools firm and
_.
strong.
"Some new wools have arrived In this mar
ket and show up pretty well considering the
dry season.
"On Saturday, the 4th inst., a meeting was
held by the California Wool Association to take
steps to have the Government open the forest
reservations for sheep on account of the
drought, especially in the southern part of the
State.
"A committee was appointed consisting of
Messrs. Jacob Woolner, B. F. Flint and E. H.
Trvon, which went to Sacramento to prevail
upon the Legislature to pass a Joint resolution
and telegraph to the President and Secretary
of the Interior, telling of the prevalence of the
drought and have the forest reservations in
this State opened for the season of 1899. ; â¢ . -_:
"The Legislature passed a resolution and
forwarded It. and the Governor and the chair
man of the State Central Committee telegraph
ed the existing state of affairs."

THE STOCK MARKET.
There was an active business In local securi
ties on the morning session. There were no
pronounced changes, though the sugar stocks
were firm. On the afternoon session Contra
Costa Water sold higher, but fell back, and
the sugar stocks were also easier.
Mining stocks opened firmer at prices above
those of the preceding day, but soon weakened
again, as will be seen. Trading was quiet. -
A semi-annual dividend of 4 per cent la how
payable by the London, Paris and American
Bank, making 7 per cent for the year.
The Live Oak and Mlnuett Mining Company
of Nevada County has levied an assessment
of 5 cents per share, delinquent April 8.
The official report from the Standard Con
solidated mine of Bodle for the week ending
March 4 states that raise 4 In the Moyle vein,
from the 150- foot level, has sixteen Inches of
fair grade ore In the top. Raise 3 above the
318 level shows forty Inches of low grade ore.
Raise 3 in No. 10 ledge shows eighteen inches
of fair grade ore, and raises 4, 5 and 7 show
twenty, sixteen and twenty-four inches wide
respectively of fair grade ore. The east cross
cut ln the Black vein, same level, has a ledge
twenty-four Inches wide in the face, mixed
with quartz and porphyry. Winze No. 1 in the
Maguire vein, below the EM-fOOt level, shows
five inches of fair ore. Good ore Is also show
ing in the raise on the 470 level. The usual
quantity of fair to good ore Is being extracted
from the stopes on the 150, 270, 318 and 582 levels,
from the Moyle, No. 10 and Fortuna veins. At
the mill 241 tons of ore were crushed during
the week; average assay vanner tailings, $12 2S;
concentrates produced, 1 2-3 tons; amalgam
produced, 2113% ounces; value per ounce, $2 94.

NOTEâ In the above exposition of the tides
the early morning tides are given In the left
hand column, and the successive tides of thcj
day in the order of occurrence as to time. The
second time column gives the second tide of
the day. the third time column the third t da
and the last or right hand column gives; the,
last tide of the day. except when there are hut
three tides, as sometimes occur. The heights
given are additions to the soundings on tha
United States Coast Survey charts, except
when a minus sign ( â precedes the height,
and then the number given Is subtracted from
the depth given by the charts. The plane of
reference Is the mean of the lower low waters.

Tor Late Shipping See Page - Ten.RUBICON WAS
"SHOOED" IN FOR
THE HANDICAP
Rankest Fiasco of the
Season.
THE STAND PASSED IT OVER
.
JINKS SHOWED OLD - TIME
FORM.
Caldwell Fancied Carter Harrison.
Andrisa a Likely Two-Year-Old.
Thorpe in Bad â Some
Favorites Won.
The last race on the card at Ingleside
yesterday, the seven furlong handicap,
was the most palpable exhibition of
"shoo-in" seen at the Corrigan track
this season. Rubicon, whose destinies are
looked after by Danny Lynch, of Shar
\ key-Fitzsimmons fame, was the medium
of the coup. The chestnut horse, with 114
pounds on his back, was backed down
from 8 to 5 to 4 to 5, the books being
besieged with coin. Meanwhile the odds
against all of the other starters receded
in the betting. Caldwell kindly gave the
big chestnut horse an excellent send
off at the post. With the others whistling
"down brakes." Rubicon won by himself.
O'Connell taking the place from Good
Hope. It was the most disgraceful farce
seen on a track for many moons. A blind
man, with a jute sack pulled down over
his head, could scarcely have failed to see
a glimmering of "something doing." On
the back stretch. Hal Brown was pulling
Good Hope up in plain view of ail, when
the filly could easily have taken the
lead, with her light impost of 84 pounds.'
\ Possibly it was hazy and the judges
;did- not perceive this piece of work.
; O'Connell could have gone to the front,
-or attempted it. but yesterday he was
rated. At the end the sprinter was full
of run. Olinthus. was left, and Sam Mc-
Keever ran as if a racetrack was foreign
to his nature.
â¢ The whoie affair was the joke of
the season in the betting ring. If the Pa
cific Coast Jockey Club, realizing that it
is on its last legs, intends to sanc
tion safe-cracking and all other forms of
robbery, it should so inform the public.
Races could then be doped accordingly.
The sport, if such it could be termed,
flavored of the unsavory from the begin
ning. Four favorites were successful, but
that is no indication of honesty. There
is one rider on whom the California rac- â¢
ing public will "bank"â Joe Piggott. He
did not suit the highly flavored tastes of
Starter Caldwell and the Ingleside outfit
and was suspended for getting left at the
post.
Corrigan's gelding. Carter H. Harrison
Jr., was a prohibitive, favorite for tne
opening scramble for maidens, with a big
field of fourteen starters. To make the
purse doubly sure Caldwell sent him away
four lengths in front, and he won under
wraps. Festoso, a 25 to 1 shot, secured
the place from Racebud.
A strong tip was out on W. B. Jennings'
filly Andrisa to win the two-year-old
scramble, and she performed the feat very
Impressively. Backed down to even mon
ey favoritism, Spencer, on the daughter
of St. Andrew, came with a rush the last
part, beating Tanobe out over a length. i
Bamboulia. a 20 to 1 shot, showed plenty
of speed, taking- the show.
The hurdle handicap fell to Our Cli- |
mate, carrying top weight. Honig's horse
receded in the- betting, and then won
easily from Tom Smith. The favorite,
Three Forks, dumped his rider on the !

Mrs. Arbogast Not to Blame.
W. A. Botkin, who began suit for di
vorce from bis wife, Mrs. Cordelia Bot
kin. last Wednesday, is indignant at the
statement attributed to her t,hat
hi*? reason for seeking a legal separ
ation is in order that be may marry
Mrs Clara Arbogast. and intimating that
they have been on terms of undue friend
liness for a long time. Mr. Botkin stated
last night that an estrangement existed
between himself and his wife before he
ever knew Mrs.- Arbogast, and that the
aspersions cast upon that lady are unjust
and malicious and are the result of an
unworthy desire to injur.- a most estima
ble woman. Mr. Botkin said his acquain
tance with Mrs. Arbogast extended- over
a period of about two years; that their
acquaintance was simply one of friend
ship and their marriage has never been
contemplated, and that Mrs.- Arbogast is
in no way responsible tor his suit for di-
Torca.

GREAT COURSING EXPECTED.
Eastern Dogs Will Be Slipped With
Some of the Natives at Ingle
side Park To-Day.
The stake to be run at Ingleside Cours
ing Park commencing to-day filled with
seventy-two nominations, and in the
bunch of fliers are to be found some of
the best quality of greyhounds running
} on the coursing swards. '
The stake for the two days of coursing
i is open, and among the nominators will
! be found such old lovers of the leash as
T. Cronin, with no less than four dogs,
all of which have placed a snug sum to
the credit of their owner. Among the en
trants are such grand performers as
Thornhill. and Wild Tralee, a pair that
have won first money on several occasions
In the hottest kind of company. Mr. Cro
i nin has decided tb run all of his dogs at
Ingleside Park in the future. Mr. Keenan
has in the stake the old reliable trio-
Royal Buck, Flying Buck and Fear Not,
dogs that are never beaten in a "course
until the flag goes up. Lynch has got
two grand ones on the list, viz., Lottie
M and Mystic Maid. The latter bitch ran
in grand form on Sunday last, and when
right the dog that defeats her will cer
tainly know he has had a race. H. A.
Deckelman will have such noted perform
' er - as Flyaway, Old Glory. Rocket, Law
rence and Glen Chloe running. In this
stake they should go well into the money,
as in the hands of Trainer Geary these
dogs are always brought to slips in the
i pink of condition. There are a great
many cracks who will meet to-day, and it
: would be well for those attending the
meeting to be careful on giving much

Stevens vs. Minervaâ should be a
! good short end. Las Palmas vs. Flyaway

' is very dangerous, as she runs up a score
rapidly. Brutus vs. Lillle M-Wlll run
very close-. One of the hardest races of
! the card will be that between Thornhill
i and Royal Prize. They are both fast and
I rlever greyhounds, possessed of nearly
â an equal amount of speed. The two old
. '--^--diggers. Gladiator and Old Glory,
will meet again, and as they have on
Several occasions divided the honors the
' course to-day will be watched with much
' interest. The next pair are. the midgets
' of the coursing sward, Susie and Hum

CALIFORNIA VS. FIREMEN.
The College Men Will Line Up
Against the Insurance Athletes.
The University of California baseball
nine and the Fireman's Fund team will
cross bats this afternoon at the Recrea
tion Grounds, Eighth and Harrison
streets. This will be the third meeting of
the -colleagians and the Firemen. The
blue and gold has won the other two, not
on account of their own superior playing,
but on account of the surprisingly poor
showing of the other team.
When Stanford and the Firemen play
against one another the latter team putt,
up a. fine quality of ball. Although Stan
ford has been defeated three times by
the Firemen out of four games played
and California has .won all its games.
Stanford is a stronger team to-day than
California. -'-.-<â 'â > Â, _ _. -_.

in which California will have, appeared
in this city and a crowd Is expected on

GOLFING AT PRESIDIO.
Miss Houghton Wins the March
Competition for Winslow Medal.
The' members of the San Francisco Golf
.Club are now occupying their new quar
ters, the old house having already been
raised and moved some little distance
from its former site. The new clubhouse.'
like the old one, stands outside the mili
tary reservation.
On March 7 the following scores were
made on the links: Baron Alexander yon
Schroeder, 69; Miss Ella O'Connor, 87;
Miss Maud O'Connor, 88; Mrs. H. B.
Goodwin, 74 and 72. Yesterday, March
10, in the morning the regular ladies
handicap competition for the Winslow
medal took place. There were seven en
tries, of whom Mrs. H. B. Goodwin, Miss
E. W. Morgan and Miss M. B. Houghton
played at scratch, the rest receiving
handicaps. Miss M. B. Houghton, who
won the medal in the February competi
tion, proved the winner with a gross and
net score of 136 for eighteen holes, beat
ing all her competitors handsomely. The
full results are shown in the table:

March being the great month for golf
ing at Del Monte several of our local
golfers have taken themselves and thel.
bags of clubs thitherâ among them E. J.
McCutchen and S. L. Abbott Jr., two of
the strongest players in the San Fran
cisco Golf Club. The links at Del Monte
are now in excellent condition.
On Friday morning, March 10, the la
dies' regular competition, eighteen holes
with handicap, for the Winslow medal,
was held. Next Saturday, March 18,
men's competitions in driving and ap
proaching will take place on the links of
the San Francisco Golf Club.

March 11) there will be a competition for
men, match play, eighteen holes with
handicap, for a silver cup already in pos
session of the club. Next Saturday,
March IS, there will be putting and ap
proaching contests for ladles and gen
tlemen for silver medals.

States Golf Association. H. M. A. Miller,
secretary of- the club, leaves the State
shortly for a two . months visit to Old

AMATEUR ATHLETES TALK.
Pacific Association Intends to Take
Jurisdiction Over Rowing
and Baseball.
At the meeting of the -Pacific Associa
tion of the Amateur Athletic Union the
following representatives were present: J.
R. Bockman of the Ariel Rowing Club
(in the chair), W. C. Espy of the Pioneers,
A. P. Rothkopf of the Dolphins, S. J.
Pembroke of the Alamedas, George James
of the Olympic Club, M. Breen of the
South End, H. A. Keeler of the Academic
League, P. D. Franklin of the University
of California and C. L. Neumiller of the
Stockton Athletic Association.
President J. R. Bockman, while admit
ting that the Amateur Athletic Union ex
ercises no jurisdiction over rowing, base
ball and football, expressed himself as
strongly in favor of the Pacific Athletic
Association assuming jurisdiction over
these sports, sfnee, if it does not do so, the â â 
rowing clubs, which form half the mem- !
bership of the association, would have
no reason for belonging to it. There is \
! already a movement on foot among some i
J of the rowing clubs to join the National I
Association Qf Amateur Oarsmen or to |
I form a new organization. A circular will ,
i be sent to the various rowing clubs on the
bay in San Diego, Oregon. Washington
' and British Columbia, asking each club
I to send three delegates to a conference,
at which the rowing men decide what will
best further the interests of that sport.
After considerable discussion, the presi
dent and secretary drew up an amend- j
ment to the constitution of the Pacific
Association, enumerating the sports over ;
which the association claims jurisdiction, j
Among these are rowing, baseball, indoor !
baseball, football and many others, j
Notices will be -ent out to-day to all clubs j
' in the association, so that the necessary
thirty days' notice may be given before ;
j the association holds its next meeting.
The association, in view of the confused j
condition in which cycling affairs now â 
' are has decided for the present to recog- j
I nize neither the League of American ,
; Wheelmen nor the California Associated
' Cycling Clubs. Cycling has been dropped
! from the list of intercollegiate sports, and
i so the sport does not concern the uni
versities except indirectly.
A question as to whether the Ariel Row- :

of managers of the Pacific Association,

TENNIS PLANS.
Some New Leagues to Foster the
Sport Among Young Players.
On the California Tennis Club courts j
this morning the racket wleldera of j
Boone's Academy. Berkeley, will play a j
series of matches to try out the candi
dates who wish to represent thair college t
in the Amateur Academic League cham- !
pionship tournament. This tournament !
will be one of the big tennis events of the
year and will be held some time next
m Sam Hardy is now arranging for an in
terscholastic championship tournament.
It is his idea to make it an annual affair
as well as the Pacific Coast championship
meet on the Fourth of July. Both the big
colleges Have expressed a willingness to
enter teams as Individual players. Any
student of a recognized institution of |
learning will be a qualified player. From !
present indications this association will
be formed and the initial tournament held j
this spring. '"_, â  _ ,- '
The lady members of the Alameda Ten- \
nis Club played a round robin tournament j
yesterday afternoon, which was as much j
a society as en athletic event.

Olympic Baseball Game. .
The next game of indoor baseball in the
Reliance/ Olympic and Young' Men's
Christian Association series will be played
on Tuesday evening in the Olympic gym
nasium between the Olympic and Young
Men's Christian Association teams. Great
Interest Is displayed In these games, which
have proved big drawing cards whenever
played. â . 44
On Sunday afternoon the Olympic base
ball nine will meet the Golden Gates on
the Eighth and Harrison street grounds.
The teams are evenly matched and will
play for the championship.

Quite Unusual.
James Fisher, alias Ryan, a well-known
pickpocket, was yesterday held to answer
before the Superior Court by Judge Con
lan In $3000 bonds on a charge of grand
larceny. February 12 at the ferry he stole
a purse from the pocket of C. H. Croweil,
teacher in a night school.

ALAMEDA COUNTY NEWS.
MAKING THE
FINAL VOWS
TO VOTERS
Oakland Turns Into a
Circus.
W W. CAMRON MAKES. REPLY
CANDIDATES APPEAR AT THE
THEATER.
Republicans to Wind Up With a Par
adeâlndependents Will Conclude
With Rally at the
Tabernacle.
'. â¦
' ' Oakland Office San Francisco Call.
908 Broadway, March 10.
For' forty-eight hours Oakland will be
at a standstill as regards everything ex
cept politics. With sunrise on Monday
morning the pent-up volcano will begin
to erupt, and by midnight the results will
be known and the city will settle down to
business.
From oratory and the circulation â of
cards the campaign has now descended to
bell wagons and circulars. The Streets
to-day look like* a circus. The thinker
has been at work and many episodes that
candidates would fain have forgotten are
now being prominently exploited. No
matter whether true or not, this is the '
eleventh hour, and there is no time for
contradictions, consequently any ; flaring
statement that appears after this should
be received very cautiously and relied on
still less. -.-?â ="
W. W. Carrtron has made a reply to the
Statement of Councilman Girard regard
ing the incident which has rent in twain
the Second Ward. Mr. Camron says:
"Ever since I announced myself as a can
didate for Councilman there has been per
sistently and continuously circulated in
the interests of the candidacy of Mr. Gi
rard. for the same office, the most cow
ardly, scurrilous and scandalous reports
affecting my private and business life,
all of which were well known to be untrue
both by Mr. Girard and his friends. I do
not wish to conceal the fact that the cir
cular referred to in to-day's ("all was is
sued by my permission, and for justifiable
ends. The record shows that both Mr.
Girard and his attorney were present in
'court and that he had an opportunity to
have answered his wife and her witnesses,
but he did not then dispute the truth of
any of the allegations and the court found
that they were all true.
"I have been in many political fights be
fore, but this is the first time I have been
forced into a fight of this character. lam
and always have been, as my record
Fhows, in favor of decent politics. I even
quarreled with Mr. Girard over my efforts
to keep outside matters out of this cam
paign, but he would not cease."
The Democratic and Independent parties
did not hold any political meetings to
night. They were to have met at Thir
teenth avenue, but decided that they
could do'more politics by appearing at the
Acme benefit at the Macdonough Theater,
where they occupied boxes.
Mrs. Fannie M. Pugh, nominated by the
Prohibitionists for School Director, is still
a candidate. This lady polled the highest
Prohibition vote at the last State elec
tion, and has been induced to withdraw
her resignation from the city ticket.
To-morrow evening the Republican
campaign will close at Hamilton Hall
Prior to the meeting the uniformed corps
of the Young Men's Republican League
will parade. This evening the Republi
cans held a meeting at Hanson's Hall
West Oakland, and at Becker's Hall, in
the Sixth Ward. The Independents will
hold their final rally at the Tabernacle
to-morrow night.

OAKLAND. March Miss Emma
Mier, residing with her mother at CO
Sixth street, was examined for insanity
to-day by-Drs. Knox and Wakefield and
committed to the asylum at Stockton
The unfortunate young woman has been
ln poor health for some time, and four
days ago her mind perceptibly weakened.
Last night she became violent, and be
fore her mother, Mrs. J. T. Mier real
ized it the daughter had thrown half the
furniture out of the house and was break
ing things in general.
The mother's remonstrance proved use
less, and Officer Sherry was called in-
After a lively struggle Miss MieF was
overpowered and taken to the Receiving
Hospital, where she was placed in a
â¢traitjacket.

GHOSTLY CIGARETTES MAKE
HIM CLIMB THE WALLS
OAKLAND, March 10.â delusive locally prohibited little cigarette has
claimed another victim, and the secret poisons that lurk within some
of the paper covered rolls have totally wrecked the mind of "W. T. Dar
nal, a telegraph operator, residing with his -parents at 941 Campbell
street. Darnal was taken to the Receiving Hospital this evening and placed'
in the insane ward. To-morrow morning he will be examined by the Lunacy
Commissioners, Drs. Selfridge and Tisdale. ;; r J-l
. The unfortunate man is 28 years, of age and he has been an inveterate
cigarette fiend, consuming a half dozen packages each day for a long time
past, and until he imagines the walls of his room contain shelves upon shelves,
filled with the little wicked paper covered weeds. Yesterday Dr. Selfrtdge
was called ln to attend the young man, but his services proved useless. At
night Darnal ' arises from his bed and attempts' to climb the walls of his bed
chamber in the hope of reaching his coveted cigarettes, and after hours of
fruitless efforts he would sink exhausted to the floor. S-'io^-.L - . "~
His condition to-night is such that there is little doubt that he will be
recommended for commitment to an Insane asylum. '
USED HIS CAR
AS THE SCENE
OF HIS SUICIDE
A Motorman Shoots
Himself.
TWO BULLETS INTO HIS BRAIN
NO REASON EXCEPT INSANITY
CAN BE ASSIGNED.
William Lyons Writes a Brief Letter
to His Wife and Then Delib
erately Attempts Self-
Destruction.
S3 "Darling wife, you have 33
Â± been a good, loving, faithful Â±
+ wife to me. I feel I am be- Â£
& coming insane and think if is O
X better to end it this way. I q
-â¦ wish Eden Lodge would bury â¦
â J me. W.". $
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
90S Broadway, March 10.
After writing this farewell upon a quar
ter-sheet advertising card and laying it
beside him. William Lyons, a motorman
on the Twenty-third avenue branch of the
Haywards electric line, sat down in one
corner of his car and sent two bullets into
his brain this afternoon. Â» â  _
Shortly after 5 o'clock Officer R. U. Mc-
Sorley discovered the motorman lying in
one corner of the car with blood rushing
from two bullet wounds just behind the
right ear. â¢_ -.- . -,
The dying man was removed to the Re
ceiving Hospital, where Drs. Sanborn and
Horn found, after a thorough examina
tion, that one bullet had pierced the brain.
The other bullet was extracted, since it
had not penetrated over half an inch.
Mrs Lyons, who was immediately noti
fied at her Elmhurst home, was unable to
assign any cause for her husband s rash
act. Aside from statin? tha,t he appeared
in his usual' good spirits when he left
home this afternoon she was too much
grief-stricken to refer to tho sad occur
rence. The weapon used by Lyons was an
old-style 45-callber bulldog revolver that
had not been discharged for a long time.
Being on the late night runs he usually
carried this weapon for self-protection,
for it is -customary for him to run his car
without a conductor after passing Twen
ty-first street to its terminus at Twenty
seventh street. Lyons' rash act has
caused no end of surprise among tne offi
cials and employes of the Haywards line,
with whom he was a great favorite.
It lacked fifteen minutes of midnight
when Lyons breathed his last. Deceased
was a native of Canada, aged 51 years.
He was a member of Eden Lodge; F. and
A. M., of San Leandro. The inquest will
be held to-morrow night.
WAS MUSTERED OUT
AND WANTED TO DIE
EDWARD HOWATT ATTEMPTS
TO ' COMMIT SUICIDE.
Aimed to Blow His Brains Out, but
Kindly Interference' Results in
a Shoulder Wound.
Oakland Office San Francisco. Call,
908 Broadway, March 10.
Crazed with drink. Edward B. Howatt,
a soldier, aged 23 years, this afternoon at- '
tempted to commit suicide by the Smith & I
Wesson route, caliber 38, at Kohlmeyer's i
saloon, corner of Eighth and Washington
streets.
That his effort was not successful was
due' to the prompt action of George Cur
tis, who snatched the revolver just as the
would-be suicide pulled the trigger. The
bullet penetrated the left shoulder, and
Howatt dropped to the floor like a load ol
bricks.
He was taken to the Receiving Hospital, ]
where Dr. Stratton found that the bullet I
went clear through the left shoulder. The I
missile, was afterward found in Howatt's :
shirt. The injured man lay apparently â¢
unconscious for nearly half an hour while
his wound was dressed.
Howatt received his discharge, from
Battery. E of the Third Artillery yester- i
day, and has been having a good time j
along the "cocktail route" since then.
Shortly after 4 o'clock .this afternoon he
stepped into Kohlmeyer's saloon in com- |
pany with George Curtis and William I
Britton and ordered several "steams." â 
Then he began to brood over some Im- |
aginary trouble and began weeping.
"We tried to console him," salt! Curtis !
to a reporter,* "but he wouldn't have it
that way. Then he pulled out his gun, !
remarking, 'Well, I might as well settle I
it all right here.' With that he raised j
his revolver and pointing It at his head
pulled the trigger just as I grabbed it. i
Had I not done this Howatt would have
been' dead by this time."
When Howatt had recovered sufficiently j
from the shock he opened his eyes and !
begged for his revolver, but the police had I
taken it away. He said he was sorry that
he had not finished the job and repri
manded Curtis for Interfering. When as
sured by Dr. Stratton that the wound was i
not fatal he replied: "Well, I hope to
God that I die. The grave is about the !
best place for a man to go to about this â¢
time. He refused to give any reason for j
his rash act. He demanded that his name
be withheld from publication, saying he j
could do as he pleased and that It was no- I
body's business.
When Howatt enlisted in the army he j
gave his residence as Forest Hill, and he |
was an iron molder by occupation. ;
It was afterward learned that Howatt
resides with his father, E. B. Howatt, on
Grove street, near Forty-fifth. The elder I
Howatt Is a motorman on the Grove
street line, and when seen to-night he :
stated: "My son came home last night ,
very much under the influence of liquor.
In fact, I think he must have been
drugged.- Any way I scolded him severely
for drinking, and this morning when he
got up he put on my clothes and overcoat
and went out, taking my revolver with
him. I do not know what possessed him
to make him want to die."
A good' Arabian horse can canter in the
desert for twenty-four hours In summer
and forty-eight hours In winter without
drinking. ' "

Fred Gutterson Takes
a Wife. â 
WEDDED A SISTER-IN-LAW
SHE WAS RECENTLY DIVORCED
FROM HIS BROTHER.
The Secret of a Suit Filed Last Fall
Made Clear by a Recent Trip
to the State of
Nevada.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, March 10.
The most interesting chronicle in mu
sical and social circles was received to
day when It became known that the
handsome divorcee, Mamie Gutterson, is a
wife once more. And to make the incident
more interesting, the lady married the
younger brother of the husband from
whom she was divorced last fall.
A little more than six months ago the
Guttersons lived in apparent happiness on
Sixteenth street. A. L. Gutterson was a
bank official in San Francisco and his wife
was a very popular singer. Mrs. Gutter
son's tastes were all musical and she was
naturally thrown into' the company of
Fred Gutterson, the younger brother of
her husband.
Oakland was shocked last August to
learn that A. L. Gutterson had brought
suit in this county for a divorce. The ut
most secrecy was observer and the case
was transferred to San Francisco. Some
time later a decree was greeted and the
public was not taken into the secret. Mrs.
Gutterson and her child moved to San
Francisco and lived out on Sacramento
street. Fred Gutterson, the younger
brother, Is a popular celloist of the Tivoli
Opera-house and lives ln San Francisco.
"W hen news was received that Mrs. Gut
terson had remarried and was still Mrs.
Gutterson it was supposed that a reunion
had been effected and that sue had wed
ded her former husband. â  â¢ V :
Even this could not have rÂ«wÂ»n done
within a year after the divorce, but there
is no such law in Nevada, and to that
State Mrs. Gutterson and her brother-in
law went. The records show that Fred S.
Gutterson was given a license at Reno on
February 25 to wed Mrs Marie Gutter
son, who is several years his senior.
On his return to his duties at the Tivoli
Fred Gutterson told his friends he had
taken unto himsele! a wife, but 'id not
inform them that he had married his sis
ter-in-law:. ' __â '. â â "'.'
UNIVERSITY OF
CALIFORNIA NEWS
Professor Le Conte's Unique Birthday
â Vacant Scholar
ships Filled.
BERKELEY, March 10,â Professor Jo
seph I-e. Conte, the venerable professor of
geology and zoology, received this after
noon a peculiar and valuable grift from
some of his old students. It came all the
way from Johannesburg, South Africa, ma
a modest tin box, accompanied ' by a
typewritten letter signed by seven old U.
C. graduates. Professor Le Conte thought
aât first that it might contain some trivial
scientific specimen of interest, and ac
cordingly set it aside to look at later.
He was very much surprised on examin
ing the package after some little time to
find it filled with large gold nuggets,
which his old students in South Africa
had collected and sent to him as a birth
day present.
Some of the nuggets were unusually
fine speciments, the intrinsic value of
the whole amounting to . some hun
dreds of dollars. The letter accompany
ing the gift was signed by Harry w.
Webb. '75; Eugene Hoefer. 'SI; E. 11.
(larthwaite, 'SO: R. H. Robertson, '75;
Leslie Slmson. '91: Henry Hay, '94, and
Fred.C. Roberts, '86.
The faculty committee on scholarship's
nut this morning and appointed the fol
lowing students, to fill vacancies: Mrs.
Florence E. Barnard of San Francisco for
the State of California scholarship, dis
trict 5; Ralph H. Curtlss cf Redlands for
the Levi Strauss scholarship, district 7,
in place of H. M. Bagley, resigned.
The meeting of the Associated Students
called for this morning was postponed for
one week, as a quorum could not be ob
tained. President Charles E. Thomas will
call a special meeting for next Friday, as
several matters of Importance now await
the action of .student body.

English Masters Against
â¢Americans.
FOURTH CONTEST UNDER WAY
PLATING FOR THE SIR GEORGE
NEWNES TROPHY.
At v the Close of the First Sitting the
Westerners Appear to Have
the Battle Well in
Hand.
Special Dispatch to .Th* Call.
NEW TORK, March 10.â For the fourth
time in the history of chess, players of
this country contested a match' on ten
boards against the best recognized players
of Great Britain. The series of contests
Is being played for a magnificent trophy
presented by Sir George Newnes, presi
dent of the British Chess Club, London.
The first match, in 1896, was won by the
Americans, but the second and third
matches, played in 1597 and 1898, were won
by the Britishers. b . f
The contest begun to-day was all the
more important because another victory
for the Britishers would have won for
them the trophy. ; Many metropolitan ex
perts were present in the assembly room
of the Brooklyn Academy of Music, where
this end of the match was played, under
the auspices of the Brooklyn Chess Club.
There were at least. 400 persons present.
The players were seated on a big platform
facing the spectators, and above them
were appended ten giant chess boards
with movable pieces, and as soon as a
move *was made either in Brooklyn or at
the Hotel Cecil, London, where the match
is being played under the auspices of the
British Chess Club, It was repeated on
the giant boards to enable the spectators
to follow the progress of the game from
move to move. The operators and the
representatives of the press were placed
in a room adjoining the big hall, close to
the players. No time was lost in repeat
ing the moves for the press and specta
tors.
The pairing was arranged as follows:

A BOY ABSCONDER
CAUGHT IN SANTA ROSA
OSCAR ANDERSON, CHARLES
NELSON'S CLERK, ARRESTED.
William Baker and Lorenzo Peterson,
His Two Accomplices, Are in
the "Tanks" Here.
Oscar Anderson, the boy clerk in the
employment of Charles Nelson, proprie
tor of lumber mills, was arrested yes
terday afternoon at Santa Rosa on a dis
patch from Captain Bohen and Detective
T. B. Gibson will bring him back to the
city to-day.;Â§Â»y^ :'.'.';;
About thr^s weeks ago Anderson was
sent to the Crocker- Woolworth Bank with
two checksâ to be paid to: Will tier,
Fuller & Co.. and the other, for $800, to be
disbursed ln paying the men on one of
Nelson's steam schooners. Anderson paid
the" check to Whittler, Fuller & Co., but
absconded with the $800 intended for the
crew. â¢ -- -~- *, Â»'â '.!. ''â%''' tv- : "V ? : 'â '-- â â _-".* ' K -^-*~ "
The police ascertained that the boy had
got into bad company and had been play
ing the races. He was induced to steal
the money by William Baker, alias
"Dutchy," a brother of the pugilist, and
Lorenzo Peterson. Warrants were sworn
out for the arrest of the trio and since
then Detectives Egan and T. B. Gibson
have 'been working on -the case. They
traced the trio ' from Pleasanton, where
Anderson posted Whittler,. Fuller & Co.'s
receipt to Nelson, and from there to Sac
ramento, Ukiah, Geyservllle, Windsor,
and finally to Santa" Rosa. They pur
chased a' road house a few miles out of
Santa Rosa and. Baker and Peterson came
to the city quietly to make arrangements
for running it. The detectives yesterday
morning found Baker and Peterson in the
Dunlap House and took, them to "the
tanks'* at the City Prison.
Captain Bohen then sent a dispatch to
Santa Rosa to arrest Anderson, who had
assumed 'the name of Edward .Stanley,
and received a reply that he was in jail
there.
SANTA ROSA, March 10.â Oscar Ander
son, a warm young sport, who struck
town about three weeks ago with his
pockets tilled with 20-dollar gold pieces
and who since that time has been set
ting quite a hot pace for the amateur
bloods of this city, was arrested here this
afternoon by Officer L.lndley and lodged
in the County Jail, charged with embez
zlement.
Anderson, during his spectacular, career
in this city, has been known as Edward
Stanley, and he blew in on the evening
of Washington's birthday in company
with three other kindred spirits, who
registered at the Grand Hotel as L. Pe
terson, A. Smith and William Baker. Ba
ker claimed to be a pugilist and the oth
ers stated they were horsemen employed
at Ingleside and were enjoying a vaca
tion.
The morning after their 'arrival the four
visited a local livery stable, where Ander
son hired four rigs and all started out
for a drive, Anderson paying the bill
with great flourish in advance. Upon- re
turning to town in the afternoon the
quartet visited the clothing establishment
of H. L,. Tripp, on Fourth street, where
the Croesus purchased his three friends
each a suit of clothes 'and complete out
fit. '
During his stay here until a few days
ago Anderson spent money like water
and a number of the fair sex will mourn
his sudden removal.
About a week ago he. became the pro
prietor of a local road house near the
outskirts of the city and a few days later
his three friends departed for sections
unknown. The arrest this afternoon was
upon the request' of Chief Lees of -San
Francisco. When arrested Anderson ad
mitted he was the man wanted and he
was at once taken to jail, where he
passed the night.
WAS MRS. JOHNSON
RAILROADED TO JAIL?
HABEAS CORPUS BRINGS TO
LIGHT STRANGE FAC-~^
Case of a Woman Who Was Never
Tried, -et Was Serving a Three
Mo iths' Sentence for
Vagrancy.
Mrs. Mary A. Johnson was released
from- the County Jail yesterday morning
on the order of Judge Murasky, and there
by rests a story past explaining. It is
evident that Mrs. Johnson was "railroad
ed" to prison; how is a mystery, but sub
sequent developments may clear this haze
away.
Mrs. Johnson Is the wife of John A.
Johnson, a marble cutter, employed at
Holy Cross Cemetery, and the mother of
five children. Husband and wife did not
live happily together of late, and last De
cember Johnson applied for a divorce,
alleging intemperance as a cause of ac
tion. She filed an answer denying the al
legation, and in a cross-complaint charged
her husband with extreme cruelty. A few
weeks 'ago Mrs. Johnson was arrested
while on her way to her home, 503 Edna
street, the officer who arrested her charg
ing her with being Intoxicated. She was
taken to the City Prison and from there to
the County Jail to serve a three months'
sentence for vagrancy.
No record of Mrs. Johnson having been
found guilty of vagrancy appears on the
Police Court records, yet the commitment
bears the signature of Judge Conlan.
Judge Conlan admits that the signature
on the commitment appears to be genuine,
but does not recollect that Mrs. Johnson
was eVer tried in his court.
Later Judge Conlan discovered that
Judge Graham' had been sitting in the
case in his absence.
Such being the facts. Assistant District
Attorney Soloman, when habeas corpus
proceedings on behalf of the prisoner were
taken up by Judge Murasky, admitted er
ror and said that- no earthly right existed
for the woman's commitment.
The District. Attorney's office and C. E.
Phillips,. Mrs. Johnson's lawyer, will make
a thorough investigation, and In the event
of evidence of any ."crookedness" center
ing on any official there is trouble in store
for him.
Money for the Tax Collector.
The Finance Committee of the Super
visors, at its meeting yesterday, allowed
Tax Collector Edward I. Sheehan $10,500
with which to conduct his office until the
end of the fiscal year inÂ»June. The latter
official has not as yet been able to esti
mate whether "the money will pay for all
the extra help required to handle the vol
ume of business that passes through the
office.

BILLY LAVIGNE SAVED HIS
BROTHER FROM A KNOCKOUT
Entered the Ring at a
Critical Time.
REPEATS M'VEY'S TRICK
REFEREE McDONALD THEN DE
CLARED SMITH WINNER.
Did Not Declare Bets Off, However,
Nor Take Notice of Smith's
Repeated Foul Tac
tics.
Filly Smith, "the Mysterious/.' was j
given a decision last evening at Wood- j
ward's Pavilion over "Kid" Lavigne In
the fourteenth round of what was to have
been a twenty-round contest. Jim Mc-
Donald, the baseball umpire, was referee.
Billy Jordan, master of ceremonies, an- j
nounce- before the contestants were or- j
dered to toe the scratch that the. fighters
had agreed to break clean, or in other
Words, not to hit in a clinch. The referee i
heard this announcement, but ho did not I
enforce the mandate. Smith had two very' I
decided advantages over his stocky op- i
ponent, viz.: Five inches in height and
three inches in the reach of arms.
The old saying that a good little man
cannot cope with a good big man was ful- j
ly exemplified ail through the mill. But j
although Lavigne has proven to be a won- j
der in several battles, it must be .-aid that
according to the display he gave last
evening in hitting and staying qualities j
he is fast on the retrograde. Smith made j
rio apparent endeavor to deliver to his cus- j
tomer any of the hard goods Which lie is
in possession of until the thirteenth round i
arrived. In fact, he toyed with Lavigne
for several rounds and let opportunities
pass. If he had taken advantage of these
the contest would have ended long before
the fourteenth round.
Smith has a record of sing foul tactics, i
end the thousands of people who wit-,
nessed tin mill will bear testimony to the
fact that he administered at least twenty
foul blows between the first round anil
tlie fifth round of the contest. Referee
McDonald cautioned him time and again
in the first and second rounds, but Smith |
laughed at the commands and continued
to hit in the clinches and smile at his I
seconds each time he committed a foul.
Cries from the gallery and pit of "Foul"
and "What is the referee there for?" were
numerous, but McDonald paid not the
least attention to the demands of "Get on j
to yourself. Jim!"
Smith continued to hit in the clinches
and finally Lavigne resorted to the same
trick when he was satisfied that the
referee would not pay any attention to'
the agreement which the fighters had en
tered into before the contest commenced.
Smith, as previously stated, could have
put his opponent to sleep long before the
fourteenth round was reached if he had
forced the battle, as time and again he j
allowed opportunities to pass which if he
had taken advantage of them would have I
ended in a knockout for Lavigne. But for
some reason best known to the mysteri- j
oils gentleman he allowed the interest in
the affair to go on until the fourteenth
round; near the end of which he smashed
Lavigne a brace of left-handers on the j
SACRIFICED LIFE
FOR HIS PATIENTS
DR. FREDERICK P. MUFFE, Who Died
Suddenly Yesterday at His Residence,
1211 Jackson Street.
HUNDREDS of friends of Dr. Frederick P. Muffe will regret to learn of his
sudden death yesterday. Seemingly in the best of health, the well-known
physician attended to his manifold duties on Wednesday and Wednesday
evening, but retired to his much-needed rest feeling the effects of over
work. Early yesterday morning he called to his wife to bring him some whisky
to sustain him. and then quietly fell back on his pillow dead. Dr. Perrault was
immediately called in, and found his fellow-practitioner at rest. Death was at
tributed by him to angina pectoris and overwork.
The news of Dr. Muff 's death has been a severe shock to all who knew him.
Many were aware that in- was suffering from too close attention to his patients,
and even warned him. but deceased was unable to disappoint his fellow-sufferers,
and his untimely death was due to sacrifices in their behalf.
A more kindly, generous and charitable man could hardly be found in his
profession. He was beloved by his patients, and unknowingly their selfish de
mands on his time proved his undoing. Working early and late, he ministered to
their needs, never murmuring, but gradually eking out his own life.
Had Dr. Muffe lived but two weeks longer he would have reached the age
of 50. His was a busy life. After completing his medical studies in the University
of New York, he entered the Bellevue Medical College of that city and graduated,
and then entered the army as surgeon. For years he occupied an honored position
under Uncle Sam. In 1886 he resigned and opened an office at 425 Kearny street, at
which place he practiced until death closed his busy life.
The deceased was connected with many fraternal organizations. He was past
president of the California Schuetzen Club, a member of the Masonic order, the
Odd Fellows and the Red Men, and was also chairman of the executive com
mittee of the German-American Club. He was a prominent Republican and did
good work for his party during the past campaign. It is said he was to have
received the next appointment on the Health Board from Governor Gage.
Dr. Muffe leaves a widow and two children to mourn his untimely taking off.
His daughter, Mrs. Baum of Fort Bragg, was notified of his death.
The funeral will take place to-morrow at 10 o'clock from his late residence.
1211 Jackson street, and the cortege will then proceed to the Masonic Temple,
where services will be held. The remains will be cremated at Odd Fellows' Cem
etery.

The question at Issue is, How many
times will it become necessary for a
fighter to commit a transgression of the
rules to satisfy a referee that a decision
against the transgressor is absolutely
necessary?
The fight was practically uninteresting.
Smith can whip the "has-been" Lavigne
in five rounds at any time he desires to
open up his guns of war. When Billy
Lavigne entered the ring apparently to
save his brother from a knock-out. neither
man suffered much damage, although La
vigne appeared to be a bit groggy.
Doubtless the McVey scheme of jump
ing into the ring purposely to save a
good friend and at a supposed critical
moment will be introduced again on this
coast before a stop will be put to hippo
d-or-p boxing contests in the Golden
West.
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL,' SATURDAY, MARCH 11, 1899.
Smith and Lavigne entered the ring
simultaneously at 9:17 p. m. Smith's sec
onds were Alec Greggains, Tim McGrath
and one Hennessy. Lavigne was esquired
by his brother Billy, "Young Mitchell,"
Teddy Alexander and Arthur Walker.
Jim McDonald, the popular baseball um
pire, acted as referee. Smith weighed
exactly 142 pounds, and Lavigne about
13.5. Announcer Jordan Introduced the
men ami read a telegram from Tom
O'Rourke stating that Joe Walcott woulo
fight the winner for a purse and a side j
bet of $5000. , ;;
Billy Lavigne objected to some band
ages which Smith had on his left wrist
and insisted that they be removed, They
had an animated discussion about it. but ,
George Lavigne finally decided it by say
ing that Smith could wear all the band
ages he' wanted. -- â 'â ''.-:
The men agreed to break away clean,
without hitting in the clinches. It was
exactly 9:30 when the battle commenced.
In the first round, after some prelimi
nary fiddling Smith was the first to lead.
Both ran into a clinch, smashing their
rights into the ribs. Lavigne tried hi_
right for the head and Smith clinched.
Smith feinted and they clinched again.
Lavigne landed a hard left on the head
and after they had clinched Smith threw
his right hard into the body, the crowd
veiling "Foul," as the men had agreed
not to hit in clinches. Lavigne swung
his left hard on the head and Smith
thumped him twice in the ribs after the
clinch, raising another cry of "Foul. Tin
referee warned Smith repeatedly to de
sist hitting in the clinches. Lavigne
rushed, without damage. and then,
straightened out his left, landing slightly
on the head.
Between the first and second rounds
Referee McDonald talked to Smith, warn
ing him not to hit in the clinches. Smith
opened the second with a stiff right on the
head. In the succeeding clinch Smith
resumed his old tactics of hitting and was
roundly hissed. Lavigne essayed a rush,
landing his right in the side. Smith tried
a vicious right for the head and missed, j
Lavigne drove his right hard into the ;
face and Smith swung his right into the
"Kid's" body after they had clinched.
Lavigne rushed in, landing his left on the
head, and Smith clinched without strik
ing back. Smith blocked a vicious left
and Lavigne missed with his left for the
head, clinching and receiving another of
Smith's foul blows in the ribs. The ref
eree again warned Smith. Lavigne landed
a terrific right in the ribs. They rushed
Into a clinch as the gong sounded.
Lavigne opened the third round with a
light left in the face. This made Smith
mad and he came back, but fell short. j
I avigne rushed again. Smith clinching
and hitting the "Kid" while he held him.
raising another cry of "Foul. Lavigne ;
planted a hard left in the face and then j
rushed in. swinging both hands on the
head, Smith returning on the body. La- j
vigne landed another hard one on the face
and a left in the ribs, and Smith seemed i
winded. Lavigne ducked a terrific left I
swing and as he clinched Smith again
dallied with his ribs. Smith swung his
right around the "Kid's" shoulder and
they exchanged hard lefts in tiic body.
In the fourth, after a little cautious
sparring, Lavigne led his left for the
head. Smith getting in on the- body. La
vigne led and fell short, then rushed and
was blocked. Smith rushed Lavigne to
the rones, missed with his left and
brought his right across hard on the head.
Smith rushed again, landing his ieft on
the Kid's back as the latter ran away.
Smith tried a wild uppercut and missed
by a yard. Lavigne rushed in .and Smith
clinched, smashing him on the back after
ward.
Opening the fifth, Smith swung his
right anil Lavigne ducked, landing p. hard
left on the neck with his right. Smith
then blocker] a similar succeeding blow. ;
Lavigne rushed and fell short. Lavigne.
missed a right swing and Smith blocked ;
other. Both rushed Into a clinch with- j
out harm to either. Lavigne landed light
ly on the forehead, Smith countering on
the ribs. Lavigne ducked a right and
rushed into close quarters. Both swung
rights and lefts for the head, but they
were too close to do damage.
Lavigne opened the sixth with an inef
fective rush. He ducked from Smith's
vicious right, but received it a second
later hard on the neck. Smith got in with
left and right hard on the body, and
rushed the Kid to the ropes, landing his
left on the body. He tried his right for
the head, but went around Lavigne's
neck and the latter clinched. Lavigne
placed a hard right on Smith's shoulder
and -Other in the ribs. He rushed
Smith, missing with a left swing, but
landing his right, on the bo.lv. They
clinched and Smith hit him. raising the
usual cry of "Foul."
Smith opened the seventh round with a
rush, throwing his elbow into Lavigne's
face as they clinched. Lavigne rushed
and Smith smothered the effort. Smith
landed hard in the face and tried a right
uppercut, which was a mile short La
vigne landed a hard left in the face and
Smith came back with his left in the
body. Smith hit him in the ensuing
clinch, and everybody saw it but the ref
eree. Smith blocked a straight left and
received a hard right on the head. La
vigne planted another hard left in the
face without return. Lavigne rushed
Smith to the ropes and they exchanged
straight lefts in the face. Smith rushed
and swung both hands hard for the body
but the Kid jumped away, getting in with
his left on the ribs as he did so.
In the eighth there was some clever
blocking and ducking at first. Smith hit
in the clinch again. Smith landed hard
in the mouth after the breakaway He
then threw his left into the ribs as La
vigne went around his neck with a right
swing. Lavigne landed light left on face
and jumped away as Smith essayed a
counter. Both ducked and blocked left
swings for the head. Lavigne rushed;
overreaching himself, falling against the
ropes. Smith followed him up and swung
his right, landing hard on the Kid's spine.
Lavigne clinched and came up laughing.
The ninth was the slowest round of the
fight. They rushed, clinched, ducked and
blocked without damage to either.
The pace was slower now, both men
being tired. From the tenth to the thir
teenth little of interest happened, an oc
casional stiff body blow relieving the
monotony.
The end came in a curious, way in the
fourteenth round. After several counters
and blocks Lavigne landed hard on the
head without a return. The blow warmed
up Smith, who came back at his antagon
ist swinging his left and right for the
head. He landed twice on the face and a
hard right on the jaw. The "Kid" reeled
as if nearly out, but essayed a weak rush
back, covering his jaw with his left hand.
Then his brother Billy jumped through
the ropes and threw his arm over the
"Kid's" shoulder, saving something to the
referee about Smith having fouled him
long enough, and led the "Kid" to his
corner. The referee awarded the fight to
Smith at once on Billy Lavigne's breach
of the rules in entering the ring. It was
plain, however, that the "Kid" would
have been put out by another good blow,
though the gong might have saved him,
as the round was almost over.
Lavigne had the best of it up to the
tenth round, after which Smith gained a
distinct lead. To many, however, it looked
as if Smith was holding back early in the
fight, and he surely did not employ ids
usual tactics of rushing, by which he has
wen many a fight. Smith's superior height
and reach were against Lavigne, how
ever, ami his confident air all through told
that he felt victory was in his grasp. If
Lavigne had pulled through the round he
might have kepi away and lasted the
limit; but the impression among ring
goers seemed to be- that Smith had him at
any stage. Lavigne went out of his class
to meet Smith, and this will teach him a
lesson in that respect.
Billy Otts succeeded in landing a pretty
right cross on Paddy Maloney's jaw in the.
third round of their fight and won the
preliminary bout of the evening. While
tin- fight lasted it was a pretty exhibition
of pluck and cleverness and was greatly
enjoyed by all present. Otts clearly had
the advantage in weight and reach and to
this may be attributed Maloney's defeat.
It is said that when the lads weighed in
Otts tipped the scales at IS." 1 , pounds, five
and a half over the weight agreed on by
both when articles were signed. On the
other hand Maloney weighed but 125%.
Despite this handicap Maloney went in
and put up a good fight while it lasted.
The first round was devoted to long dis
OFFICERS OF CHITOSE
ROYAL ENTERTAINERS
Captain Sakurai Extends the Hospitality of the
New Cruiser.
CAPTAIN SAKURAI and his officers
yesterday extended the hospitality of
his Imperial Japanese Majesty's ship
Chitose to the State and Federal of
ficers of California, the municipal officers
of San Francisco, the representatives of
the United States army and navy and to
the prominent citizens generally. It was
a gala day aboard the new cruiser, which
lay just off the Union Iron Works, within
the very shadow of her nativity as it
were. From the prow of the Chitose float
ed the national colors of Japan, the rising
sun upon the snow-white field, while from
the stern the flag of the royal Japanese
navy flapped defiantly in the wind.
The reception was from 2 to 5 o'clock,
and during that interval innumerable
tugs and launches were kept busy plying
tance sparring, a few blows being landed
by both without any damage. The second
fennel Otts on the aggressive, but every
attack he made was met by Maloney with
stinging lefts and rights. Otts nearly went
through the ropes in trying to avoid one
of Maloney's rushes.
The end came unexpectedly in the third
round. After fiddling for an opening Otts
led with his long left which Maloney neat
ly blocked, but Otts shot across his right
and landed flush on his opponent's jaw,
sending him to the floor. While Referee
Jim Neal counted he slowly arose to his
knees and on the ninth count reached his
feet and clinched with otts. On being
parted Otts tried left and right and man
aged to land again on the face, sending
bis dizzy rival down. At this juncture
Captain Gillen entered the ring and
stopped further hostilities. Neal awarded
the decision to Otts.
Wants to Meet Green.
"Lavigne Is the best lightweight that
ever breathed," said Smith after the
bout; "but he was a little out of his class
when he tried to tackle a welter-weight.
He is the fastest fellow I have ever seen
for. his size. I had him going a dozen
times, hut he would not go down. I wal
loped him on the jaw time and again, but
he stood it like a little man.
.â  '.'When the articles of agreement were
signed we agreed to break clean, but hit
with one hand free. That is the reason
why I fought as I did. As to the bandage
on my left hand, that was also agreed to.
and when 'Billy' Lavigne objected to it I
refused to take it off. as my hand was
sore. I didn't feel a blow that the 'Kid'
gave me. Half of them I stopped with
my glove. I intend staying here and hope
to get on a match with Green or Walcott.
1 am willing to take on Walcott and then
light Green two weeks afterward. I am
glad 'Billy' Lavigne jumped Into the ring
when he did, as I did not want to knock
the 'Kid' out. and another punch might
have settled him."
What Lavigne Said.
"Kid" Lavigne, on reaching his dressing
room after the fight, did not upbraid his
brother "Billy" for interfering when he
did, but calmly dressed and left the build
ing. The champion lightweight showed no
signs of punishment, but declared he was
fresher than his late opponent. â¢ In speak
ing of the fight the "Kid" said:
"I admit I was a little bit on queer
street, but was not a bit distressed and
could have gone on and finished if my
brother had not come into the ring. I was
not a bit tired, but was slightly dizzy.
I'm willing to bet Smith was more tired
than I was "when the interruption oc
curred:
"I didn't quite get on to what Billy was
doing. I thought he was saying the gong
Bounded and was trying to drag me to my
corner. Smith fought foul throughout the
contest, but I did not care, as I thought
I could do him."
"Billy" Lavigne Talks.
"Billy" Lavigne, the manager and
brother of the champion lightweight, who
followed the example set by "Connie" Mc-
Vey by jumping into the ring at an im
proper moment, seemed unperturbed at
what he had done, but gave the following
reasons for doing so:
"The referee should have awarded the
fight to my brother earlier in the contest.
Smith repeatedly fouled him and kept up
these tactics right along. He had a great
advantage in fouling. I got tired of see
ing him continuing it and jumped into the
ring. I did not do so to save my brother
from a knockout, tiit simply because I
would not stand foi*3mith's rough work.
Had George followed Smith's game I
would not have Interfered, but as he
didn't and was getting the worst of the
deal, I jumped in."
The Referee's Views.
Immediately after declaring "Mysteri
ous" Billy Smith the victor. Referee Mc-
Donald informed the press representa
tives that he did so because Lavigne's
brother entered me ring at an untimely
moment. .
"Had this not occurred," said the ref
eree, "and the fight had gone the limit,
1 would have declared it a draw, because
in mv opinion Lavigne had the best of
the fight up to the tenth round. From the
tenth to the fourteenth Smith showed to
advantage. I could not decide differently,
as the rules expressly prohibit any of
the seconds entering the ring during the
fighting." ,_____,
Ring-goers were greatly surprised that
Referee McDonald did not follow the pre
cedent established by "Honest" John Kel
ly in the Corbett-Sharkey go. when Mc-
Vey entered the ring, and declare all
bets off, thus saving the public's money.
In the opinion of many present McDon
ald should have followed his example.

Ellis Company Arrives To-Day.
The Ellis Opera Company of nearly iw
people will arrive from Denver this morn
ing about 10 o'clock. They come in a spe
cial train, made up of Mme. Melba s pri
vate car. the "Campania," six Pullman
sleepers and four baggage cars.
zuki. Assistant Engineer T. O. Oka
zaki. Sub-Lieutenants S. Yokoo and T.
Tajiri and Assistant Engineer Y. Ikeda.
. The officers were the personification of
polite and attentive courtesy. They
proved themselves charming hosts and
royal entertainers. The guests were
shown over the Chitose from stem to
stern, and every facility was given them
for seeing this newest fighting terror,
which Is the pride of the Union Iron
Works, despite the fact that over her
floats an alien flag.
Captain Terry of the lowa, and his staff,
when they called, were received at the
starboard gangway with due honors, as
was Major General Dickinson and staff.
Every moment of the time spent aboard
the cruiser proved delightful in the way
of something to see and admire, and each
guest was made happy by the presenta
tion of some appropriate souvenir of the
occasion.
